“Corpus linguistics is one of the fastest-growing areas of second language 
acquisition research. In this timely and compelling overview, Xiaofei Lu 
shows us why. He illustrates the use of native and learner corpora as objec­
tive, sharable evidence of patterns of language uniformity, variation, and 
growth, and he explains the methods, measures, and tools for analyzing 
corpus data.An excellent source for researchers and students alike.” 
Nick Ellis, University of Michigan, USA 
“This book is a must-read for both beginning and experienced research­
ers who are interested in corpus-based explorations of second language 
acquisition. It offers a comprehensive and accessible review and discussion 
of a wealth of carefully chosen topics that may help unravel the mysteries of 
second language acquisition.” 
Lei Lei, Professor of Applied Linguistics, 
Shanghai Jiao Tong University, China 
“This volume is an excellent resource for those who want to apply corpus 
methods to the study of second language acquisition. Importantly, the text 
covers both relevant theoretical constructs (from a range of perspectives) 
and ways in which these theoretical constructs have and can be operation­
alized when studying learner language.This book is a must-read for SLA 
researchers, and I look forward to using it in both graduate classes devoted 
to SLA more generally and those that focus on learner corpus research.” 
Kristopher Kyle,Associate Professor of Linguistics, 
University of Oregon, USA

<!-- page 4 -->

 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
CORPUS LINGUISTICS AND SECOND 
LANGUAGE ACQUISITION 
In Corpus Linguistics and Second Language Acquisition, Xiaofei Lu comprehensively 
reviews empirical studies that employ corpus linguistic methods to investigate 
issues in second language variation, processing, production, and development. 
These methods enable advanced students and researchers to: 
• 
Examine learner and task variables that condition variation in second lan­
guage use 
• 
Understand the effects of various input factors on second language process­
ing and production 
• 
Track group longitudinal trajectories of second language development and 
the input, learner, and task factors that affect such trajectories 
• 
Profile inter- and intra-learner variability and individual variation in second 
language longitudinal development 
This book will serve as an excellent resource for students and researchers with 
interests in corpus linguistics and second language acquisition. 
Xiaofei Lu is Professor of Applied Linguistics and Asian Studies at The 
Pennsylvania State University. His research specializations include corpus lin­
guistics, second language acquisition, second language writing, English for 
Academic Purposes, and computer-assisted language learning. He is the author 
of Computational Methods for Corpus Annotation and Analysis (2014).

Cognitive Science and Second Language Acquisition Series 
Peter Robinson, Series Editor 
The Cognitive Science and Second Language Acquisition series is designed to provide 
systematic and accessible coverage of the links between basic concepts and find­
ings in cognitive science and second language acquisition (SLA). Titles in the 
series summarize issues and research in areas of cognitive science which have rele­
vance to SLA, and when read in combination, provide a comprehensive overview 
of the conceptual and methodological intersects between these two fields.The 
series is a valuable reference for scholars who want to increase their knowledge of 
theoretical and operational definitions in cognitive science, and their applications 
to SLA. Its titles are ideal for graduate students and researchers in SLA, applied 
linguistics, cognitive psychology, educational psychology, and language education, 
and can also serve as textbooks for advanced courses in these fields. 
Metalinguistic Awareness and Second Language Acquisition 
Karen Roehr-Brackin 
Connectionism and Second Language Acquisition 
Yasuhiro Shirai 
Crosslinguistic Infuence and Second Language Learning 
Kevin McManus

Perspectives, Issues, and Findings 
Xiaofei Lu

SERIES EDITOR’S PREFACE 
The Cognitive Science and Second Language Acquisition (CS&SLA) series is 
designed to provide accessible, and comprehensive coverage of the links between 
basic concepts, and findings, in cognitive science (CS) and second language acqui­
sition (SLA) in a systematic way.Taken together, books in the series should com­
bine to provide a comprehensive overview of the conceptual and methodological 
intersects between these two fields. This means the books in the series can be 
read alone, or (more profitably) in combination.The field of SLA is related to, but 
distinct from, linguistics, applied linguistics, cognitive psychology, and education. 
However, while a great many published book series address the link between 
SLA and educational concerns, SLA and linguistics, and SLA and applied lin­
guistics, currently no series exists which explores the relationship between SLA 
and cognitive science. Research findings and theoretical constructs from cogni­
tive science have become increasingly influential upon SLA research in recent 
years. Consequently there is great reason to think that future SLA research, and 
research into its educational applications, will be increasingly influenced by con­
cerns addressed in CS and its subdisciplines. The books in the CS&SLA series 
are intended to facilitate this interdisciplinary understanding, and are grouped 
into four domains: (1) Knowledge Representation; (2) Cognitive Processing; (3) 
Language Development; and (4) Individual Differences. 
Xiaofei Lu’s book, Corpus Linguistics and Second Language Acquisition, has 
implications for all of these four domains, but is most centrally concerned with 
language development and its measurement. Lu provides a valuable, broad, 
and up-to-date summary of corpus linguistic methods for analyzing language 
variation caused by learner factors and task factors, and for longitudinal studies 
of language development. Chapters describe in detail usage-based approaches to

---

<!-- page 14 -->

 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Series Editor’s Preface xiii 
SLA, and the development of L2 constructions; developmental trajectories of the 
accuracy, fluency, and complexity of learner language; and Complex Dynamic 
Systems approaches to describing variability of learner language during SLA. 
Throughout his book, Xiaofei Lu provides accessible introductions to vari­
ous corpora of learner language, and to various programs available to analyze 
them – such as Xiaofei Lu’s own Syntactic Complexity Analyzer (L2SCA) – and 
a thorough description of the units of analysis operationalized in these programs. 
All of the issues and analytic measures Lu describes are essential to understanding 
current and future research into second language development and task variation. 
Xiaofei Lu’s book is a very welcome, important, and stimulating addition to the 
CS&SLA series. 
Peter Robinson 
Series Editor

---

<!-- page 15 -->

 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
PREFACE 
The increasing availability of native and learner corpora and automated 
and semi-automated tools for corpus annotation and analysis has drasti­
cally expanded the scope and scale of second language acquisition (SLA) 
research. Over multiple iterations of the graduate seminar on Applied Corpus 
Linguistics in the Department of Applied Linguistics at the Pennsylvania State 
University, my students and I have read, discussed, learned from, critiqued, 
and partially replicated numerous inspiring SLA studies that use corpus lin­
guistic methods to investigate issues in second language variation, processing, 
production, and development.Written for students and researchers who share 
our passion for corpus linguistics and SLA, this book provides a systematic 
review and discussion of the perspectives, issues, and findings of this body 
of research, while explicitly showcasing the ways in which corpus linguistic 
methods have empowered second language researchers to employ new data 
sources and analyses to address long-standing issues as well as to validate new 
theoretical hypotheses in the field of SLA. 
I owe a special thanks to the editor of Routledge’s Cognitive Science and 
Second Language Acquisition Series, Peter Robinson, for his inspiring trust and 
guidance throughout the process. I want to thank all the students who have 
taken the seminar with me at the Pennsylvania State University, in particular 
Edwin A. Dartey,Yingying Liu, and Yuanheng (Arthur) Wang, who assisted me 
with the literature search and provided detailed feedback on earlier drafts of 
the manuscript. I am indebted to Kristopher Kyle and Hongyin Tao for shar­
ing their insightful thoughts on the directions of the book with me, Kevin 
McManus for sharing his book writing experience with me, and Tan Jin for his 
continuous encouragement and support. I am also grateful to the colleagues

---

<!-- page 16 -->

 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Preface xv 
who generously shared their research with me, including Nick Ellis, James 
Garner, Sara Gesuato, Lei Lei, Charlotte Stormbom, Nicole Tracy-Ventura, 
Helen Zhao, and many others. Last but not least, I would like to thank the 
editors at Routledge for their patience and support as I struggled to make this 
book a reality during this pandemic.

<!-- page 18 -->

  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
1 
INTRODUCTION 
1.1 Corpus Linguistics Meets Second Language Acquisition 
This book presents a comprehensive overview of second language acquisition 
(SLA) research that utilizes corpus linguistic methods to examine learner and task 
variables that condition variation in second language (L2) use, to understand the 
effects of various input factors on L2 processing and production, to track group 
longitudinal trajectories of L2 development and the input, learner, and task fac­
tors that affect such trajectories, and to profile inter- and intra-learner variability 
and individual variation in L2 longitudinal development.We use the term “corpus 
linguistic methods” in this book to include native and learner corpora as data 
sources, automated and semi-automated tools for corpus annotation, as well as 
methods, measures, and tools developed for analyzing corpus data in various ways. 
The target readership of the book includes students and researchers with vary­
ing degrees of familiarity with corpus linguistics and/or SLA theories who are 
becoming seriously interested in learning about or pursuing the use of corpus 
linguistic methods in SLA research. With this readership in mind, along with a 
thorough discussion of the theoretical perspectives and findings of the body of 
research reviewed, the book aims to explicitly showcase the ways in which cor­
pus linguistic methods have enabled L2 researchers to employ new data sources 
and analyses to address long-standing issues as well as to validate new theoretical 
hypotheses in the field of SLA. 
Both native and learner corpora have constituted valuable data sources for 
this body of SLA research. Native corpora, including large-scale general-purpose 
corpora such as the British National Corpus (BNC; Burnard, 2007) and the 
Corpus of Contemporary American English (COCA; Davies, 2009) and smaller-
scale corpora of instructional or interactive input provided to L2 learners by first 
DOI: 10.4324/9781003054948-1

---

<!-- page 19 -->

 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
2 Introduction 
language (L1) speakers, have been used in studies of L2 processing and develop­
ment either to derive various types of input statistics or to serve as reference 
points for assessing learner development (e.g., Ellis et al., 2014). Learner cor­
pora, including large-scale corpora made available to the research community by 
research institutions and teams and smaller-scale corpora of L2 learners’ spoken 
or written production compiled by individual researchers, have been analyzed 
in various ways to gain insights into L2 variation, production, and development. 
Specifically, cross-sectional corpora that encode rich learner and task information, 
such as each learner’s proficiency level and L1 background and each language 
sample’s genre and task type, allow researchers to examine how L2 learners’ lan­
guage use may vary as a result of different learner and task variables and their 
interaction and how L2 learners’ language production may be affected by usage 
features of their language input.Two examples of such corpora include the Trinity 
Lancaster Corpus (TLC; Gablasova et al., 2019) and the Written English Corpus 
of Chinese Learners (WECCL) Version 2.0 (Wen et al., 2008). Longitudinal cor­
pora of learner production, such as the Longitudinal Database of Learner English 
(LONGDALE; Meunier, 2016), allow researchers to profile and compare group 
and individual longitudinal trajectories of L2 development. Hybrid corpora with 
data representing both cross-sectional and longitudinal development, such as the 
EF-Cambridge Open Language Database (EFCAMDAT; Geertzen et al., 2014) 
and the Kansas Developmental Learner corpus (KANDEL;Vyatkina, 2016), fur­
ther enable researchers to compare individual longitudinal trajectories of L2 
development with cross-sectional developmental trends. 
In addition to the use of native and learner corpora as data sources, the tools 
and methods developed for corpus annotation and analysis have proven highly 
useful in SLA research as well (e.g., Lu, 2022; Lu & Chen, 2019). Some tools 
have been designed to identify various types of lexical and phraseological units 
such as words, collocations, lexical bundles, and phrase-frames from raw corpora 
(i.e., corpora that have not been annotated with specific linguistic information), 
to facilitate close qualitative analyses of the use of such units in context, and 
in some cases to perform relevant statistical analyses of the units extracted. For 
example, AntConc (Anthony, 2022), a freely available concordancing program, 
can be used to obtain a word frequency list from a corpus, to identify keywords 
in a corpus (i.e., words that are used significantly more frequently in the corpus 
than in a relevant reference corpus), to extract collocates of a word in a corpus 
and calculate the association strength between the word and each of its col­
locates using several statistical measures (e.g., mutual information, t-score, and 
log-likelihood), and to generate a concordance of a head word or phrase (i.e., 
all occurrences of the head word or phrase in the corpus presented in a key-
word-in-context format) for close textual analysis. Another program, AntGram 
(Anthony, 2021), can be used to extract n-grams as well as phrase-frames (i.e., 
n-grams with one or more open slots) meeting various criteria (e.g., length, 
frequency, and range of text samples in which they occur) from a corpus.The

---

<!-- page 20 -->

 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Introduction 3 
language samples in a corpus can also be automatically annotated with various 
types of linguistic information, such as the part-of-speech (POS) category of 
each word and the syntactic structure of each sentence (see, e.g., Lu, 2014). For 
example, the Stanford CoreNLP (Manning et al., 2014) can be used to derive 
a range of linguistic annotations, including POS categories and constituency 
and dependency parses, among others, for eight languages (i.e.,Arabic, Chinese, 
English, French, German, Hungarian, Italian, and Spanish).Additional tools can 
then be used to automatically extract instances of target linguistic structures or 
patterns from linguistically annotated corpora and subsequently perform rel­
evant quantitative analysis over such instances. For example, the L2 Syntactic 
Complexity Analyzer (L2SCA; Lu, 2010) and the Tool for Automated Analysis of 
Syntactic Sophistication and Complexity (TAASSC; Kyle, 2016) utilize the con­
stituency and dependency parses from the Stanford Parser (Chen & Manning, 
2014; Klein & Manning, 2003) respectively to extract a range of syntactic units 
(e.g., dependent clauses and noun phrases) and constructions (e.g., verb-argu­
ment constructions) and subsequently compute various syntactic complexity 
and sophistication indices. Indeed, the search for new ways to quantify linguistic 
phenomena has also been a prominent concern of research intersecting corpus 
linguistics and SLA.This search has resulted in many novel measures that have 
proven useful in understanding L2 production and processing and assessing L2 
variation and development, as illustrated by endeavors to develop new ways to 
measure the directional association between linguistic elements (e.g., Gries & 
Stefanowitsch, 2004; Gries & Ellis, 2015) and new indices to gauge lexical and 
syntactic complexity and sophistication (e.g., Biber et al., 2016; Kyle, 2016; Kyle 
& Crossley, 2015; Lu, 2010, 2012; Lu & Hu, 2022). Some measures directly uti­
lize distributional information of relevant linguistic elements in corpora, such 
as the usage-based syntactic sophistication measures proposed by Kyle (2016) 
(see Chapter 2). Other linguistically or cognitively motivated measures, such as 
measures of noun phrase complexity based on different structural types (e.g., Lu 
& Wu, 2022) and measures of lexical sophistication based on processing or reac­
tion time (e.g., Kyle & Crossley, 2015), have been validated using various types 
of corpus data. 
The issues addressed in the body of SLA research reviewed in the book center 
around the following core questions related to L2 variation, processing, produc­
tion, and development: 
• 
How might different learner and task variables give rise to variation in 
L2 use? 
• 
How might different input factors affect L2 processing and production? 
• 
What do the longitudinal trajectories of L2 learners’ development of differ­
ent subsystems of an L2 look like? How might different input, learner, and 
task factors interact with time in shaping those trajectories? 
• 
What types of individual variability and variation exist in L2 development?

---

<!-- page 21 -->

 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
4 Introduction 
These questions have been explored from multiple theoretical perspectives, 
among which usage-based linguistics (e.g.,Ellis et al., 2014;Wulff & Ellis, 2018) 
and Complex Dynamic System Theory (e.g., de Bot et al., 2007; Larsen-Freeman, 
1997) are especially prominent.They have also been examined at a wide range of 
linguistic levels (e.g., phonological, lexical, phraseological, and morphosyntactic) 
using features with varying degrees of specificity (e.g., features of global complex­
ity, accuracy, and fluency as well as features pertaining to specific structures or 
constructions).The studies reviewed in the book collectively illustrate how cor­
pus linguistic methods can be compatible with diverse theoretical points of depar­
ture and linguistic analytical foci and complementary of other research methods 
(e.g., psycholinguistic experimental methods). Large-scale corpora that encode 
rich learner and task information, coupled with tools for automated annotation 
and analysis, have not only expanded the scale and scope of data analysis in SLA 
research but also allowed for the complex relationships between key input, learner, 
and task variables and L2 variation, processing, production, and development to be 
investigated in new ways. It should be noted that this does not mean SLA studies 
taking advantage of corpus linguistic methods forego manual analyses of learner 
corpus data. Quite to the contrary, close qualitative analyses of such data are an 
important component of many of the studies reviewed, as they shed critical light 
on the specific ways in which L2 development takes place and in which varia­
tion and/or variability manifest in L2 usage, production, and development (e.g., 
Gilquin, 2019; Ivaska, 2015). It is also the case that many aspects of L2 variation 
and production cannot yet be fully or reliably analyzed automatically, includ­
ing, for example, aspects related to the appropriateness, contextual meanings, and 
pragmatic or rhetorical functions of the linguistic features used, due to the high 
degree of subjectivity or ambiguity involved. Semi-automated, computer-assisted, 
or manual analyses of such aspects are still necessary. 
Throughout the book, we will explicitly describe the composition of the 
native and/or learner corpora used or compiled as well as the tools and meth­
ods utilized for corpus annotation and/or analysis in each study reviewed.This 
description serves to highlight the appropriateness and contribution of corpus 
linguistic methods for addressing the research questions of concern to the body 
of SLA research reviewed. At the same time, it also makes it easier to discuss 
the limitations and necessary further considerations in using such methods in 
SLA research. 
1.2 Overview of the Book 
The rest of the book is organized as follows. Chapter 2 reviews empirical studies 
that incorporated corpus linguistic methods to examine variation in L2 use at 
the group level.The chapter first provides an overview of the types of variation 
in L2 use that have been of concern to SLA researchers and the primary ways 
in which such variation has been measured. It then systematically reviews results

---

<!-- page 22 -->

 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Introduction 5 
of empirical studies that used corpus linguistic methods to examine theoretically 
and pedagogically important learner- and/or task-related variables that condition 
variation in L2 use. 
In Chapter 3, we synthesize empirical research that utilized corpus linguis­
tic methods to investigate factors influencing L2 processing and production.The 
chapter starts with a section on usage-based approaches to SLA, focusing in par­
ticular on the view that language acquisition and processing involve associative 
learning that reflects the probabilities of occurrence of constructions in usage 
(e.g., Ellis et al., 2014;Wulff & Ellis, 2018). It then systematically reviews results of 
empirical studies that either combined corpus linguistic and experimental meth­
ods to examine factors affecting L2 learners’ processing of specific constructions 
or employed corpus analysis on its own to investigate factors affecting L2 learners’ 
production of certain constructions. 
Chapter 4 reviews empirical studies that employed corpus linguistic methods 
to track group-level longitudinal trajectories of L2 development and/or examine 
the input, learner, and task factors that affect such trajectories. Most of these stud­
ies were conducted within the usage-based linguistic theoretical framework, but 
studies from other theoretical perspectives are included as well.The chapter starts 
with a section on the development of complexity, accuracy, and fluency and con­
tains four additional sections that focus on the development of lexical knowledge, 
phraseological development, the development of grammatical and morphosyn­
tactic constructions, and the development of stance, respectively. 
Chapter 5 examines the use of corpus linguistic methods in investigating vari­
ability and variation in L2 development.The chapter starts with an overview of 
the major tenets and investigative concerns of the Complex Dynamic Systems 
approach to SLA (e.g., de Bot et al., 2007; Larsen-Freeman, 1997). It then sys­
tematically reviews the methods and findings of a small body of empirical studies 
that integrated corpus linguistic techniques to investigate inter- and intra-learner 
variability and individual variation in L2 development. 
Finally, in Chapter 6, we summarize the ways in which corpus linguistic 
methods have contributed to the four broad areas of SLA research discussed in 
Chapters 2 to 5, reflect on the reliability, validity, effectiveness, and limitations of 
the ways in which they have been applied, and conclude the book by proposing a 
number of directions for future applications of corpus linguistic methods in SLA 
research, including both new ways of using existing techniques and new applica­
tions of novel/emerging techniques.

<!-- page 25 -->

  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
2 
VARIATION IN SECOND 
LANGUAGE USE 
2.1 Introduction 
Differences in the linguistic behavior among second language (L2) learners with dif­
ferent proficiency levels, first language (L1) backgrounds, and other learner-related 
variables have received much attention in second language acquisition (SLA) research. 
Such differences may exist in various aspects of language production and use, such as 
vocabulary, grammar, and pragmatic features, among others. At the same time, vari­
ous task-related variables (e.g., the cognitive complexity of the tasks used to elicit 
learner production) may also lead to variation in L2 learners’ language use.A system­
atic understanding of the variation in L2 use as a result of different learner- and task-
related variables has important theoretical and pedagogical implications. 
This chapter reviews empirical studies that incorporated corpus linguistic methods 
to examine variation in L2 use.The chapter starts with a discussion of the types of var­
iation in L2 use that have been of concern to SLA researchers and the primary ways 
in which such variation has been measured. It then systematically reviews results of 
empirical studies that used corpus linguistic methods to investigate the ways in which 
L2 use varies as a result of various learner- and task-related variables. In addition to 
summarizing the major issues, perspectives, and findings of this body of research, the 
review attends to the theoretical underpinnings and specific methodological choices 
of the studies covered. Note, however, that the chapter does not cover studies that 
focus exclusively on the differences between L2 production and L1 production or the 
relationship between different linguistic features and L2 production quality. 
2.2 Measuring Variation in Second Language Use 
Much research on variation in L2 use has focused on the complexity, accuracy, 
and fluency (CAF) of the spoken or written language produced by L2 learners 
DOI: 10.4324/9781003054948-2

---

<!-- page 26 -->

 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Variation in Second Language Use 9 
(e.g., Housen & Kuiken, 2009; Norris & Ortega, 2009; Wolfe-Quintero et al., 
1998). Linguistic complexity, seen “as a dynamic property of the learner’s inter-
language system at large and as a more stable property of the individual linguistic 
elements that make up the interlanguage system” (Housen & Kuiken, 2009, p. 
463), has often been characterized as “the extent to which language produced 
in performing a task is elaborate and varied” (Ellis, 2003, p. 340). Accuracy has 
been defined as “the conformity of second language knowledge to target lan­
guage norms” (Wolfe-Quintero et al., 1998, p. 4) or “the extent to which an L2 
performance deviates from a norm” (Housen & Kuiken, 2009, p. 4). Fluency 
can be understood as the degree of smoothness or ease with which language 
is produced (Lennon, 1990) or, more specifically, “the production of language 
in real time without undue pausing or hesitation” (Ellis & Barkhuizen, 2005, p. 
139). Ortega (2012) summarized three primary purposes for investigating the 
linguistic complexity of L2 production in SLA research, namely, to understand 
whether it increases with L2 proficiency, whether it varies systematically across 
different task conditions and tasks with different characteristics, and whether it 
increases with growing grammatical development. Investigations of the accuracy 
and fluency of L2 production in SLA research have been concerned with simi­
lar questions regarding between-proficiency differences, cross-task variation, and 
development over time. 
Linguistic complexity, accuracy, and fluency have each been operationalized 
using a large set of global and specific measures (e.g., Lu, 2017; Suzuki, 2017; 
Wolfe-Quintero et al., 1998). Linguistic complexity research has focused over­
whelmingly on lexical complexity and syntactic complexity, both of which have 
been conceptualized as multidimensional constructs on their own. Lexical com­
plexity has been seen to encompass lexical density, diversity, and sophistication 
(e.g., Lu, 2012). Lexical density is usually measured as the ratio of the num­
ber of lexical or content words in a text to the total number of words in the 
text (Ure, 1971). Lexical diversity captures the extent to which the words used 
in a text are varied and non-repetitive (Yu, 2010), measured commonly using 
type-token ratio (TTR), i.e., the ratio of the number of word types (i.e., unique 
words) in a text to the number of word tokens in the text. Given that TTR tends 
to decrease as text length increases, various transformations of the TTR have 
been proposed to mitigate or eliminate the text length effect, such as Root TTR 
(RTTR; Guiraud, 1960), Moving-Average TTR (MATTR; Covington & McFall, 
2010), the vocd-D measure (Malvern et al., 2004), Hypergeometric Distribution 
D (HDD; McCarthy & Jarvis, 2007), and the Measure of Textual Lexical Diversity 
(MTLD; McCarthy & Jarvis, 2010), among others.TTR and some of its transfor­
mations have also been used to assess the diversity of specific categories of words, 
such as lexical words, modifiers, and verbs (e.g., Lu, 2012). Lexical sophistication 
assesses the proportion of words in a text that are “relatively unusual or advanced” 
(Read, 2000, p. 203). Lexical sophistication has been traditionally operational­
ized in terms of the reference-corpus frequency of the words in a text, either by

---

<!-- page 27 -->

 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
10 Variation in Second Language Use 
profiling the proportion of words in the text belonging to different frequency 
bands in a large reference corpus (e.g., Laufer & Nation, 1995) or by computing 
the mean reference-frequency scores of the words (e.g., Graesser et al., 2004). 
Recent research has operationalized lexical sophistication using indices that tap 
into a wide range of lexical and n-gram properties beyond reference-corpus fre­
quency to assess both the inherent and relative difficulty of lexical items for learn­
ing (Bulté & Housen, 2012). For example, the Tool for the Automatic Analysis 
of Lexical Sophistication (TAALES; Kyle & Crossley, 2015; Kyle et al., 2018) 
provides a large set of indices capturing various distributional (e.g., the range of 
texts in a reference corpus that a word occurs in), formal (e.g., measures of pho­
nological and orthographic uniqueness), semantic (e.g., measures of the degree of 
polysemy and hypernymy), acquisitional (e.g., indices based on the age of acquisi­
tion ratings of the words), and psycholinguistic (e.g., measures of the degree of 
word concreteness and familiarity) properties of words, along with a set of indices 
capturing several n-gram properties (e.g., reference-corpus frequency and range). 
Syntactic complexity is commonly construed as the degree of variation, 
sophistication, and elaboration of the grammatical structures used in language 
production (Housen & Kuiken, 2009; Lu, 2011; Ortega, 2015; Wolfe-Quintero 
et al., 1998). There is a general consensus that syntactic complexity should be 
understood as a multidimensional construct and that complexity at different 
structural levels (e.g.,global,clausal,and phrasal) should be assessed using measures 
that highlight different aspects of complexity (e.g., amount, length, and subtypes 
of coordination, subordination, and modification) (e.g., Norris & Ortega, 2009; 
Lu, 2017; Kyle & Crossley, 2018). A large number of indices with different 
degrees of granularity now exist for measuring different dimensions and aspects 
of syntactic complexity, many of which are available through automated analyzers. 
For example, the L2 Syntactic Complexity Analyzer (L2SCA; Lu, 2010) provides 
14 holistic measures of syntactic complexity shown to be significantly related 
to L2 proficiency in previous research or recommended by Wolfe-Quintero 
et al. (1998) to be useful indices to pursue. These measures are classified into 
five categories. The first category consists of three measures that gauge length 
of production at the sentential,T-unit, and clausal levels, namely, mean length of 
sentence, mean length of T-unit, and mean length of clause. In these measures, 
a T-unit is defined as “one main clause plus any subordinate clause or non-
clausal structure that is attached to or embedded in it” (Hunt, 1970, p. 4), and a 
clause is defined as a structure with a subject and a finite verb (Hunt, 1965).The 
second category includes an overall sentence complexity measure (i.e., clauses 
per sentence).The third category comprises four subordination measures, namely, 
clauses per T-unit, complex T-units per T-unit, dependent clauses per clause, and 
dependent clauses per T-unit. The fourth category contains three coordination 
measures, namely,coordinate phrases per clause,coordinate phrases per T-unit, and 
T-units per sentence.The last category comprises three phrasal measures, namely, 
complex nominals per clause, complex nominals per T-unit, and verb phrases per

---

<!-- page 28 -->

 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Variation in Second Language Use 11 
T-unit.While such holistic indices have been shown to be useful in assessing the 
syntactic complexity of L2 production, researchers have also argued for the need 
for fine-grained indices that can adequately capture variation in the use of specific 
clausal and phrasal structures (Biber et al., 2016; Kyle & Crossley, 2018) as well 
as for usage-based indices of syntactic sophistication that take into account the 
frequencies of and association strengths between different components of target 
constructions (i.e., form-meaning mappings; see, e.g., Tomasello, 2003) (Kyle, 
2016). Building on these developments, the Tool for the Automatic Analysis of 
Syntactic Sophistication and Complexity (TAASSC; Kyle, 2016) offers a large set 
of fine-grained clausal complexity, phrasal complexity, and syntactic sophistication 
indices.The clausal complexity indices calculate the average number of specific 
structures per clause (e.g., nominal complements per clause), with the exception 
that two indices calculate the mean and standard deviation of the number of 
dependents per clause. For phrasal complexity, seven types of noun phrases (i.e., 
agent, direct object, indirect object, nominal subject, nominal complement, passive 
nominal subject, and prepositional object) and ten types of phrasal dependents 
(i.e., adjective modifiers, adverbial modifiers, conjunction and, conjunction or, 
determiners, nouns as modifiers, possessives, prepositions, relative clause modifiers, 
and verbal modifiers) are identified.The actual indices fall into three types: Mean 
number of dependents per each phrase type (e.g., number of dependents per 
nominal subject), occurrence of specific dependent types (e.g., occurrence of 
adverbial modifiers), and mean occurrence of specific dependent types in each 
phrase type (e.g., number of adverbial modifiers per nominal complement). Five 
sets of syntactic sophistication indices are calculated using frequency information 
of main verb lemmas, verb-argument constructions (VACs), and verb-VAC 
combination in the following five subcorpora of the Corpus of Contemporary 
American English (COCA;Davies,2009):Academic,fiction,magazine,newspaper, 
and spoken. In each set, the indices calculate average frequency scores and TTRs 
of main verb lemmas, VACs, and verb-VAC combinations; the percentage of 
main verb lemmas,VACs, and verb-VAC combinations attested in the COCA 
subcorpus; and mean association strength of verb-VAC combinations assessed 
using several association measures. 
Accuracy has also been measured using a wide range of holistic and specific 
indices. In their comprehensive review, Polio and Shea (2014) categorized such 
measures into five major types.The first type includes the use of various holistic 
scales to assess the overall accuracy of the language produced, such as Hamp-
Lyons’ (1991) linguistic accuracy scale or, in some cases, the overall accuracy of 
either one specific aspect of language use, such as vocabulary, or two or more 
specific aspects at the same time, such as vocabulary and grammar. The second 
type consists of indices that compute the number or proportion of error-free 
units, most commonly the proportion of error-free T-units and proportion of 
error-free clauses. The third type contains measures that take into account the 
number of errors, such as the number of errors per text, per T-unit, per words,

---

<!-- page 29 -->

 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
12 Variation in Second Language Use 
or per 100 words, generally with specification of the kinds of errors counted. 
Measures of the fourth type examine the number of specific types of errors, such 
as percentage of articles used in obligatory contexts and percentage of correct 
verb forms (regarding tense, aspect, modality, and subject-verb agreement). Finally, 
the fifth type includes a small number of measures that take into account the 
severity of the errors made, such as proportion of T-units with errors in three 
degrees of severity. 
While these accuracy measures involve human judgments of whether a 
specific usage is erroneous or not, Wulff and Gries (2011) proposed a corpus-
driven approach for assessing accuracy in learner production that is based on 
conditional probabilities and that does not involve binary human judgments. 
Adopting a constructionist approach to language (Goldberg, 1995) that 
understands constructions as form-meaning pairs existing at all levels of linguistic 
representations, they defined accuracy in L2 production as “the selection of a 
construction (in the Goldbergian sense of the term) in its preferred context 
within a particular target variety and genre” (p. 71). Instead of treating accuracy 
as a binary concept, this definition assumes “a continuum of differently 
schematized constructions” characterized by various parameters (e.g., frequency, 
semantic transparency, and pragmatic function) and sees native-like selection of 
constructions as an “inherently scalar phenomenon” (p. 71) conditioned by those 
parameters or contextual factors. The specific corpus-linguistic method used 
to operationalize this definition of accuracy is “collostructional analysis” (Gries 
& Stefanowitsch, 2004), which refers to a family of measures of the association 
between two constructions at various linguistic levels. By quantitatively assessing 
the likelihood of one construction in the environment of another construction, 
these measures offer information on how native-like a learner’s selection of a 
construction is, making them especially appropriate for the definition of accuracy 
adopted in this approach. A number of learner corpus studies of variation in 
L2 learners’ constructional knowledge or use within the usage-based or 
constructionist paradigm have adopted collostructional analysis to examine the 
extent to which L2 usage approximates L1 usage, although not necessarily always 
explicitly labeling it as a way to measure accuracy (e.g., Römer & Garner, 2019). 
Fluency of spoken production has been measured using indices that tap into 
the following three aspects, namely, speech rate, repair, and silence/breakdown 
(Michel, 2019). Speech rate is commonly measured using the number of syllables 
produced per second. Repair measures examine the number of false starts, rep­
etitions, and self-pairs in a speech sample. Silence measures look at the number, 
duration, and location of pauses in a speech sample, and some researchers have 
also argued for the use of the proportion of time spent speaking out of the total 
time used to produce the speech sample (Kormos & Dénes, 2004).As Abdel Latif 
(2013) noted, researchers have adapted speaking fluency measures to assess writ­
ing fluency. For example, measures of composing rate (e.g., number of words 
produced in a given amount of time) have been derived from measures of speech

---

<!-- page 30 -->

 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Variation in Second Language Use 13 
rate, measures of changes made to the text have been derived from measures of 
repair, and measures of a writer’s pausing have been derived from measures of 
silence or breakdown. He argued, however, that these measures cannot adequately 
capture the real-time dimension of writing and may also be affected by various 
task performance variables (e.g., planning time). Other product-based measures 
of writing fluency examine linguistic features characterizing specific rhetorical 
functions or the number and length of T-units or sentences, but Abdel Latif (2013) 
pointed out that such measures mainly assess writing accuracy or complexity 
rather than fluency. 
Along with the focus on CAF measures in research on variation in L2 use, 
some studies have examined variation in L2 learners’ knowledge and use of 
specific linguistic features or constructions (e.g., capitalization, epicene pronouns, 
discourse markers, phraseological units, and verb-argument constructions).While 
such features and constructions have frequently been quantitatively analyzed in 
terms of the frequency, accuracy, diversity, and sophistication of the forms used, 
qualitative analyses of their structural and functional characteristics and usage 
contexts are also common. 
2.3 Learner-Related Variables and Variation 
in Second Language Use 
A number of learner-related variables may give rise to variation in L2 use, 
including the learners’ L2 proficiency level, linguistic and cultural background, 
sociolinguistic characteristics such as age, gender, and education, and learning 
experience variables such as age of exposure, learning history, and patterns of L2 
use (Gablasova et al., 2019). Indeed, the effects of such learner-related variables on 
variation in L2 use have been a major research area in SLA research (e.g., Mackey 
& Gass, 2015).While SLA researchers who collect their own L2 data to address 
specific research questions can use existing instruments or devise reliable ways to 
obtain information about such variables, those who use existing learner corpora 
in their research are bound by the information that is available in the corpora and 
the specific ways in which information about the variables are gathered, both of 
which vary widely in different learner corpora. 
There is wide variation in the types of variables included in different learner 
corpora.With the importance of such variables and their reliability in SLA research 
in mind, recent learner corpus compilation efforts have increasingly prioritized the 
inclusion of information about such variables. For example, the Trinity Lancaster 
Corpus (TLC), “the largest corpus resource for the investigation of spoken 
(interactive) L2 English production” (Gablasova et al., 2019, p. 127), includes 
detailed information about each learner’s proficiency in English, first language 
background, cultural background (based on national affiliation), sociolinguistic 
characteristics including age,gender,and highest completed educational level,and 
language learning experience including age of first exposure to English, context

---

<!-- page 31 -->

 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
14 Variation in Second Language Use 
of L2 acquisition (e.g., at school in a foreign language classroom, in an English 
speaking country, etc.), and time spent in each kind of learning context. 
Among these learner-related variables, existing corpus-based studies of 
variation in L2 use have focused heavily on the effects of L2 proficiency and to 
a lesser extent L1 background but have generally not routinely attended to the 
effects of sociolinguistic variables or variables pertaining to language learning 
experience, with some exceptions (Gablasova et al., 2019), likely due to the fact 
that these two variables tend to be more commonly available in learner corpora 
than other variables. The rest of this section thus focuses on the effects of L2 
proficiency and L1 background on variation in L2 use, while echoing Gablasova 
et al.’s (2019) call for greater attention to other learner-related variables in this 
line of research. 
2.3.1 Second Language Profciency 
The examination of variation in L2 production among learners at different 
language proficiency levels has been a core focus of learner corpus research with 
a cross-sectional design. Bachman (1990) defines language proficiency as “the 
knowledge, competence, or ability in the use of a language, irrespective of how, 
where, or under what conditions it has been acquired” (p. 16). A proficiency 
scale is generally comprised of a series of ascending bands or levels of proficiency 
spanning over either the entire conceptual range of learner proficiency or, perhaps 
more commonly, the range relevant to a particular learning context, institution, 
or sector, with each band or level characterized with a set of features that make 
it different from other levels in the scale (e.g., Council of Europe, 2001). Clearly, 
corpus-based studies of cross-proficiency variation in L2 use would require 
that the spoken or written samples in the learner corpus being analyzed have 
proficiency-level assignments, and the validity of the findings of such studies 
would in a large part depend on the validity of the definition of proficiency in 
the corpus and the reliability with which proficiency-level assignments of the 
language samples in the corpus are made. 
Recognizing the fundamental importance of a valid conceptualization 
of proficiency and a reliable proficiency level assignment in SLA research in 
general and in learner corpus research in particular, Carlsen (2012) provided a 
comprehensive review of some of the most common methods used to assign 
texts to proficiency levels in learner corpora, with an overall distinction between 
learner-centered and text-centered methods. Learner-centered methods assign 
texts at proficiency levels based on learner (as opposed to text) characteristics, 
such as institutional status (e.g., school year, program level, or number of years 
studying a language at university level), teachers’ judgment of the learners’ levels, 
or the learners’ overall scores on a language proficiency test, which reflect not 
just the learners’ speaking or writing proficiency but their overall proficiency in 
the language. Text-centered methods assign texts to proficiency levels based on

---

<!-- page 32 -->

 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Variation in Second Language Use 15 
characteristics of the texts alone, without regard to learner characteristics, such 
as a teacher’s judgment of the levels of the texts or holistic scores of the spoken 
or written texts based on a rating scale. Some researchers have questioned the 
reliability of the use of learner characteristics, particularly institutional level, for 
learner proficiency-level assignment, because of the potential variation in the 
standards used for status assignment across educational institutions (e.g.,Thomas, 
1994). Other researchers, however, argue that program level is a developmentally 
valid method for assessing learners’ proficiency level, at least within a particular 
curricular program, as it is usually determined using criterion-based diagnostic 
decisions (e.g., Wolfe-Quintero et al., 1998). Scores of proficiency tests, either 
norm-referenced or criterion-referenced, offer a reliable way to assess the 
proficiency level of L2 learners regardless of their institutional status. Norm-
referenced tests compare the scores of a student or a group of students to the 
scores of a reference group, while criterion-referenced ones compare a learner’s 
knowledge against a predetermined criterion (e.g., a performance level or 
standard) in order to classify the learner’s mastery level of a particular skill area or 
the overall learning goal. Holistic scores of speaking or writing quality based on 
a rating scale provide a useful way to assess speaking or writing quality but not 
necessarily the overall language proficiency. For this reason, recent research has 
usually examined the relationship of variation in L2 use to holistic speaking or 
writing scores separately from its relationship to L2 proficiency. However, such 
scores can be combined with the learners’ overall performance in the proficiency 
test to inform the classification of learners into different proficiency levels (e.g., 
Gablasova et al., 2019). Carlsen (2012) took a somewhat different approach to 
assign the texts in a corpus of written essays produced by adult L2 Norwegian 
learners in response to two standardized tests of Norwegian as a second language. 
Specifically, each essay was evaluated by five to ten raters who had experience 
with the descriptors of the Common European Framework of Reference for 
Languages (CEFR) and subsequently assigned to a CEFR scale. This approach 
was argued to be both reliable and widely interpretable. All in all, while learner 
corpora may encode the proficiency levels of the texts they contain in one way or 
another, it is important to be aware of the differences among the various possible 
operationalizations of proficiency and utilize a valid and reliable operationalization 
that is appropriate for the analytical goals of the study. It is also important to 
interpret different results reported from studies using different conceptualizations 
of proficiency with caution. 
Lu (2011) was one of the earliest studies that adopted a large-scale learner 
corpus analysis approach to systematically investigate differences in the syntactic 
complexity of L2 writing among learners at different proficiency levels. Most 
prior studies that examined the same issue had resorted to manual analyses of 
small amounts of L2 writing data using small sets of syntactic complexity measures 
due to the labor-intensive nature of such analyses. With substantial variation in 
the choice and definition of the measures used among those studies, the results

---

<!-- page 33 -->

 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
16 Variation in Second Language Use 
reported on the same measure were often mixed.Taking advantage of the then 
newly released L2SCA for automated syntactic complexity analysis to address 
the methodological constraints of previous research, Lu (2011) used a large-
scale learner corpus to examine whether and how the 14 syntactic complexity 
indices in the L2SCA could differentiate learners at different proficiency levels. 
All 14 indices in the L2SCA were selected based on the recommendations from 
Wolfe-Quintero et al.’s (1998) research synthesis of CAF measures in L2 writing 
development.The corpus used in the study was the Written English Corpus of 
Chinese Learners (WECCL;Wen et al., 2005), which contains 3,678 argumentative, 
narrative, and expository essays (2304 timed, 1252 untimed) written by English 
major undergraduate students from nine Chinese universities.
 Lu (2011) operationalized proficiency using school level and justified the valid­
ity of this operationalization with its comparability to program level in ESL contexts. 
Specifically, it was noted that students admitted into the same English major program 
were generally expected to have the same proficiency level, and they must all pass the 
same set of required English courses to move on to the next level.As genre, timing 
condition, and institution were all found to significantly affect syntactic complexity, a 
subset of 422 timed argumentative essays from a single college was used to assess dif­
ferences in syntactic complexity across the four school levels. 
Ten of the 14 indices significantly differentiated either adjacent or nonadjacent 
school levels, and the patterns of variation across proficiency levels constituted a 
major analytical focus of Lu (2011).The three length-based measures tended to 
increase with proficiency level. Mean length of clause increased linearly across 
the four levels; it was significantly lower in Level 1 than in Levels 2–4 and addi­
tionally also significantly lower in Level 2 than in Level 4.The mean length of 
sentences and the mean length of T-units both increased linearly from Level 1 to 
Level 3 and then declined slightly from Level 3 to Level 4. Both measures were 
significantly lower in Level 1 than in Levels 2–4, and the decline from Level 3 to 
Level 4 was insignificant.The sentence complexity measure, clauses per sentence, 
declined linearly across the four levels, although the only significant difference 
was found between Level 1 and Level 4. Two clausal subordination measures, 
dependent clauses per clause and per T-unit,increased from Level 1 to Level 2 and 
then declined linearly from Level 2 to Level 4, with significantly higher values in 
Level 2 than in Level 4.The two coordinate phrase measures both increased lin­
early from Level 1 to Level 3 and stayed stable between Levels 3 and 4; coordinate 
phrases per T-unit was lower in Level 1 than in Level 3, and coordinate phrases per 
clause was lower in Level 1 than in both Levels 3 and 4.The two complex nomi­
nal measures increased linearly across the four levels; complex nominals per clause 
and complex nominals per T-unit were both significantly lower in Level 1 than in 
Levels 2–4, and complex nominals per clause was also significantly lower in Level 
2 than in Levels 3–4. No significant between-proficiency differences were found 
for clauses per T-unit, complex T-units per T-unit,T-units per sentence, and verb 
phrases per T-unit.

---

<!-- page 34 -->

 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Variation in Second Language Use 17 
The variation observed across the proficiency levels in the syntactic complexity 
of argumentative writing produced by Chinese learners of English was taken 
to support several claims regarding the relationship of syntactic complexity to 
proficiency. For example, the nonlinear pattern observed for dependent clauses 
per clause and per T-unit and the linear increase observed for complex nominals 
per clause and per T-unit offered support for the developmental prediction of 
“non-linear complexification as far as subordination is concerned” (Ortega, 2003, 
p.514), based on the expectation that advanced proficiency learners should engage 
in complexification more at the phrasal, rather than the clausal level. Employing 
an automated tool to analyze a large-scale learner corpus with a comprehensive 
set of indices, this study usefully expanded the scope of previous research on 
cross-proficiency variation in L2 writing syntactic complexity and allowed for 
richer comparisons among different indices. It is important to note, however, that 
the findings and the claims they support should only be interpreted to reflect 
patterns and trends at the group level and not at the level of individual learners. 
In addition, the discussion in the following two sections of this chapter should 
make it clear that even such group-level patterns and trends may be subject to 
influences of various learner and task variables. 
Römer and Garner (2019) investigated how the use of verb-argument 
constructions (VACs) varies in the spoken English of L2 learners across proficiency 
levels, using datasets of L1 Italian and Spanish learner language from the Trinity 
Lancaster Corpus Sample (TLCS). The study also examined the effect of the 
learners’ L1 background as well as the potential effect of L1 English usage on 
the learners’VAC production, and we return to a discussion of those effects in 
Section 2.4 and Chapter 4, respectively. A component of the larger TLC, the 
TLCS contained transcripts of recorded speech produced by 129 L1 English 
examiners and 1,244 L2 English learners sitting Trinity College London’s Graded 
Examinations in Spoken English (GESE).The corpus represented learners from 
a range of L2 proficiency levels (lower intermediate or B1, upper intermediate 
or B2, and advanced or C1/C2 on the CEFR scale) and L1 backgrounds, all 
of whom completed either three or four different speaking tasks during a test 
session: Presentation (C1 and C2 only), interactive tasks (B2–C2 only), discussion 
(all levels), and conversation (all levels). The authors included in their analysis 
nine TLCS subcorpora, each representing one of three proficiency levels (low­
intermediate or B1, high-intermediate or B2, and advanced or C1/C2 combined), 
one of two L1 backgrounds (L1 Italian and L1 Spanish), and one of three national 
affiliations (Italy for L1 Italian and Mexico and Spain for L1 Spanish), with a 
total of 750 samples (60 to 100 samples in each subcorpus). For each subcorpus, 
they retrieved all instances of the following five VACs:“V about n” (e.g., care about 
fashion), “V for n” (e.g., ask for a taxi driver), “V in n” (e.g., believe in ghosts), “V 
like n” (e.g., look like real people), and “V with n” (e.g., talk with your cousin).This 
was done by first using CQL (Corpus Query Language) syntax searches in the 
Sketch Engine (Kilgarriff et al., 2014) to extract verbs followed by about, for, in,

---

<!-- page 35 -->

 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
18 Variation in Second Language Use 
like, or with, possibly with one intervening word (e.g., a hesitation marker such 
as er), and then manually filtering the extracted instances, resulting in a total of 
7067 instances across the five target VACs. 
For each subcorpus and each target VAC, the authors created a verb lemma 
frequency list as well as a verb type-token overview.Two types of analyses were then 
performed to address the research question pertaining to changes in L2 learners’ 
productive verb-construction knowledge with increasing proficiency. First, the 
authors compared the verb type and token distributions for each target VAC 
across learner proficiency levels and L1 groups. Specifically, they compared the 
most frequent verbs in each VAC across the subcorpora and calculated normalized 
entropy scores for each VAC in each subcorpus as a measure of the uncertainty 
of the probability distribution of verb types within the VAC, with lower values 
(closer to 0) indicating a more uneven and predictable distribution and higher 
values (closer to 0) indicating a more even and unpredictable distribution. The 
normalized entropy scores for each VAC in the British National Corpus (BNC; 
Burnard, 2007) were also computed to provide an L1 usage benchmark. The 
normalized entropy analysis showed that, for four of the target VACs examined, 
verb type and token distributions were found to be more predictable and closer to 
L1 English usage for the advanced learners (C1/C2) than for the low-intermediate 
(B1) learners. Particularly for “V about n” and “V with n”, the advanced learners 
(C1/C2) had the lowest entropy scores across all three L1 groups. 
Second, the authors also conducted correlation analyses on verb-in-VAC 
distributions in order to systematically compare the verbs produced for each VAC 
in each subcorpus. Specifically for cross-proficiency variation, they correlated 
verb distributions in a VAC between each learner subcorpus and native usage 
(e.g., L1 Italian B2 vs. BNC; nine correlations for each target VAC) as well as 
between each pair of proficiency levels with L1 kept constant (e.g., L1 Italian B2 
vs. L1 Italian C1/C2; nine correlations for each target VAC).The results showed 
that across the three L1 groups, the correlations with native usage were higher at 
the C1/C2 level than at the B1 level, indicating that compared to low proficiency 
learners, high proficiency learners produced verbs in VACs more similar to L1 
English usage patterns.The results further showed stronger correlation values for 
adjacent proficiency levels (i.e., B1 vs. B2 and B2 vs. C1/C2) than for nonadjacent 
proficiency levels (i.e., B1 vs. C1/C2) across all three L1 groups, indicating that 
learners at adjacent proficiency levels produced sets of verbs in VACs that are 
more similar than learners at proficiency levels that are further away. 
Taking advantage of the large-scale spoken data encoded with rich learner 
variables in the TLCS and building upon analytical methods tested in previous 
research, Römer and Garner (2019) expanded on findings regarding cross-
proficiency variation in the production of verbs in VACs in L2 English from 
previous studies that were either narrower in scope (e.g., comparing only advanced 
learners to L1 English speakers) or smaller in scale (Ellis & Ferreira-Junior, 2009; 
Eskildsen, 2009).The authors carefully controlled for the effect of L1 background

---

<!-- page 36 -->

 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Variation in Second Language Use 19 
in examining cross-proficiency variation and also called for closer attention to the 
potential effects of the speaking tasks learners at different proficiency levels were 
required to perform in future research. 
In another study of cross-proficiency variation in L2 learners’ constructional 
knowledge and use using the TLCS, Gilquin (2019) examined variation in the 
use of light verb constructions (LVCs) in spoken English among English as 
a foreign language (EFL) and English as a second language (ESL) learners at 
different proficiency levels. LVCs, which consist of a delexical verb (generally a 
high-frequency verb that is semantically light, e.g., get, give, make, and take) and an 
eventive noun (e.g., have a look, take a walk), have been shown to be problematic 
for L2 English learners (e.g., Altenberg & Granger, 2001). The author used the 
same three groups of data employed in Römer and Garner’s (2019) study (i.e., 
learners from Italy, Mexico, and Spain) to represent EFL learners. In addition, data 
from 260 Indian learners of English was also included to represent ESL learners, 
with 116, 107, and 37 learners at the B1, B2, and C1/C2 levels, respectively, and 
data from 1,124 L1 English examiners in all discussion and conversation tasks was 
used as a reference corpus.As such, a total of 13 subcorpora were analyzed:Three 
for each of the four learner groups (one for each proficiency level), and one for 
the L1 English reference group. The author generated a concordance for each 
of the following three lemmas, MAKE, TAKE, and GIVE and then manually 
examined each concordance line to determine whether it contained an LVC or 
not.This procedure allowed the authors to identify a total of 1,503 LVCs across 
the 13 subcorpora. 
Among the EFL learners, the normalized frequency of LVCs was found to 
increase modestly but steadily across the three proficiency levels. B1 learners used 
significantly fewer LVCs than C1/C2 learners, and both B1 and C1/C2 learners 
used significantly fewer LVCs than L1 English examiners. However, the pattern of 
variation differed among the LVCs with the three verbs. Compared to L1 English 
examiners, EFL learners at all three levels were underused LVCs with GIVE, those 
at the B1 and C1/C2 levels also underused LVCs with MAKE, while learners at 
all three levels overused LVCs with TAKE. A qualitative analysis indicated that 
the increase in EFL learners’ proficiency was accompanied by an increase in the 
semantic abstraction and syntactic complexity of LVCs. For example, while B1 
learners from Spain used two concrete nouns (i.e., film and picture) in LVCs with 
MAKE, C1/C2 learners from Spain used a range of abstract nouns (e.g., change, 
discovery, difference, effort, and mistake). In terms of syntactic complexity, C1/C2 
learners were found to use LVCs in the passive voice with two arguments, that is, 
a subject and either a direct object (e.g., he was afraid of being given a … treatment…) 
or a by-agent (e.g., the single most important … was … the allegations made by …) 
more frequently than B1 learners, who primarily used with the active voice or the 
passive voice with a single argument (e.g., the film was made in a small village …). 
Compared to EFL learners, ESL learners exhibited a different pattern of cross-
proficiency variation in their use of LVCs. The normalized frequency of LVCs

---

<!-- page 37 -->

 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
20 Variation in Second Language Use 
was comparable between ESL learners at the B1 level and L1 English examiners, 
dropped insignificantly at the B2 level, but then increased to a level that was 
significantly higher than both the B1 level and L1 English examiners at C1/ 
C2. In other words, while ESL learners at the B1 and B2 levels used LVCs with 
comparable frequency as L1 English examiners, those at the most advanced level 
overused LVCs more than L1 English examiners, a scenario the author considered 
problematic. While the LVCs with the three verbs were analyzed separately, no 
significant difference was found among learners of the three proficiency levels 
in the frequency of any of the three verbs; compared to L1 English examiners, 
learners at B1 and B2 levels were found to overuse MAKE, while learners at B2 
and C1/C2 levels were found to overuse TAKE. ESL learners were also found 
to use LVCs with a greater level of syntactic complexity, as evidenced in their 
greater use of the passive voice, including some cases of three-argument passive 
constructions that were absent in the EFL data (e.g., I’ve been given many suggestions 
by my parents), more frequent premodification of the deverbal noun (e.g., take the 
few basic steps), the presence of coordinated preverbal nouns (e.g., gives them all the 
love and caring), and combinations of different types of complexity. 
The author concluded that the data in the TLC allowed for a productive 
exploration of the variation in the use of LVCs among EFL and ESL speakers at 
different proficiency levels and L1 English speakers. Both EFL and ESL learners 
used LVCs with different verbs in divergent ways, indicating the usefulness to 
take into account the specific verbs used in such constructions in analyzing 
cross-proficiency variation in LVC usage. In general, as EFL learners’ proficiency 
level increased, they tended to use LVCs more frequently with greater semantic 
abstraction and syntactic complexity, converging toward L1 English usage. ESL 
learners, however, tended to overuse LVCs as their proficiency level increased, and 
the author interpreted this tendency as resulting from a constructional “teddy bear 
effect”. These results highlight the importance of taking learners’ acquisitional 
context into account in examining cross-proficiency variation in L2 use. Similar 
to Römer and Garner (2019), the author cautioned against a potential task effect 
on the results reported, as the data in the corpus represented examiner-candidate 
interactions only, which could differ from spontaneous interactions. 
In addition to cross-proficiency variation in L2 writing syntactic complexity 
and L2 learner’s knowledge and usage of specific constructions, a line of learner 
corpus research has examined cross-proficiency variation in L2 learners’ use of 
multi-word sequences or phraseological units. For example, Staples et al. (2013) 
examined variation in the use of lexical bundles in written responses in the 
TOEFL iBT produced by L2 English learners across three proficiency levels. 
Lexical bundles refer to consecutive sequences of three or more words (e.g., in 
light of the) that meet both frequency and range (e.g., in terms of the number of 
different texts in which they occur) thresholds in a corpus, regardless of whether 
they are structurally or semantically complete (Biber et al., 1999). The authors 
used a corpus of 960 written texts produced by 480 L2 English learners (two texts

---

<!-- page 38 -->

 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Variation in Second Language Use 21 
per learner) in response to two writing tasks (integrated and independent) on the 
TOEFL iBT for a total of 249,417 words. All texts were scored on a five-point 
scale with half-point increments, the mean score of the two texts was computed 
for each learner, and the learners were then divided into three proficiency levels 
based on their mean scores as follows: Low (1.00–2.75; 170 learners), intermediate 
(2.76–3.75; 165 learners), and high (3.76–5.00; 145 learners).The authors used an 
R script to retrieve four-word sequences and determine whether they met the 
criteria for analysis (i.e., occurring in 12 or more texts written for two or more 
separate prompts). The sequences were categorized as prompt-based bundles if 
they occurred in a prompt and were clearly related to the writing topic; all other 
bundles were categorized as non-prompt-based bundles. Normed token rates of 
occurrence (per 100 words) of prompt and non-prompt bundles were calculated 
for each learner. Non-prompt-based bundles occurring 25 or more times in the 
corpus were functionally categorized into referential bundles, stance bundles, and 
discourse organizing bundles; they were also categorized as either fixed bundles (if 
all four slots were filled by the same words 50% or more of the time) or variable 
slot bundles (if at least one of the four slots was filled by a different word 50% or 
more of the time). 
The authors found that the normed token rates of occurrence of lexical bundles 
decreased as the learners’ proficiency level increased and that low proficiency 
learners relied more heavily on prompt-based bundles than the other two groups. 
Results of the functional analysis showed that the percentages of the three types of 
bundles used by learners at the three proficiency levels were largely comparable. 
All three groups of learners used attitudinal/evaluative stance bundles (e.g., agree 
with the statement, I am interested in) the most to express their opinions about the 
topic, while they only used two referential bundles (i.e., are a lot of, there are a lot) to 
frame reference to entities.Analysis of bundle fixedness also revealed little cross-
proficiency variation, and about 90% of the bundles used by all three proficiency 
groups were fixed bundles. Overall, the authors concluded that learners relied 
more heavily on fixed bundles provided by the immediate context but moved 
toward self-constructed patterns of language use as their proficiency increased. 
They further argued that the lack of cross-proficiency variability in bundle 
functions and fixedness may be explained by the learners’ greater exposure to 
stance and discourse organizing bundles than referential bundles and to fixed 
sequences than to variable sequences and recommended greater pedagogical 
attention to referential bundles and variable slot bundles. 
Garner (2016) noted that Staples et al.’s (2013) analysis of variable slot bundles 
“did not fully reveal the degree of variability that students use” (p. 35) as they only 
categorized a bundle as variable if it contained one or more slot that varied 50% 
of the time and only analyzed the percentage of such bundles against fixed ones. 
To address this limitation, the author took a phrase-frames approach to examine 
variation in the production of variable multi-word sequences by L1 German EFL 
learners at five different proficiency levels.The data used in the study came from

---

<!-- page 39 -->

 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
  
 
  
  
 
 
  
 
 
  
 
 
 
  
 
  
  
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
22 Variation in Second Language Use 
the EF-Cambridge Open Language Database (EFCAMDAT; Geertzen et al., 
2014), which at the time of the author’s writing consisted of 33 million words of 
texts written by EFL learners for coursework on Englishtown, Education First’s 
(EF) online school. The Englishtown curriculum contained 16 levels ranging 
from A1 to C2 of the CEFR. Coursework at each level consisted of eight units, 
and learners completed a written assignment based on a writing prompt at the 
end of each unit, such as writing an argumentative essay or a summary of a text. 
The author used the German subsection of the corpus, which contained about 
2.2 million words from 28,000 texts produced by learners who claimed German 
nationality, excluding the small number of texts at Level 16 (C2). 
The author followed Römer’s (2010) definition of phrase-frames as recur­
rent multi-word sequences that are identical with the exception of one internal, 
variable slot that can be filled by different words (e.g., the * of the, in which the 
variable slot indicated by * can be filled by such words as beginning, end, and size, 
etc.).The automated text analysis tool KfNgram (Fletcher 2002–2007) was used 
to extract a separate list of four-word phrase-frames for texts at each of the five 
levels considered.The automatically extracted phrase-frame lists were then man­
ually refined to exclude items that included metadata related to the identification 
of the learner or text, that had an open slot at the beginning or end (e.g., * as 
well as), that overlapped with a higher-frequency phrase-frame (e.g., the fourth * of 
and four * of my were both part of the five-word phrase-frame the fourth * of my, 
and in such cases only the phrase-frame with the highest frequency was kept), or 
that did not constitute meaningful units (e.g., the * twice a with the variable slot 
filled by floor and dogs).After filtering these phrase-frames and correcting all spell­
ing errors, the author identified the top 100 most frequent four-word phrase-
frames for each proficiency level for further analysis.To assess cross-proficiency 
difference in the variability of the phrase-frames used, the author calculated the 
variant/phrase-frame ratio (VPR=number of variants / phrase-frame token 
frequency) for each phrase-frame in each proficiency level and characterized 
phrase-frames as highly variable (VPR>.70), variable (.30≤ VPR ≤ .70), or fixed 
(VPR<.30).To assess cross-proficiency variation in the predictability of phrase-
frames used, the author computed a predictability score (PS=percentage of 
phrase-frame tokens in which the word that most frequently filled the variable 
slot occurred) for each phrase-frame in each proficiency level and characterized 
phrase-frames as highly predictable (PS>75%), predictable (25% ≤ PS≤ 75%), 
or unpredictable (PS<25%). Finally, to assess cross-proficiency variation in the 
structural and functional characteristics of the phrase-frames used, the author 
categorized each phrase-frame structurally as a verb-based frame, a frame with 
other content words, or a function word frame and functionally as a referential 
expression, a stance expression, a discourse organizing expression, or a special 
conversational expression. 
The results showed that the normalized frequency of the 100 most frequent 
phrase-frames generally declined as the learners’ proficiency level increased.

---

<!-- page 40 -->

 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
  
  
 
 
  
  
 
  
 
Variation in Second Language Use 23 
Phrase-frame variability generally increased from low to high proficiency levels, 
with C1 learners using significantly more variable or highly variable phrase-frames 
than learners at all other levels, and B2 learners using significantly more variable 
or highly variable phrase-frames than both A1 and A2 learners.A general decrease 
in the predictability of the phrase-frames analyzed was observed from low to high 
proficiency levels, and a significant difference was found between Levels A1 and 
C1. No significant between-proficiency difference was found in the structural 
characteristics of phrase-frames (in terms of the percentage of phrase-frames of 
different structural types). In terms of phrase-frame functions, B2 and C1 learners 
were found to use phrase-frames to fulfill a wider variety of discourse functions 
than A1 and A2 learners. 
The author provided two possible explanations for the finding that higher 
proficiency learners tended to employ more variable and less predictable phrase-
frames to fulfill more diverse discourse functions than lower proficiency learners. 
First, from a usage-based perspective, higher proficiency learners likely had greater 
exposure to phrase-frame exemplars, and the increase in input would have helped 
entrench phrase-frames in their linguistic inventories and increase the variability 
that the phrase-frame could exhibit. Second, the increasing complexity of the 
communicative tasks they were required to perform likely affected the variability 
of the phrase-frames as well, as more variable phrase-frames were needed to 
meet the communicative needs of a wider variety of contexts.This second cause 
suggested by the author echoes the previously discussed point regarding the 
importance of examining task effects on the variation in L2 use. 
Marrying the research interest in cross-proficiency variation in the use 
of phraseological units with the usage-based approach to assessing syntactic 
sophistication (e.g., Kyle, 2016), Paquot (2019) proposed a set of new measures of 
phraseological complexity (including phraseological diversity and sophistication) 
and examined cross-proficiency variation in the phraseological complexity of L2 
written production.The learner texts used in the study were sampled from the 
Varieties of English for Specific Purposes database (VESPA), which consisted of 
98 research papers (3,436 words on average) written by French EFL learners 
for linguistics courses at the University of Louvain. Each text was evaluated 
by two professional raters (with a third adjudicator in case of disagreement) 
using the CEFR descriptors for linguistic competence, and all texts were rated 
at B2 (25 texts), C1 (62 texts), or C2 (11 texts). The author considered word 
combinations with three types of dependency relations in developing measures of 
phraseological diversity and sophistication, namely, adjective+noun (amod, e.g., 
overwhelming+majority), adverbial modifier+adjective, adverb, or verb (advmod, 
e.g., evenly+distribute), and verb+direct object (dobj, e.g., fill+gap). To identify 
occurrences of the dependency relations from the corpus, the author first used 
the Stanford CoreNLP (Manning et al., 2014) suite of tools to lemmatize 
and part-of-speech (POS) tag each text and parse it with the Stanford typed 
dependency relations, and subsequently extracted all pairs of words in the text

---

<!-- page 41 -->

 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
24 Variation in Second Language Use 
that instantiated these relations in the form of lemmas and simplified POS tags. 
Phraseological diversity was measured using the root type-token ratio (RTTR) 
of each dependency relation. Phraseological sophistication was operationalized 
in two ways:As the proportion of amod, advmod, and dobj tokens in the Academic 
Collocations List (ACL; Ackermann & Chen, 2013) and as the mean pointwise 
mutual information (MI) score for amod, advmod, and dobj dependencies in a 
reference corpus of published journal articles produced by L2 English scholars. 
No significant between-proficiency difference was found in the phraseological 
diversity measures or the phraseological sophistication measures based on the ACL. 
For the MI-based phraseological sophistication measure, the mean MI scores for 
each of the three dependencies increased from the lowest level (B2) to the highest 
level (C2), and significant differences were found between Levels B2 and C2 for 
amod dependencies, between Levels B2 and C1/C2 for advmod dependencies, 
and between B2/C1 and C2 for dobj dependencies. The author interpreted
