these results as indicating less reliance on non-collocational dependencies by 
higher proficiency L2 writers. This study makes highly useful theoretical and 
methodological contributions to the study of interlanguage complexity by arguing 
for the theoretical need to integrate the construct of phraseological complexity 
in such research and demonstrating “that corpus linguistics and natural language 
processing provide the tools and techniques to operationalize the dimensions of 
such a construct” (p. 140). 
While the brief review of learner corpus analysis of cross-proficiency variation 
in L2 use presented in this section is far from exhaustive, it has nonetheless 
allowed us to highlight several relevant methodological considerations in this 
line of research and important insights into the nature of this variation.Variable 
definitions of proficiency level have been used in different learner corpora and 
the studies utilizing them, but there is increasing effort to map proficiency levels 
to a common proficiency scale such as the CEFR to improve comparability across 
different corpora and studies. Large-scale learner corpora consisting of learner 
texts representing a wide range of proficiency levels, coupled with automated 
tools for various types of linguistic analysis, have substantially increased the scale 
and scope (in terms of the number of texts and the range of proficiency levels 
and linguistic features analyzed) of studies of cross-proficiency variation in L2 use, 
but manual analyses are still necessary for some types of analyses (e.g., contextual, 
functional) or to ensure the reliability of some automated analyses. The studies 
reviewed demonstrated that different patterns of variation may be found for 
different dimensions of language use as well as across different ranges of proficiency 
levels and that the absence of variation in some dimensions of language use for 
some proficiency levels does not necessarily contradict predictions of certain SLA 
theories. Several of these studies also pointed to the need to consider the effects 
of other learner- and task-related variables to fully account for the variation 
observed among learners with different proficiency levels.We turn to a discussion 
of the effect of L1 background on variation in L2 use in the next section.

---

<!-- page 42 -->

 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Variation in Second Language Use 25 
2.3.2 First Language Background 
The relationship between a learner’s L1 knowledge and L2 use has received 
much attention in SLA research. A sizable body of research has shown that L2 
learners’ L1 knowledge and experience can affect their L2 use in various ways 
(e.g., MacWhinney, 2012) and that differences between their L1 and the L2 can 
also affect the development and processing of L2 morphosyntax (e.g., McManus, 
2015).While some learner corpora contain spoken or written language samples 
produced by learners from a single L1 background, such as the WECCL, many 
learner corpora include language samples produced by learners from diverse L1 
backgrounds, such as the TLC and the International Corpus of Learner English 
(ICLE, Granger et al., 2009). Large-scale learner corpora that explicitly code 
each sample with information about the learner’s L1 background have facilitated 
corpus-based studies of the effect of L1 on L2 use, substantially extending the 
analytical scope (e.g., in terms of the linguistic features and L1 groups examined) 
and/or scale of the line of SLA research on this topic. 
Some corpus-based studies of variation in L2 use focused exclusively on the 
effect of L2 learners’ L1 backgrounds on their use of specific target linguistic 
features in spoken or written production.A good example of this type of research 
is Stormbom (2018), who investigated variation in the use of epicene pronouns he, 
he or she, and they among L1 English writers and L2 English writers with different 
L1 backgrounds, with the choice of such pronouns interpreted as an indicator 
of the extent of non-sexist language use in L2 writing. For L2 writing data, the 
author used 13 subcorpora of the ICLE representing learners with European 
languages as their L1, namely, Bulgarian, Czech, Dutch, Finnish, German, Italian, 
Norwegian, Polish, Russian, Spanish, Swedish, and Turkish. These subcorpora 
contained a total of 4,218 samples (231 to 514 per L1 group) and 2.8 million 
words. For L1 writing data, the author used the Louvain Corpus of Native English 
Essays (LOCNESS; Granger, 1996), which contained 232 samples produced by 
college-level L1 American English writers and 204 samples by college- and 
A-level L1 British English writers, for a total of 3.2 million words.The author 
used WordSmith Tools 5.0 (Scott, 2008) to extract the following three pronoun 
types from the two corpora: Generic he, coordinations (he or she, he/she, s/he), and 
singular they. To this end, the author designed a set of queries that consisted of 
search words representing a potential antecedent, including indefinite pronouns 
such as someone and everybody and determiners such as the and each, and context 
words (i.e., all forms of the three epicene pronoun types), with a maximum of 
25 words between the search and context words.The surrounding context of each 
instance was then manually examined to exclude referential uses of the pronouns. 
While the normalized frequencies of the epicene pronouns in the L1 and L2 
corpora were comparable (103 vs. 105 per 100,000 words), L1 writers were found 
to use singular they significantly more frequently than L2 writers and generic he 
significantly less frequently than L2 writers.Among the 13 L2 subcorpora, seven

---

<!-- page 43 -->

 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
26 Variation in Second Language Use 
were found to use generic he significantly more frequently than the L1 corpus, 
namely, Russian, Italian, French, German, Dutch, Czech, and Finnish. In terms of 
the preferred gender-inclusive or non-sexist alternative, four L2 subcorpora used 
he or she and singular they with roughly equal frequency (i.e., Bulgarian, Dutch, 
French, and Norwegian), and nine L2 corpora used he or she more frequently, 
while the L1 corpus used singular they more frequently. The antecedent type 
appeared to have affected both L1 and L2 writers’ choice of epicene pronouns. 
For example, both L1 and L2 writers used singular they in reference to indefinite 
pronouns (e.g., someone) more frequently than noun phrases (e.g., a person), while 
the opposite was true for he and he or she. Citing Bußmann and Hellinger (2003), 
the author attributed the variation in the L2 subcorpora partly to L1 influence, 
arguing that L2 writers who used generic he the most frequently had L1s that 
are “gendered languages with a traditional practice of using masculine generics” 
(p. 15). The author further discussed L2 input and L2 writers’ sociocultural 
background (e.g., public attention to issues related to language and gender) as 
additional factors that may have given rise to the variation observed. 
In a study that largely shared a similar research design as that of Stormbom 
(2018) but with a focus on the use of a different linguistic feature in oral 
production, Castello and Gesuato (2019) investigated variation in the use of 
lexical backchannels in the oral discourse of L2 English learners from differ­
ent L1 backgrounds, with backchanneling understood to be a signal of active 
listenership and an important manifestation of discourse competence. For 
their analysis, they used the Sketch Engine to compile a subcorpus from the 
TLCS that included speech samples produced for the Discussion, Conversation, 
and Interactive tasks of the GESE by L2 English learners from China, Italy, 
and India who achieved the levels of B2, C1, or C2. They focused on lexical 
backchannels of the following two categories: Convergence (Category 1) and 
Request for Confirmation (Category 2).The first category included 25 items, 
such as absolutely, probably, I know, that’s right, and that’s possible, while the second 
category included a single item, namely, really. The two researchers indepen­
dently inspected the concordances of such items retrieved from the subcor­
pus to determine whether they were instances of the two focal categories of 
backchannels and resolved all discrepancies through discussion. Learners from 
all three L1 backgrounds were found to use the lexical backchannels analyzed, 
with those in Category 1 being more frequent than those in Category 2.They 
reported that Chinese learners of English used both categories of lexical back-
channels most frequently and argued that L1 transfer likely played a role here, 
citing Clancy et al.’s (1996) observation that Chinese speakers tend to prefer 
“lexically contentful” backchannels over interjections and vocalizations. They 
further reported differences in the lexical backchannels employed to express the 
same function among the three groups of learners. Specifically, Italian learners 
of English and to a lesser degree Chinese learners of English tended to express 
convergence using items that convey uncertainty, such as maybe, possible, and I

---

<!-- page 44 -->

 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Variation in Second Language Use 27 
think, while Indian learners of English preferred items that convey certainty 
in their supportive turns, such as right and sure. The authors argued that these 
differences may be partly attributed to differences in conversational topics and 
partly due to transfer from the L2 learners’ pragmatic behavior in their L1 
linguistic and cultural contexts.While the study limited its analytical scope to 
backchannels of two categories and L2 learners from three L1 backgrounds, it 
provided valuable insights into L1-related variation in the use of lexical back-
channels in L2 oral production with useful implications for understanding, 
assessing, and possibly enhancing L2 learner’s backchanneling behavior, fluency 
in interaction, and oral performance in general. 
In addition to studies that focused exclusively on the effect of L2 learners’ L1 
backgrounds on the variation in their L2 use without considering its relation to 
or interaction with L2 proficiency level (i.e., with learners at different proficiency 
levels lumped together), some learner corpus studies have taken L2 proficiency 
level into account in one way or another in examining the L1 effect on L2 use 
or acquisition. In a study set out to verify whether previous proficiency-based 
claims regarding variation in L2 writing syntactic complexity hold among learn­
ers with different L1 backgrounds, Lu and Ai (2015) investigated variation in 
the syntactic complexity of L2 English writing among college-level L2 English 
learners with different L1 backgrounds.Their L2 writing data consisted of 1,400 
argumentative essays sampled from the International Corpus of Learner English 
Version 2.0 (ICLE 2.0; Granger et al., 2009), with 200 produced by EFL learners 
from each of the following seven L1 backgrounds: Bulgarian, Chinese, French, 
German, Japanese, Russian, and Tswana.The L1 groups represented four language 
families: Sino-Tibetan, Japonic, Niger-Congo, and Indo-European. In terms of 
proficiency level, the Chinese, Japanese, and Tswana learners were character­
ized as upper intermediate, while learners with the other four L1 backgrounds 
were characterized as advanced, based on quality ratings of a random sample of 
20 essays from each group using the CEFR descriptors for writing (Granger 
et al., 2009). For comparison with L1 English writing data, the authors also sam­
pled 200 argumentative essays produced by L1 English university students in the 
US from the LOCNESS.They analyzed each essay using the 14 measures in the 
L2SCA that captured the following dimensions of syntactic complexity: Overall 
sentence complexity (i.e., clauses per sentence), length of production unit (e.g., 
mean length of clause), amount of coordination (e.g., coordinate phrases per 
clause), amount of subordination (e.g., dependent clauses per clause), and phrasal 
complexity (e.g., complex nominals per clause). 
They reported that, when all L2 writers were treated as a single group, 
they differed significantly from the L1 group in only three of the 14 measures. 
However, when the seven L2 groups were compared with the L1 group separately, 
significant differences emerged between the L1 group and one or more L2 groups 
in all 14 measures. More importantly, they found varied patterns of difference from 
the L1 group among the three upper intermediate L2 groups (Chinese, Japanese,

---

<!-- page 45 -->

 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
28 Variation in Second Language Use 
and Tswana) as well as among the four advanced L2 groups and argued that the 
intergroup variation in syntactic complexity could not be explained by proficiency 
alone but that the learners’ L1s likely also played a role in the syntactic complexity 
in their L2 writing.They further discussed the implications of their findings for 
previous claims about the relationship of syntactic complexity to L2 proficiency. 
For example, previous studies associated greater use of coordinate sentences with 
lower levels of L2 proficiency (Bardovi-Harlig, 1992; Norris & Ortega, 2009). 
Their results, however, showed that three advanced groups (French, German, and 
Russian) produced significantly more sentential coordination than the L1 group, 
while the Chinese group,which was ranked lowest in terms of proficiency among 
the seven groups, was the only group that produced significantly less sentential 
coordination than the L1 group.They noted the coincidence of this observation 
with the fact that coordination of two independent clauses does not always entail 
the use of a coordinating conjunction but may be indicated by punctuation in 
Chinese (Li & Thompson, 1981). 
They also tentatively noted a potential influence of the typological similarities 
and differences among the L1s examined (seven non-English L1s and English) 
on the syntactic complexity patterns observed for the different learner groups. 
Specifically, the L1s of the four advanced groups (Bulgarian, French, German, 
and Russian) are from the same language family as English (i.e., Indo-European), 
while the L1s of the three upper intermediate groups (i.e., Chinese, Japanese, and 
Tswana) belong to different language families (i.e., Sino-Tibetan, Japonic, and 
Niger-Congo). Compared to the upper intermediate groups, the four advanced 
groups displayed usage patterns closer to that of the L1 English group. However, 
among the four advanced groups, L1 French and Bulgarian learners showed 
patterns of usage that were the most similar to that of the L1 English group, 
despite the fact that German is typologically the closest to English and that L1 
German learners supposedly had the highest proficiency level among all learner 
groups. 
Their study was largely descriptive, and the authors hinted at but did not 
attempt to systematically explain what features in the learners’ L1s likely gave rise 
to the L1-related differences observed in the syntactic complexity of L2 writing. 
Nevertheless, their findings highlighted the ways in which the syntactic complexity 
of L2 writing may vary among L2 learners with different L1 backgrounds, even 
for those assessed at the same proficiency level, and thus the need to attend to the 
learners’ L1 backgrounds and possibly also the interaction between the effects of 
L1 and proficiency level in L2 writing syntactic complexity research. 
A probably more direct way in which L2 proficiency level has been taken 
into account in learner corpus studies of the L1 effect on the variation in L2 use 
is to explicitly control for L2 proficiency level in the analysis of the L1 effect. A 
good example of this method is the study by Römer and Garner (2019) discussed 
in Section 2.3.1 above of the variation in the use of VACs by L1 Italian and L1 
Spanish EFL learners at different proficiency levels in the TLC. In addition to the

---

<!-- page 46 -->

 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Variation in Second Language Use 29 
research question on the effect of L2 proficiency level on the verb-VAC association 
in the five target VACs produced by the learners, a second research question 
asked whether there would be any L1 effect on these verb-VAC associations.The 
authors addressed this research question using a correlation analysis on verb-in-
VAC distributions as well, specifically by correlating verb distributions in a VAC 
between each pair of L1 groups, with proficiency level kept constant (e.g., L1 
Italian B2 vs. L1 Mexican Spanish B2; nine correlations for each target VAC). 
The analysis revealed strong correlations between Italian, Mexican Spanish, and 
Iberian Spanish learner groups when proficiency level was controlled for, ranging 
from .54 (L1 Italian B1 vs. L1 Mexican Spanish B1 for “V for n”) to 1.00 (L1 
Italian B1 vs. L1 Iberian Spanish B1 for “V like n”).The authors concluded that, 
when it comes to the five target VACs investigated in their study, learners from 
the different Romance language backgrounds included in their analysis were very 
similar in their VAC knowledge as well as in their verb-in-VAC production. 
Finally, the interaction of the effects of L2 learners’ L1 backgrounds and 
their L2 proficiency levels on the variation in L2 use has also been examined in 
some learner corpus studies. For example, Shatz (2019) looked into the effect 
of L2 English learners’ L1 backgrounds as well as the effect of the interaction 
of L1 with proficiency level on the learners’ capitalization abilities. He used an 
earlier version of the EFCAMDAT that contained over 133,000 scripts writ­
ten by nearly 38,000 EFL learners representing seven different L1s for course­
work on Englishtown. About 36% of the scripts in the corpus contained error 
annotations, and capitalization errors were marked with a “capitalization” tag 
in cases where the learner failed to capitalize a word (e.g., by Sunday) or capi­
talized a word that should not be capitalized (e.g., by Tomorrow). The author 
used the learners’ nationalities where 90% of the population speak a single L1 
to approximate their L1 backgrounds, as the corpus did not directly provide 
information on the learners’ L1s. Specifically, all error-annotated scripts pro­
duced by learners in Levels 1 to 15 from seven different nationalities where over 
90% of the population speak a single L1 were included in the analysis, as there 
were insufficient samples for Level 6 for these learners and insufficient samples 
for learners from other nationalities meeting the L1 approximation criterion. 
Shatz’s (2019) findings showed that learners of all L1 backgrounds, particularly 
those at lower proficiency levels, made a large number of capitalization errors, 
both in terms of error frequency (errors per word) and proportion (out of all 
errors), with under-capitalization (i.e., not capitalizing words that should be 
capitalized) more common than over-capitalization (i.e., capitalizing words that 
should not be capitalized). Surprisingly, learners whose L1s shared the Latin 
script with English turned out to produce more capitalization errors, suggesting 
that (assumed) perceived similarity between the L1 and the L2 increases inter­
ference. He also reported an interaction between the learner’s L2 proficiency 
and potential L1 influence, with the differences between speakers of different 
L1 backgrounds at higher proficiency levels.

---

<!-- page 47 -->

 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
30 Variation in Second Language Use 
Taken together, the studies reviewed in this section show that L2 learner’s L1 
backgrounds can be expected to affect many (e.g., syntactic complexity) but not 
necessarily all aspects of their L2 use and acquisition, that the potential effects 
of the learners’ L1 backgrounds and their L2 proficiency levels should best be 
explored together, and that greater typological similarities between the learners’ 
L1s and the target L2 do not always translate into usage patterns that are more 
similar to the usage patterns of L1 speakers of the target L2. Methodologically, 
these studies demonstrate the usefulness of large-scale learner corpora containing 
spoken or written language samples produced by learners from diverse L1 back­
grounds in investigating the L1 effect on the variation in L2 use or acquisition. 
Meanwhile, they also point to the importance of considering the effects of differ­
ent task types and topics to achieve a more comprehensive understanding of the 
sources of variation in L2 use.We turn to a discussion of learner corpus studies of 
the effects of task-related variables on L2 use in the following section. 
2.4 Task-Related Variables and Variation 
in Second Language Use 
The effects of different task design features such as cognitive task complexity 
on the learners’ linguistic performance have been a highly productive line of 
research in task-based language teaching (TBLT) (e.g., Ellis, 2003; Skehan, 1998). 
Research in this area has drawn heavily from the Limited Attentional Capacity 
Model (Skehan, 1996, 1998) and the Cognition Hypothesis (Robinson, 1995, 
2001; Robinson & Gilabert, 2007), which posit rather different impacts of the 
cognitive complexity of a task on the CAF of the language that speakers and 
writers use to meet the communicative and cognitive demands of the task. From 
an information-processing perspective, task complexity has been defined as “the 
result of the attentional, memory, reasoning, and other information processing 
demands imposed by the structure of the task to the language learners” (Robinson, 
2001, p. 29). Skehan (1996) argues that tasks should be “sequenceable on some 
principled criterion, since the basis on which tasks are ordered will be a reflection 
of what attentional resources they require” (p. 51). The scheme he developed 
for such task sequencing differentiates code complexity (formal factors such as 
lexical and syntactic range and difficulty) from cognitive complexity (cognitive 
processing and cognitive familiarity) and communicative stress (e.g., time pressure 
and modality of communication) of a task.Advocating a limited-capacity view of 
attention, Skehan (1998) hypothesizes that because learners cannot equally attend 
to all dimensions of their linguistic performance, there will be a trade-off among 
those dimensions. Specifically, he predicts that in cognitively demanding tasks, 
learners will direct their attention primarily to content and fluency, and there will 
be competition for attention between complexity and accuracy. 
Robinson (2001), however, holds a multiple-resource view to attention. His 
triadic framework distinguishes cognitive task complexity from task conditions and

---

<!-- page 48 -->

 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Variation in Second Language Use 31 
task difficulty.Task complexity is seen as consisting of two categories of cognitive 
dimensions, namely, resource-directing (e.g.,+/- few elements,+/- here-and­
now,+/- no reasoning demand) and resource-depleting (e.g.,+/- planning,+/- 
single task,+/- prior knowledge), that can be manipulated during task design, 
with “+” and “-” indicating the presence and absence of the dimension in the 
task.These cognitive dimensions constitute sequencing criteria based on which 
prospective decisions about task units are to be made. Task conditions have to 
do with the interactive demands of tasks, which are determined by participation 
variables (e.g., open vs. closed solution, one-way vs. two-way flow of information, 
and convergent vs. divergent goal orientations) and participant variables (e.g., 
gender, familiarity among participants or with task role, and status of power and 
solidarity). Task difficulty is not determined by cognitive factors contributing 
to the complexity of a task but learner factors, including affective variables 
(e.g., motivation, anxiety, and confidence) and ability variables (e.g., aptitude, 
proficiency, and intelligence). Robinson (1995, 2001) argues that for monologic 
tasks, an increase in task complexity along resource-directing dimensions (e.g., 
reasoning) will trigger learners to activate and allocate additional attentional 
resources to the linguistic forms they use to meet the greater resource demands 
and therefore give rise to less fluent but more accurate and more syntactically 
complex learner output. 
A plethora of empirical studies have been conducted to validate the Limited 
Attentional Capacity Model and the Cognition Hypothesis (e.g., Jackson & 
Suethanapornkul, 2013; Skehan, 2018), and the results have been mixed due to 
the substantial variation both in the manipulations of task complexity and in the 
measures used to assess CAF. Jackson and Suethanapornkul (2013) conducted 
a meta-analysis of nine comparable studies to investigate the overall effects of 
raising resource-directing demands of monologic tasks on the CAF of learner 
production.Their analysis identified small positive effects for accuracy (based on 
39 measures used in nine studies) and small negative effects for fluency (based on 
15 measures used in six studies), which support the Cognition Hypothesis, but 
statistically nonsignificant effects for syntactic complexity (based on 17 measures 
from eight studies), in contrast to the positive effects posited in the Cognition 
Hypothesis. 
As Alexopoulou et al. (2017) pointed out, while the importance of task effects 
in learner language analysis has been widely recognized in SLA research, such 
effects have typically been examined in controlled experimental studies but have 
not traditionally been a focus of learner corpus research. In an effort to offer a 
conceptual and methodological example of how the insights from TBLT can be 
used to inform the analysis of task effects in learner corpus analysis,Alexopoulou 
et al. (2017) investigated how the complexity and accuracy of L2 learners’ 
language use in both global and specific features or structures may vary as a result 
of differences in aspects of task design, particularly with respect to task complexity, 
task type, and instructional focus.The authors cited their focus on L2 writing as

---

<!-- page 49 -->

 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
32 Variation in Second Language Use 
the reason to exclude fluency from their analysis. Different from studies that took 
an experimental approach with the specific parameters of task design carefully 
controlled, the authors sought to take advantage of the opportunities to analyze 
learner data at a large scale afforded by learner corpora collected from online 
language learning platforms. Specifically, they used the prereleased second version 
of the EFCAMDAT, which contained 1,180,543 scripts written by 174,771 
English learners from around the world for coursework on Englishtown. The 
scripts in the corpus were analyzed for accuracy and complexity. For accuracy, the 
authors first used the 24 error labels provided by the EF teachers in the corpus 
to identify errors and calculate relative error frequency.They then also examined 
target-like uses of prepositions and past-tense verbs in obligatory contexts.This 
was done by performing POS tagging of a version of the scripts with teacher 
corrections using the TreeTagger (Schmid, 1994), identifying obligatory contexts 
as words tagged as prepositions and past-tense verbs in those scripts, and then 
counting correct uses by subtracting omission and misformation errors from 
obligatory contexts.The Measure of Textual Lexical Diversity (MTLD; McCarthy 
& Jarvis, 2010) was used to assess global lexical complexity, and the following 
three measures were used to assess global syntactic complexity:Average sentence 
length, mean length of clause, and subordinate clauses per T-unit. Several fine-
grained indices of syntactic and lexical complexity were also computed, such as 
number of wh-phrases per sentence and number of gerundial -ing forms, among 
others. The complexity indices were computed using the code developed by 
Vajjala and Meurers (2012), which integrated the lexical complexity features from 
the Lexical Complexity Analyzer (Lu, 2012) and the syntactic complexity features 
from the L2SCA. 
The authors used the categories and features in task-based frameworks to 
characterize the tasks in the corpus. For example, the descriptive writing task 
that asked learners to complain about a cruise was characterized as involving low 
vocabulary load, medium structure, and high clarity in the Limited Attention 
Capacity Model (Skehan, 1998); the same task was also characterized as involving 
many similar task elements, talking about there and then (as opposed to here 
and now), likely entailing reasoning, and requiring perspective taking in light 
of the Cognition Hypothesis (Robinson & Gilabert, 2007). Based on these 
task characterizations, the authors made six predictions following the Limited 
Attention Capacity Model and the Cognition Hypothesis in terms of which task 
types (i.e., descriptive, narrative, and professional), which specific tasks within each 
task type (e.g., complaining about a cruise vs. complaining about a restaurant as 
descriptive tasks), and what instructional focus of the unit would elicit language 
with higher accuracy and linguistic complexity. For example, following the claim 
in Skehan’s Limited Attention Capacity Model that tasks with low vocabulary 
load would keep cognitive resources available to speakers to focus on linguistic 
forms, the authors predicted that writing a job ad and complaining about a cruise, 
both of which were coded as involving low vocabulary load, would elicit language

---

<!-- page 50 -->

 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Variation in Second Language Use 33 
with higher accuracy and syntactic complexity than other tasks (Prediction 1). 
Following Robinson’s Cognition Hypothesis, the authors predicted high accuracy 
for professional tasks and low accuracy for narratives, as the former were coded 
as more complex than the latter (Prediction 2). The results from the accuracy 
and complexity analysis were used to confirm or disconfirm each prediction. For 
example, Prediction 1 was not confirmed as the cruise complaint task elicited 
language with medium to low linguistic complexity and accuracy effects were 
generally not adequately pronounced to draw a conclusive conclusion. Prediction 
2 was also not confirmed as all professional tasks elicited more accurate language 
than all narrative tasks.This study is exemplary in showcasing why it is critical to 
attend to task effects in modeling learner language in large-scale learner corpora 
and how such corpora can usefully complement the experimental paradigm in 
TBLT research. 
In a more focused study of the relationships among writing topics, measures 
of syntactic complexity, and judgments of writing quality, Yang et al. (2015) 
drew on Robinson’s Cognition Hypothesis to explicate the effect of topic on 
the syntactic complexity of ESL learners’ argumentative writing. They sampled 
380 argumentative essays written by 190 matriculated ESL graduate students on 
two different prompts from a larger corpus of essays collected by Weigle (2011) 
for validating automated scores of TOEFL iBT independent writing tasks. The 
first prompt asked students to discuss whether people place too much emphasis 
on personal appearance (the appearance topic), and the second prompt asked 
students to discuss whether careful planning while young ensures a good future 
(the future topic).The authors argued that, in terms of task complexity, the two 
prompts did not differ along most cognitive complexity dimensions (e.g., familiar 
vs. unfamiliar), but they did differ in the resource-directing dimension of causal 
reasoning. Specifically, they argued that the future topic would be more likely to 
elicit causal reasoning than the appearance topic, as it required students to justify 
why careful planning would or would not result in a good future. The essays 
were automatically analyzed for syntactic complexity using a slightly modified 
version of the L2SCA with eight measures: Mean length of sentence, T-units 
per sentence, mean length of T-unit, mean length of clause, dependent clauses 
per T-unit, coordinate phrases per clause, complex noun phrases per clause, and 
non-finite elements per clause. The essays on the appearance topic displayed a 
significantly higher amount of elaboration at the finite clause level, with longer 
clauses, more coordinate phrases per clause, and more complex noun phrases 
per clause.Those on the future topic, however, showed greater overall sentence 
complexity and a significantly higher amount of subordination, with longer 
sentences, more dependent clauses per T-unit, and more non-finite elements per 
clause.The authors argued that “since causal reasoning requires juxtaposing the 
relationship between two or more entities or events, it is reasonable that the 
future topic elicited more frequent use of multipropositional sentences containing 
subordination” (p. 63).They further argued that the appearance topic, which did

---

<!-- page 51 -->

 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
34 Variation in Second Language Use 
not demand causal reasoning, involved one entity and one proposition only and 
therefore elicited more descriptions rather than propositions. The essays on the 
two topics did not differ in overall T-unit complexity or clausal ordination, as 
measured by mean length of T-unit and T-units per sentence, respectively.These 
findings partially supported the prediction of the Cognition Hypothesis in some 
syntactic complexity subconstructs (i.e., overall sentence complexity and amount 
of subordination), but not others. They concluded that specific topics may 
naturally elicit more use of certain syntactic complexity features, a conclusion 
pointing to a possible functional interpretation of topic effects on the syntactic 
complexity of the ESL writers’ argumentative essays. 
Indeed, some researchers have systematically sought to explain task effects on 
learners’ linguistic performance from a functional perspective rather than purely 
from an information-processing perspective. Motivated by findings from prior 
register variation research that the distribution of grammatical complexity fea­
tures in different registers is conditioned by the extent to which the features are 
suited for the situational context and communicative purposes of those registers 
(e.g., Biber & Conrad, 2009), Biber et al. (2016) argued that “linguistic variation 
has a functional basis” and that acknowledging the functions and register distri­
butions of different grammatical features enables “specific predictions concern­
ing the expected patterns of variation” (p. 640). From the functional perspective, 
they examined variation in the use of a large set of grammatical complexity 
features among language samples produced for different task types.The learner 
corpus they used consisted of 2879 spoken texts and 960 written texts produced 
by L2 English learners taking the TOEFL iBT, which included four major task 
types: Spoken independent, spoken integrated, written independent, and written 
integrated.The independent tasks required test takers to give personal opinions 
on a given topic, while the integrated tasks required test takers to describe and/ 
or summarize the information in an external text. They predicted that writ­
ten texts would have higher grammatical complexity than spoken texts because 
of “the increased opportunities for planning”, and integrated texts would have 
higher grammatical complexity than independent texts as a result of “the com­
municative purposes of explaining information rather than reporting personal 
experiences” (p. 650). 
All texts in the corpus were automatically annotated for lexico-grammati­
cal features using the Biber Tagger (Biber et al., 1999), followed by automatic 
and manual tag correction for certain features known to be problematic. The 
authors then examined variation in the normalized frequency (per 1,000 words) 
of 23 features among texts in different modes (spoken vs. written), for different 
task types (independent vs. integrated), and between different scores (high vs. low 
or one point vs. four points on a four-point scale).A few features were found to be 
associated with speech and independent tasks, including adverbs, finite adverbial 
clauses, and desire verbs (e.g., want) controlling a to-clause.A larger set of features 
were found to be associated with writing and integrated tasks, including word

---

<!-- page 52 -->

 
 
 
  
 
  
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Variation in Second Language Use 35 
length, nouns, nominalizations, and various types of noun phrase modifiers (i.e., 
prepositional phrases, noun+of-phrase, attributive adjectives, premodifying nouns, 
noun+that-clause, noun+to-clause, and passive -ed relative clauses). Score level, 
however, was not found to be a significant predictor of variation for the gram­
matical complexity features examined. 
The authors further pursued a multi-dimensional (MD) analysis using a modi­
fied set of 28 features. Specifically, a factor analysis was conducted to identify 
dimensions (i.e., co-occurrence patterns) of the features, and these dimensions 
were then interpreted in terms of “underlying functional associations” (Biber 
et al.,2016, p.646). Four dimensions emerged from their analysis, namely,“Literate 
versus oral responses”, “Information source: Text versus personal experience”, 
“Abstract opinion versus concrete descriptions/summary”, and “Personal narra­
tion”, each with a set of features loading on it. For example, features with positive 
loadings on the dimension “Literate versus oral responses” included common 
nouns, concrete nouns, premodifying nouns, prepositional phrases, noun+ of-
phrase, attributive adjectives, topical adjectives, word length, finite passives, and 
postnominal passives.Those with negative loadings on this dimension included 
present tense verbs, mental verbs, modal verbs,third-person pronouns, that-clauses 
controlled by likelihood verbs, that-clauses with that-omission, and finite adver­
bial that-clauses.All four dimensions varied significantly among the TOEFL iBT 
task types, and the first dimension was also found to vary significantly by score 
level.The authors concluded that the variation observed among different TOEFL 
iBT task types corresponded to the patterns of linguistic variation documented 
for spoken and written registers in prior research and that analyses of the co-
occurrence patterns of linguistic features may be more robust than the analysis 
of individual features.The findings were also taken to show “that it is possible to 
predict linguistic differences among task types based on the situational character­
istics associated with spoken versus written, and independent versus integrated 
task types” (p. 641). 
In a study of ESL students’ linguistic development in two written genres,Yoon 
and Polio (2017) made it an explicit goal to explore whether the possible reasons 
for genre differences in complexity aligned more with the Cognition Hypothesis 
or Biber et al.’s (2016) functional account. Partially informed by the results from 
Lu (2011), who reported significantly higher values of 13 syntactic complexity 
measures from the L2SCA (all but clauses per sentence) in argumentative essays 
than in narrative essays produced by college-level Chinese EFL learners but did 
not attempt to explain the genre differences, the authors predicted increased com­
plexity in the argumentative essays compared to narrative essays in ESL writing. 
Their primary line of argument was that, if increased complexity in the argumen­
tative essays was found in both ESL writing and L1 English speakers’ writing, then 
“Biber’s explanation that genre differences were related to the resources needed 
for that genre was a likely explanation”; if, however, increased complexity in 
the argumentative essays was found in ESL writing but not L1 English speakers’

---

<!-- page 53 -->

 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
36 Variation in Second Language Use 
writing, then “it was possible that increased cognitive demands of different genres 
might account for any differences” (p. 282). 
Their data consisted of 111 narrative essays and 111 argumentative essays 
written by 37 adult ESL students studying at a US university at six different time 
points over a 15-week semester, as well as 46 narrative essays and 46 argumentative 
essays produced by 46 L1 English undergraduate students majoring in education. 
All essays were analyzed using a set of syntactic complexity, lexical complexity, 
accuracy, and fluency measures. Syntactic complexity was assessed using 12 of the 
14 measures (all but clauses per sentence and complex T-units per T-unit) in the 
L2SCA; lexical complexity was assessed using three measures from Coh-Metrix 
(Graesser et al., 2004), namely, average word length, word frequency index (i.e., 
the mean logarithmic frequency of all words in an essay, using frequency norms 
derived from Baayen et al.’s (1995) CELEX database), and vocd-D; accuracy was 
measured as the number of syntactic, morphological, preposition, and spelling 
errors per 100 words; and fluency was measured by the number of words in the 
essay produced within a 30-minute time limit. 
The authors found that in ESL writing, argumentative essays had significantly 
higher values in eight syntactic complexity indices than narrative essays, including 
three length measures (i.e., mean length of sentence,T-unit, and clause) and five 
phrase-level measures (i.e., coordinate phrases per clause and per T-unit, complex 
nominals per clause and per T-unit, and verb phrases per T-unit). However, there 
was no significant genre effect on the complexity measures of clausal coordina­
tion (i.e.,T-units per sentence) or subordination (i.e., dependent clauses per clause 
and per T-unit). In terms of lexical complexity, argumentative essays displayed 
significantly higher lexical sophistication (i.e., greater average word length and 
lower word frequency index) but significantly lower lexical diversity (i.e., lower 
vocd-D). No significant genre difference was found for accuracy or fluency. In 
L1 English writing, some differences between argumentative and narrative essays 
were similar to those found in ESL writing. Specifically, argumentative essays dis­
played significantly higher values in the three length measures (i.e., mean length 
of sentence,T-unit, and clause) and the two lexical sophistication measures (i.e., 
average word length and word frequency index) and significantly lower values in 
the lexical diversity measure (i.e., vocd-D). Similarly, no significant genre effect 
was found in clausal coordination, clausal subordination, or fluency.The effect of 
genre on the phrase-related measures differed somewhat from the effect found 
in ESL writing, with argumentative essays showing significantly higher values in 
complex nominals per clause only. However, the descriptive results for the other 
phrase-related measures were still higher in argumentative essays than in narrative 
essays, indicating a similar trend as that in ESL writing. Based on the considerable 
overlap in the results of genre effects on lexical and syntactic complexity across 
ESL writing and L1 English writing as well as the absence of accuracy differences 
between the two genres in ESL writing, the authors tentatively concluded that 
“functional difference better explains genre differences” (p. 292).

---

<!-- page 54 -->

 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Variation in Second Language Use 37 
Beyond task effects on the CAF of L2 production, some researchers have 
investigated the effect of task type on L2 learners’use of specific language features 
as well. Gablasova et al. (2015), for example, examined variation in L2 learners’ 
use of epistemic stance expressions in spoken L2 production across four speaking 
tasks.Their data consisted of speech samples produced by 132 advanced (C1/C2) 
L2 speakers from diverse L1 and cultural backgrounds selected from the TLC. 
Each speaker completed a largely monologic presentation task and three inter­
active tasks (i.e., conversation, discussion, and interaction). Epistemic stance was 
understood to be a type of evaluative discourse used to express opinion, main­
tain relations between the interlocutors, and organize discourse (e.g., Hunston & 
Thompson, 2000). In their analysis, the authors focused on three specific types 
of epistemic markers (Ems), namely, adverbial (e.g., certainly, predictably, sort of), 
adjectival (e.g., impossible, unlikely, I am confident), and verbal (e.g., appears, seems, I 
believe). Such expressions were identified from the speech samples in three steps. 
They first compiled a list of candidate EMs based on a review of existing lists 
(e.g., Biber et al., 1999) and a manual analysis of ten transcripts.They then gen­
erated a concordance for each item on the candidate list using MonoConc Pro 
(Barlow, 2003). Finally, they manually coded a sample of the concordance lines 
generated for each search item for epistemic or non-epistemic use of the form. 
The authors confirmed that the candidate forms were used predominantly to 
mark epistemic stance and subsequently included all instances of the candidate 
markers in their analysis. 
The normalized frequency of EMs (per 100K words) was found to be the 
highest in the interaction task (25.74), followed by the conversation (23.34) and 
discussion tasks (22.19), and lowest in the presentation task (8.40). A repeated-
measures analysis of variance (ANOVA) revealed a main effect of task type, and 
subsequent pairwise comparisons showed a significantly lower frequency in the 
presentation task than in all other three tasks as well as a significantly lower 
frequency in the discussion task than in the interaction task. The authors fur­
ther examined the differences in the degree of certainty expressed by L2 speak­
ers across the four tasks, using a subset of adverbial epistemic markers (AEMs) 
indicating certainty (i.e., certainly, definitely, for sure, no doubt, obviously, and surely) 
and uncertainty (i.e., maybe, perhaps, possibly, and probably).A comparative analysis 
showed that the presentation task elicited the highest proportion of AEMs sign­
aling certainty (12.03% of all AEMs used) but the lowest proportion of AEMs 
signaling uncertainty (39.18%), while the interaction task elicited the lowest pro­
portion of AEMs signaling certainty (2.86%) but the highest proportion of AEMs 
signaling uncertainty (61.98%).An analysis of all of the 31 specific EMs used by 
the L2 speakers across the four tasks showed that three of the 31 EMs accounted 
for over 65% of the EM tokens used (i.e., I @ think, maybe, and I @ know, where 
@ indicates that up to two intervening words are allowed within I think and I 
know). Overall, these results suggested a systematic variation in the number and 
type of EMs used by the L2 speakers across the four speaking tasks. In discussing

---

<!-- page 55 -->

 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
38 Variation in Second Language Use 
these results, the authors pointed out that the most prominent difference was 
found between the monologic presentation tasks and the three dialogic tasks, 
which they attributed to the varying degree of interactiveness in these tasks. 
Specifically, they argued that EMs are used more commonly in interactive speech 
to express subjectivity, co-manage the developing discourse, and manage the rela­
tionship between the interlocutors, while the intersubjective relationship needs 
not to be negotiated with the same level of intensity in prevalently monologic 
speech. In addition, the authors attributed the variation in the EMs used by L2 
speakers to express different degrees of (un)certainty among the four tasks to 
differences in the speakers’ roles and topic control, arguing that speakers with 
stronger topic control would express stronger certainty. For example, presenta­
tion tasks gave L2 speakers the greatest topic control and speech flow and also 
saw the highest proportion of AEMs used to express certainty, while in interac­
tion tasks L2 speakers had to negotiate the topic with the examiner and make 
evaluative comments on the examiner’s beliefs and opinions, necessitating the use 
of such strategies as hedging and downplaying, which entailed the expression of 
uncertainty. 
The authors argued that by using a large speech sample representing advanced 
speakers from diverse L1 and cultural backgrounds and including four different 
speaking tasks, their study “advances the agenda beyond studying writing in L2 
corpora, and beyond comparing speaking and writing into more fully exploring 
the variation in spoken interaction types” (Gablasova et al., 2015, p. 633).They 
further commented that fine-grained analyses performed on large corpora of dif­
ferent types of L2 spoken language would generally lead to a more nuanced and 
comprehensive understanding of L2 learners’ spoken pragmatic ability and com­
municative competence. 
Taken together, this line of research confirms that task design features such as 
mode (written vs. spoken), genre (e.g., narrative, expository, and argumentative), 
task type (e.g., integrated vs. independent, monologic vs. interactive), and topic 
(e.g., the appearance topic vs. the future topic) could significantly affect L2 
learners’ linguistic performance.A systematic understanding of the variation in L2 
use related to such task design features can usefully inform task-based language 
teaching, learning, and assessment.The small number of studies that incorporated 
learner corpus analysis in understanding task effects have not offered conclusive 
findings to fully support either the Limited Attentional Capacity Model or the 
Cognition Hypothesis.This is not surprising, as the findings from the large body 
of controlled experimental research have been similarly inconclusive. Meanwhile, 
such learner corpus analysis research has also pointed to the potential usefulness 
of accounts from functional and interactive demand perspectives of the effects of 
some task design features that likely require the use of certain language features 
to achieve specific communicative functions or interactive goals in particular 
situational contexts. This scenario should probably not be taken to mean that 
the information-processing, functional, or interactive demand accounts are in

---

<!-- page 56 -->

 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Variation in Second Language Use 39 
competition with one another. In fact, the different task effects that have been 
observed may well come from complicated ways in which the information-
processing demands, functional purposes, and/or interactive demands of different 
task design features interact with different dimensions of multidimensional 
linguistic performance constructs (e.g., syntactic complexity). Both experimental 
and corpus-based research of task effects could direct more attention to such 
interactions in the future. 
2.5 Summary 
In this chapter, we have discussed some of the common ways in which variation 
in L2 use has been measured in learner corpus research and reviewed a series of 
empirical studies of the effects of learner- and task-related variables on the varia­
tion in L2 use.The availability of tools for automating different types of linguistic 
analysis and large-scale learner corpora representing different types of learners and 
tasks has dramatically increased the analytical scope and scale of existing research 
on this topic.While the broad dimensions of complexity (including in particular 
lexical and syntactic complexity), accuracy, and fluency have fared prominently 
in this body of research, many studies have attended to various types of construc­
tions, phraseological units, and specific discoursal, pragmatic, and sociolinguistic 
features, often with newly developed measures and/or taxonomies. In terms of 
learner-related variables, L2 proficiency has been the most heavily studied, fol­
lowed by L1 background. Results from studies focusing on these two variables 
have shown that for both of them, the patterns of variation can be expected to 
differ for different dimensions of language use, justifying the value of the wide-
ranging analytical foci found in this line of research. Findings pertaining to the 
effect of L1 background also caution against purely proficiency-based accounts 
of variation in all dimensions of L2 use and offer useful insights into the learn­
ing challenges and opportunities for learners with different L1 backgrounds. Less 
attention has been paid to other learner-related variables, and future studies can 
take advantage of the increasing availability of large-scale learner corpora that 
reliably encode information about such variables (e.g., the TLC) to explore their 
effects on the variation in L2 use.This endeavor will substantially expand our cur­
rent understanding of variation in L2 use among learners with different charac­
teristics.Various types of task-related variables have been reported to significantly 
affect variation in L2 use, and studies have sought to account for such effects 
in terms of the cognitive, communicative, functional, and interactive demands 
imposed by the tasks.The findings, however, are far from conclusive, and future 
studies may further explore whether the interaction among these dimensions of 
task demands could better explain the task effects observed. All in all, the body 
of research on the effects of learner- and task-related variables on variation in 
various dimensions of L2 use have offered rich and nuanced insights with useful 
implications for verifying the predictions of SLA theories and for informing L2

---

<!-- page 57 -->

 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
40 Variation in Second Language Use 
pedagogy and assessment (e.g., in terms of what dimensions to focus on for learn­
ers with different characteristics performing different tasks).

Canada:Association for Computational Linguistics.

<!-- page 63 -->

  
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
3 
FACTORS INFLUENCING SECOND 
LANGUAGE PROCESSING 
AND PRODUCTION 
3.1 Introduction 
This chapter synthesizes empirical research that has incorporated corpus analysis 
to examine factors influencing second language (L2) processing and production. 
The chapter starts with an overview of usage-based approaches to second language 
acquisition (SLA), particularly the view that language acquisition involves 
associative learning that reflects the probabilities of occurrence of constructions 
in usage (e.g., Ellis et al., 2014; Wulff & Ellis, 2018). It then reviews results of 
empirical studies that either combined corpus linguistic and experimental methods 
to examine factors affecting L2 learners’ processing of specific constructions or 
employed corpus analysis on its own to investigate factors affecting L2 learners’ 
production of certain constructions. 
3.2 Usage-Based Approaches to Second 
Language Acquisition 
Usage-based approaches to SLA posit that language learners rely on the linguistic 
input they receive as their primary source for L2 learning and that they employ 
general cognitive learning mechanisms rather than cognitive mechanisms specific 
to language learning in learning the L2 (e.g.,Wulff & Ellis, 2018).Within such 
approaches, the targets of language learning are constructions, i.e., convention­
alized form-meaning/function pairings that are entrenched in speakers’ minds, 
ranging from morphemes (the smallest form-meaning pairings in language) to 
words, phraseological units, and larger syntactic frames (Goldberg, 1995, 2006). 
For example, morphemes such as un-, which roughly means “not” (e.g., unhappy), 
are considered constructions, as are verb-argument constructions (VACs) such as 
DOI: 10.4324/9781003054948-3

---

<!-- page 64 -->

 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Factors Influencing Second Language Processing and Production 47 
“V across n”, which denotes a directed motion event (e.g., The cat ran across the 
street). Constructions vary in terms of their degree of complexity. For instance, 
morphemes are simple constructions that can form parts of more complex con­
structions such as derived or inflected words, which in turn can be parts of even 
more complex constructions such as phraseological units.They can also vary in 
terms of abstractness or schematicity, ranging from fully lexical constructions (e.g., 
the idiom kicked the bucket meaning “passed away”) to partially schematized con­
structions (e.g., the “V across n”VAC discussed above, which gives rise to lexical­
ized VACs such as run across the street), and fully schematized constructions (e.g., 
Adverb+Verb Phrase, which could be lexically specified in many different ways, 
such as happily returned home and suddenly turned right).A mature speaker’s language 
knowledge consists of knowledge of the constructions in the language as well as 
their semantic, functional, or contextual compatibility with other constructions 
(Goldenberg, 2006).The compatibility or combinability of two constructions and 
the degree to which constructional co-occurrence is entrenched in the speakers’ 
minds are both driven primarily by the frequency of co-occurrence of the two 
constructions. 
The process of learning constructions is exemplar-based and involves “learning 
the associations within and between constructions” (Wulff & Ellis, 2018, p. 39). 
This process is seen as a rational one in the sense that the mental model built by 
learners at any point in the language development process reflects the learned 
probabilities of various form-meaning associations the learners have accumulated 
from their summed language learning experience (Ellis, 2006). It is also emergent as 
the network of the learners’ constructional knowledge resulting from the learning 
process over time is highly adaptive, dynamic, and complex, despite the simplicity 
of the learning mechanisms they employ for language learning (Ellis & Larsen-
Freeman, 2009). In psycholinguistic research, this process is analyzed as one of 
associative learning of cue-outcome contingencies conditioned by construction-
related factors such as frequency, contingency, salience, redundancy, and semantic 
prototypicality and learner cognitive factors such as learned attention and transfer 
(Ellis, 2008). These factors work in tandem to affect constructional acquisition, 
processing, and use (Ellis et al., 2014). The frequency of usage of constructions 
as well as the frequency distribution of exemplars within constructions are seen 
as a driving force of construction learning (Ellis & Ferreira-Junior, 2009). The 
frequency distribution is generally Zipfian (Zipf, 1935), with the most frequently 
occurring types accounting for the majority of tokens. Such a Zipfian distribution 
aids the language learning process with the increased probability of form-function 
mappings for more frequent tokens, which could then serve as exemplars for 
acquiring less frequent ones (Boyd & Goldberg, 2009; Ellis, 2006; MacWhinney, 
1997). However, some constructions, particularly grammatical morphemes such 
as the past tense marker and closed-class constructions such as prepositions, 
appear to be less learnable than open-class ones, especially for beginning level 
learners, despite their high frequency of usage (e.g., Bardovi-Harlig, 1992).These

---

<!-- page 65 -->

 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
48 Factors Influencing Second Language Processing and Production 
differences in the learnability of constructions can be accounted for by analyzing 
the effects of other construction- and learner-related factors. The contingency 
of the relationship between cues and outcomes has been found to affect how 
the associations between them are learned, and in the case of construction 
learning, this means that the extent to which the form of a construction reliably 
predicts its interpretation directly affects its acquisition and processing (Gries & 
Ellis, 2015). In general, constructions with lower cue-interpretation contingency 
are harder to learn than those with higher cue-interpretation contingency.This 
helps explain why grammatical morphemes and closed constructions are hard 
to learn, as they often have multiple interpretations.Additionally, salience, which 
refers to the extent to which a stimulus stands out from other stimuli, can also 
affect learnability, with more salient cues generally learned more readily than 
less salient ones (e.g., Ellis, 2006). Grammatical morphemes and closed-class 
constructions such as prepositions tend to be hard to be noticed by L2 learners 
as they are usually short and unstressed. An example of learner-related factors 
that may help account for the lower learnability of these constructions is learned 
attention, which refers to the attentional bias resulting from the learners’ prior 
language knowledge (e.g., Ellis, 2006). Specifically, when learners have learned the 
association between a cue and an interpretation in their prior language learning 
experience, it may become hard for them to associate a new cue with the same 
interpretation. For example, Chinese speakers generally associate plurality with 
numerals and quantifiers, making it difficult for them to learn the plurality marker 
in English (e.g., Jiang, 2007). 
While the effects of different construction- and learner-related factors on L2 
learners’ acquisition and processing of constructions have usually been explored 
through psycholinguistic experimental investigations, many empirical studies 
have incorporated corpus analysis in several important ways. In particular, usage 
information such as frequency and contingency is now commonly obtained or 
calculated from large-scale first language (L1) corpora, and such information 
is then subsequently used to examine the effects of the usage statistics on L2 
learners’ processing and production of constructions.The patterns of L2 learners’ 
development of constructions are also increasingly analyzed using longitudinal 
or quasi-longitudinal corpora of L2 production.The rest of this chapter reviews 
empirical studies that have integrated corpus analysis to examine the extent to 
which L2 learners may be sensitive to various construction-related factors in 
processing and producing different constructions in the L2.The discussion of the 
effects of such factors on L2 learners’ development of constructions is reserved 
for Chapter 4. 
3.3 Frequency and Contingency 
Frequency and contingency constitute two of the most important and heavily 
examined factors in usage-based studies of L2 processing and production. In a

---

<!-- page 66 -->

 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Factors Influencing Second Language Processing and Production 49 
position paper on the role of frequency in language acquisition and processing, 
Ellis (2002a) argued that “language processing is intimately tuned to input 
frequency” (p. 143), with examples of frequency effects in the processing of such 
wide-ranging domains as phonology, lexis, morphosyntax, formulaic language, 
and syntax, among others, documented in a large body of experimental studies. 
Ellis (2002a) pointed out that the role of frequency had “largely been ignored 
in theoretical and applied linguistics for the last 40 years or so” preceding his 
review and called for “its reinstatement as an all-pervasive causal factor” (p. 179) 
in language acquisition and processing research. This position paper elicited 
commentaries from multiple SLA researchers from diverse theoretical perspectives, 
with some researchers advocating alternative approaches (e.g., meaning-based) to 
explaining observable frequency effects (Bley-Vroman, 2002), pushing for more 
rigorous ways to interpret such effects (e.g., by accounting for learner agency) 
(Larsen-Freeman, 2002), or outlining areas of language acquisition and processing 
that are not or only partially subject to frequency effects (Gass & Mackey, 2002), 
among others. In his response to these commentaries, Ellis (2002b) re-emphasized 
his position that in claiming that language processing is based on frequency and 
statistical knowledge, he views language learning as implicit learning, without 
denying the importance of noticing or explicit instruction or the role of other 
construction- or learner-related factors. 
Ellis (2006) further discussed how “language acquisition is contingency 
learning, that is the gathering of information about the relative frequencies of 
form-function mappings” (p. 1), in line with the view that language learning is a 
rational process in which learners optimally prepare their unconscious language 
representation systems for comprehension and production. Seeing language 
processing as probabilistic, Ellis (2006) noted the following: 
Not only do we know the constructions that are most likely to be of overall 
relevance (i.e. first-order probabilities of occurrence), but we also predict 
the ones that are going to pertain in any particular context (sequential 
dependencies), and the particular interpretations of cues that are most likely 
to be correct (contingency statistics).These predictions are usually rational 
and normative in that they accurately represent the statistical covariation 
between events. In these ways, language learners are intuitive statisticians; 
they acquire knowledge of the contingency relationships of one-way 
dependencies and they combine information from multiple cues. 
(p. 8) 
Many psycholinguistic studies have identified the effects of frequency and/or 
contingency in language processing. For example, studies of sentence processing 
have shown that less frequent constructions (e.g., passive and cleft-object 
constructions) tend to be more difficult to comprehend than more frequent 
ones (e.g., active and cleft-subject constructions) (e.g., Miyake et al., 1994) and

---

<!-- page 67 -->

 
 
 
 
 
  
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
50 Factors Influencing Second Language Processing and Production 
that adult speakers exploit probabilistic constraints from their prior experience 
to resolve syntactic ambiguities (e.g., Spivey-Knowlton & Sedivy, 1995). It is 
worth mentioning that contingency has been measured in different ways (e.g., 
Gries & Ellis, 2015). Various association measures that quantify the mutual 
preference or dispreference for co-occurrence of two linguistic elements, such 
as mutual information (MI), have been used (e.g., Pecina, 2009). A family of 
methods known as collostructional (i.e., collocation+constructional) analysis has 
been proposed to quantify the likelihood for certain words to occur in certain 
grammatical patterns or constructions (e.g., Stefanowitsch & Gries, 2003). Gries 
and Ellis (2015) pointed out two issues with the use of association measures to 
quantify contingency.The first issue is that most association measures only include 
token frequencies but not type frequencies or the type-token distributions in 
their computation, and the second issue is that almost all association measures are 
bidirectional in the sense that they assess the mutual association of two elements 
even though associations may be unidirectional. Gries and Ellis (2015) argued that 
the delta P measure (discussed in detail in Section 3.3.3) can successfully identify 
the directional association of two elements. 
In this section, we review empirical studies that integrated corpus analysis 
to investigate the effects of frequency and contingency in L2 processing or 
production. In addition to employing corpus analysis to obtain frequency and/or 
contingency information for the stimuli used in psycholinguistic experiments of 
language processing, some studies have analyzed L2 learners’ production of target 
constructions in large-scale learner corpora either to complement the findings of 
experimental studies or as a primary way to examine the effects of frequency and/ 
or contingency on L2 production. 
3.3.1 L2 Production of Lexical Categories 
and the Article Construction 
Let us start our review by taking a look at two studies that examined the effects of 
frequency and/or contingency on L2 learners’production of smaller constructions, 
in particular lexical categories and the English article construction.The first study, 
conducted by Crossley et al. (2013), investigated the extent to which frequency, 
contextual distinctiveness, and contextual diversity could predict which frequent 
nouns and verbs were produced by early L2 English learners.The authors were 
particularly interested in finding out whether word frequency would be the 
strongest predictor of early L2 lexical production independent of contextual 
distinctiveness and diversity and whether there would be differences in the lexical 
properties of nouns and verbs that could help explain such production as well. 
The authors used a list of frequent words produced by beginning L2 English 
learners and a second list of words frequent in L1 English but not produced 
by L2 English learners (Crossley & Salsbury, 2010), with the assumption that 
words on the first list should be easier to acquire.These lists were derived from

---

<!-- page 68 -->

 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Factors Influencing Second Language Processing and Production 51 
part of a longitudinal corpus of oral language samples produced by English as 
a second language (ESL) learners enrolled in an intensive English program at a 
university in the United States, using the 51-million-word SUBTLEXus corpus 
of subtitles of American movies and television shows (Brysbaert & New, 2009) as 
a native reference corpus.The learner data were collected biweekly over a year, 
but only a subset of data produced in the first months by six learners was used 
to derive the L2-produced list. Only nouns and verbs among the top 3,000 most 
frequent words in the SUBTLEXus corpus were considered, and a word must 
have been accurately used by over half of the L2 participants to be eligible for the 
L2-produced list. 
The authors used two different word-frequency indices, namely, word frequency 
in the SUBTLEXus corpus and word frequency in the British National Corpus 
(BNC; Burnard, 2007), and a word familiarity index based on word familiarity 
ratings from the Medical Research Council (MRC) database (Wilson, 1988). For 
contextual diversity, they used an index from the SUBTLEXus corpus, calculated 
as the number of films and television shows the words occurred in, and three 
indices from the BNC, calculated as the percentage of texts where a word occurred 
in the entire BNC, the written subsection, and the spoken subsection, respectively. 
Finally, for contextual distinctiveness, they used two different indices.The first one 
was based on the University of South Florida word association norms (Nelson et 
al., 1998), calculated as the mean number of associated words for each word.The 
second one was based on latent semantic analysis (LSA; Landauer et al., 1998), a 
sophisticated procedure that can be used to measure the semantic associations or 
similarity between words by computing the distance between vectors representing 
their frequencies and contexts of occurrence in a corpus.The specific LSA index 
was calculated as the number of associated words for each word in the word lists 
that occurred 20 or more times in the Touchstone Applied Science Association 
Corpus and that had an LSA value of .26 or higher. 
The authors first used a multivariate analysis of variance (MANOVA) to 
determine which of the indices discussed above differed significantly between 
the produced and unproduced words. They then ran a stepwise discriminant 
function analysis (DFA) using the indices selected separately for nouns and verbs, 
which generated a discriminant function for predicting group membership (i.e., 
whether the words were produced or not). The findings showed that word 
frequency and contextual diversity were both strong classifiers of whether a noun 
or verb was produced by beginning-level L2 learners. However, word frequency 
was the strongest classifier of whether a noun was produced or not, while 
contextual diversity was the strongest classifier of whether a verb was produced 
or not, with word familiarity contributing additional classification power in 
both cases. Specifically, the DFA with the word frequency SUBTLEXus index 
and the word familiarity index accurately classified 77 out of 90 nouns (i.e., 
86.0%) in the total set, and the DFA with the contextual diversity SUBTLEXus 
index and the word familiarity index accurately classified 70 out of the 89

---

<!-- page 69 -->

 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
52 Factors Influencing Second Language Processing and Production 
verbs (i.e., 78.6%) in the total set. A post hoc analysis comparing nouns and 
verbs showed that “nouns were more specific, concrete, imageable, meaningful, 
and unambiguous” (p. 744), and the authors interpreted these differences as 
potential evidence that such lexical properties could explain differences in 
noun and verb production.The authors concluded that while the distributional 
properties of nouns allow nouns to be acquired through frequent exposure, the 
abstractness and ambiguity of verbs make them harder to be acquired solely 
through repetition, and it is important for learners to also be exposed to verbs 
in diverse contexts. 
The second study, conducted by Zhao and Fan (2021), investigated how the 
input factors of cue availability and cue reliability influenced Chinese English as 
a foreign language (EFL) learners’ production of the English article construction. 
This study was based on the Competition Model (MacWhinney, 1997, 2008), 
which views language as a system of form-function mappings and forms as cues 
to activate meaning or function. Empirical research on this model has employed 
language processing data to show the important role of cue availability and 
reliability, which are related to the frequency of cue usage and the contingency 
of cue-function association, respectively, in both L1 and L2 acquisition and 
processing (MacWhinney, 2001). The authors noted a dearth of L2 studies that 
apply the Competition Model on language production and argued for the need to 
test the model’s core predictions regarding the role of input properties in language 
learning using production data. 
The data used in the study were drawn from the Written English Corpus 
of Chinese Learners (WECCL) Version 2.0 (Wen et al., 2008), comprised of 
argumentative, expository, and narrative essays written by college-level EFL 
learners enrolled in 34 universities in China. Specifically, the authors randomly 
sampled 20 timed argumentative essays with 150 or more words from each of 
the following four learner groups: First-year English majors, second-year English 
majors, first-year non-English majors, and second-year non-English majors.Two 
coders manually identified a total of 2,210 obligatory noun phrase contexts of 
article use in the 80-essay sample and coded each context for article cue type 
and accuracy of usage, with certain ambiguous forms excluded. For article cue 
type coding, the authors used an elaborate scheme developed by Zhao and 
MacWhinney (2018) consisting of 86 types. For example, the type “plural|ø” 
represents contexts where the zero article should be used with plural nouns 
except when they are uniquely identifiable. 
The availability and reliability of the 86 article cues computed from a 
mini-corpus of 38 texts representing ten common written English genres and 
diverse topics by Zhao and MacWhinney (2018) were adopted. In that study, 
the availability of each cue was calculated as its token frequency divided by the 
number of noun phrases in the corpus, and the reliability of a cue was calculated 
as the number of correct applications of the cue divided by its token frequency. 
The authors performed a path model analysis in the structural equation model

---

<!-- page 70 -->

 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Factors Influencing Second Language Processing and Production 53 
(SEM), with the hypothesis that cue availability and cue reliability would affect L2 
cue frequency and L2 cue accuracy. 
The results showed that cue availability significantly and positively predicted 
cue frequency in L2 production for both English majors and non-English majors, 
while cue reliability significantly and positively predicted cue accuracy in L2 
production for English majors but not for non-English majors. The authors 
interpreted these results as strong empirical evidence in support of the central 
claims of the Competition Model, namely, the input frequency of a cue is a 
significant determinant of its frequency in L2 production, while cue reliability 
is a significant determinant of successful L2 learning for more advanced learners. 
Methodologically, both studies used L1 corpora to derive information about 
the contingency and/or frequency of the target constructions and learner corpora 
to obtain features of L2 production of such constructions.The findings of these 
studies indicate that frequency and contingency significantly affect L2 production 
of smaller English constructions, specifically lexical categories and the English 
article construction, but their effects may vary among different subcategories of 
such constructions or learners at different proficiency levels. 
3.3.2 L2 Processing of Phraseological Units 
In this section, we will move on to studies that integrated corpus analysis in 
examining the effects of frequency and contingency in L2 learners’ processing of 
phraseological units or multiword sequences.This body of research has employed 
a range of processing tasks (e.g., free association tasks, self-paced reading tasks, 
and acceptability judgment tasks), has focused on different types of phraseological 
units (e.g., formulas and collocations) in different languages (e.g., English and 
Chinese), and has often compared L2 processing to L1 processing. 
Ellis et al. (2008) reported an exemplary study that showcased how cor­
pus linguistic, educational, and psycholinguistic perspectives can be brought 
together to shed light on the determinants of L1 and L2 speakers’ sensitivity to 
formulaicity in language processing.They used corpus linguistic techniques to 
identify three-, four-, and five-word formulas occurring ten or more times per 
million words in each of the following four groups of corpora: One represent­
ing academic speech consisting of the Michigan Corpus of Academic Spoken 
English (MICASE) and selected academic spoken language samples from 
the BNC; one representing academic writing consisting of Hyland’s (2004) 
research article corpus and selected academic writing samples from the BNC; 
the Switchboard corpus representing nonacademic speech; and one represent­
