ing nonacademic writing consisting of the Freiburg Lancaster Oslo/Bergen 
(FLOB) and Frown corpora.The authors used the Collocate program (Barlow, 
2004) to obtain the frequency and mutual information (MI) score for each of 
the roughly 14,000 n-grams extracted from the four sets of corpora and then 
used the log-likelihood statistic to identify formulas that occurred significantly

---

<!-- page 71 -->

 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
  
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
54 Factors Influencing Second Language Processing and Production 
more often in academic speech than in nonacademic speech as well as those that 
occurred significantly more often in academic writing than in nonacademic 
writing. Using stratified random sampling, the authors selected 54 formulas 
from the speech list and 54 from the writing list for rating by a group of expe­
rienced English for Academic Purposes (EAP) instructors and language testers. 
These formulas represented different n-gram lengths (3, 4, 5), frequency band 
(high or 43.6 or more per million, medium or 15.0–43.6 per million, and low or 
10.9–15.0 per million), and MI band (high or ≥11, medium or 6.7–11, and low 
or 3.3–6.7).The raters rated each formula in terms of its degree of formulaicity 
(six raters, interrater alpha = 0.77), cohesiveness in meaning or function (eight 
raters, interrater alpha = 0.67), and pedagogical worthiness (six raters, interrater 
alpha = 0.83) on a five-point scale. Correlation and multiple regression analyses 
showed that frequency and MI both significantly predicted these ratings, with 
MI being the major determinant. 
The authors then conducted three experiments to determine the aspects of 
formulaicity that affected the accuracy and fluency of L1 English speakers’ and 
advanced L2 English learners’ processing of formulas.The first experiment asked 
participants to judge whether a string of words shown on a screen was likely to be 
encountered in English or not. Participants included 11 L1 English students or staff 
and 11 ESL students taking EAP classes at the University of Michigan.The 108 
formulas selected above and another 108 non-phrases created by randomizing the 
word order of those formulas were presented to the participants one by one.The 
response times for correct yes responses on the 108 real formulas were analyzed to 
identify word length, frequency, and MI effects.The L1 English speakers and ESL 
learners were found to be sensitive to different metrics:While n-gram length and 
MI significantly predicted response time for L1 English speakers, n-gram length 
and frequency significantly predicted response time for ESL learners.The second 
experiment asked participants (six L1 English speakers and six ESL learners) to 
read aloud the string of words presented on a screen as quickly as they could.The 
participants’ voice onset time (VOT, i.e., the pause between a beep indicating 
the beginning of string presentation and the onset of the participant’s spoken 
response) and articulation time (i.e., the duration of the participant’s spoken 
response) were analyzed to determine the effects of n-gram length, number of 
phonemes, frequency, and MI. In terms of VOT, significant effects were found for 
n-gram length, number of phonemes, and MI for L1 English speakers, and for 
number of phonemes and frequency for ESL learners. In terms of articulation 
time, L1 English speakers were found to be sensitive only to n-gram length, while 
ESL learners were found to be sensitive only to number of phonemes.The third 
experiment was designed to measure the extent to which seeing the beginning 
part of a formula would prime the speakers’ access to the articulation of the 
formula’s last word. Participants (18 L1 English speakers and 11 ESL learners) 
were first shown the beginning part of a formula or a string of x’s; they were 
then shown the last word of a formula and asked to read it aloud as quickly

---

<!-- page 72 -->

 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
Factors Influencing Second Language Processing and Production 55 
as possible. Number of phonemes and MI significantly predicted VOT for L1 
English speakers, but no significant predictor of VOT was found for ESL speakers. 
Overall, these findings showed that the corpus-driven metrics of length, 
frequency, and MI predicted not only human raters’ judgments of n-gram 
formulaicity, identifiability of function, and pedagogical worthiness but also 
L1 and L2 English speakers’ processing of formulas, suggesting that “formulaic 
sequences, statistically defined and extracted from large corpora of usage, have 
clear educational and psycholinguistic validity” (Ellis et al., 2008, pp. 388–389). 
While the MI of the formula was found to be the predominant determinant 
of its processibility for L1 English speakers, it was the frequency of the formula 
that predominantly determined its processibility for ESL learners. The authors 
teased out two components of frequency effects: The frequency of exposure 
and the frequency of practice, and argued that both components should exhibit 
more discernible effects in the initial stages of construction acquisition in which 
learners are consolidating representations of the form-meaning mappings of 
constructions and tune them to make them appropriately accessible. To obtain 
native-like accuracy and efficiency, however, learners also need to tune “the 
system for coherence–for co-occurrence greater than chance” (Ellis et al., 2008, p. 
391), and that is where MI plays a more important role.The findings of this study 
also support the use of corpus linguistic analyses of academic registers and texts 
to identify academic formulas and prioritize which of them to teach. In sum, the 
study not only offers evidence for frequency effects in L2 learners’ processing of 
formulas and the need for them to become sensitive to contingency (measured 
using MI), but also exemplifies how the construct of formula can be successfully 
triangulated from corpus linguistic, educational, and psycholinguistic perspectives. 
While Ellis et al. (2008) focused on L2 processing of formulas determined 
using a combination of corpus linguistic criteria and expert judgment with 
no restriction on the lexical categories of or syntactic relations between the 
component words, several other studies zoomed in on L2 processing of highly 
specific types of phraseological units. Kim and Kim (2012) examined the effect 
of frequency on L1 English speakers’ and ESL learners’ processing of a specific 
type of two-word sequences, i.e., verb+out sequences.Their participants included 
11 L1 English speakers and 14 L1 Korean, Japanese, and Chinese ESL learners 
who were graduate students at a university in the United States. The authors 
extracted 32 two-word verb+out sequences from the Corpus of Contemporary 
American English (COCA; Davies, 2009), with eight sequences in each of the 
following four frequency bands: High (1200–12000 per million words, e.g., find 
out), mid-high (500–1000 per million words, e.g., sort out), mid-low (200–400 per 
million words, e.g., bail out), and low (20–100 per million words, e.g., yell out). 
The 32 sequences were embedded in 32 different critical sentences of the same 
length, which were randomly presented to the participants in a self-paced reading 
task along with 38 distractor sentences one at a time in random order. In each 
trial, the participants first saw dashes on the monitor; upon pressing the F key on

---

<!-- page 73 -->

 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
56 Factors Influencing Second Language Processing and Production 
the keyboard, they saw the first few dashes replaced with the first phrase of the 
sentence; they then pressed the F key to proceed to the next phrase, until they 
reached the last phrase of the sentence, at which point they answered a yes-no 
comprehension question. Throughout the trials, the target verb+out sequence 
always appeared as one segment. Respondents whose reading times were more 
than three standard deviations away from the mean for each group were removed, 
as were participants who answered over 20% of the comprehension questions 
incorrectly. 
The authors hypothesized that the ESL learners would show shorter reading 
times for higher-frequency multiword units if frequency is indeed a factor that 
affects the extent to which L2 learners store and retrieve multiword units as 
chunks. A repeated-measures ANOVA yielded significant main effects for both 
groups (L1 vs. ESL) and frequency, with no interaction effect between the two 
factors. For L1 English speakers, reading times for sequences in the low frequency 
band were significantly longer than those in all other three frequency bands. For 
the ESL group, reading times for sequences in the low frequency band were 
significantly longer than those in the high frequency band only. The authors 
interpreted these findings as evidence that collocational frequency affects the 
extent to which multiword units are stored as chunks for both L1 and L2 English 
speakers and attributed L2 learners’ non-sensitivity to mid frequency multiword 
units to their lack of adequate exposure. 
Yi et al. (2017) examined L1 Chinese speakers’ and Chinese as a second 
language (CSL) learners’ sensitivity to phrasal frequency and contingency in 
their online processing of a specific type of Chinese multiword sequences, i.e., 
adverbial sequences comprised of two highly frequent monosyllabic adverbs, using 
an eye-tracking study. Their participants included 20 L1 Chinese speakers and 
20 advanced CSL learners (comparable to CEFR level C1) from eight different 
L1 backgrounds enrolled in universities in Beijing, China. The stimuli were 
created as follows.The authors first selected the 33 most frequent monosyllabic 
Chinese adverbs from the Classified word frequency list of Modern Chinese (National 
Committee of Language and Script, 2015) and collected familiarity ratings of 
those adverbs from five advanced CSL learners on a four-point scale, with 1 
indicating no familiarity and 4 indicating full familiarity. The 24 adverbs that 
received an average rating of two or higher were retained, and a list of possible 
two-word adverbial sequences was constructed for each of those adverbs. The 
sequences were then checked against the CCL Modern Chinese Corpus (Center 
for Chinese Linguistics, 2015), which contained 581 million Chinese characters 
and about 300 million words. MI values for the 119 sequences with one or more 
occurrences per million words in the corpus were computed from the CCL 
corpus, and 80 sequences were chosen using a stratified sampling method to 
represent sequences with high and low frequency (using seven per million as the 
frequency cutoff point) and high and low MI (with 3.0 as the MI cutoff point). 
These sequences were divided into four groups (high frequency and high MI;

---

<!-- page 74 -->

 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Factors Influencing Second Language Processing and Production 57 
high frequency and low MI; low frequency and high MI; and low frequency and 
low MI), and each sequence was embedded in four different sentence frames. 
Several measures (e.g., position of the sequence in the sentence, word frequency, 
sentence length, and sentence neutrality) were taken to ensure the comparability, 
readability, and comprehensibility of the sentences constructed, the details of 
which are skipped here for the sake of brevity. Altogether, a total of 320 target 
sentences were constructed, along with 80 filler sentences. 
Each participant read six practice sentences, 80 target sentences, and 80 
filler sentences one by one on a computer monitor and pressed a button to 
answer a comprehension question upon finishing reading a sentence. Statistical 
analyses were conducted using mixed-effects models. Phrasal frequency, phrasal 
contingency (i.e., MI), and speaker (L1 vs. CSL) were the independent variables of 
interest in the analysis; the frequency of each of the two constituent adverbs in the 
adverbial sequence and the number of strokes of the characters representing the 
two constituent adverbs (a measure of visual complexity) were treated as covariates. 
Different models were constructed to analyze the first fixation duration, first-pass 
reading time, total reading time, fixation count, and fixation probability data. 
The analyses revealed a number of substantial findings regarding the effects 
of the independent variables and covariates and their interactions on the eye-
movement measures. After controlling for the effects of phrasal contingency 
and covariates, the authors found significant phrasal frequency effects in all 
eye-movement measures in both L1 Chinese speakers and CSL learners, with 
the exception of fixation probability. Higher-frequency sequences were read 
significantly faster by both L1 and L2 Chinese speakers in terms of both first-
fixation durations and total reading time. During the first-pass reading, L1 
Chinese speakers read lower-frequency sequences faster than higher-frequency 
ones, while the opposite was true for CSL learners. In terms of fixation counts, 
higher-frequency sequences resulted in fewer fixation counts for both L1 and L2 
Chinese speakers. In addition, analyses of the first-pass reading data and fixation 
count data also showed that CSL learners were more sensitive to phrasal frequency 
in processing the sequences than L1 Chinese speakers.The authors argued that 
these results confirm that frequency effects are not limited to the processing of 
individual words but apply to the processing of multiword sequences as well 
and that phrasal frequency effects are not limited to highly frequent multiword 
sequences but exist in a continuum, as the stimuli covered a wide frequency 
range. After controlling for the effects of phrasal frequency and the covariates, 
the authors found significant phrasal contingency effects in fixation counts for 
both L1 Chinese speakers and CSL learners and in first-pass reading for CSL 
learners. Specifically, higher-MI sequences resulted in more fixation counts for 
both L1 Chinese speakers and CSL learners and shorter first-pass reading time 
for CSL learners.These findings from an eye-tracking paradigm usefully support 
the findings regarding the role of phrasal contingency in multiword sequence 
processing from limited prior research that relied on experimental tasks with

---

<!-- page 75 -->

 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
58 Factors Influencing Second Language Processing and Production 
limited automaticity (e.g., Ellis et al., 2008).The authors noted that, while both 
L1 Chinese speakers and CSL learners were sensitive to phrasal frequency and 
contingency in processing Chinese multiword sequences, the results on first-pass 
reading time and fixation counts showed that CSL learners were more sensitive 
to such usage properties. In addition to the independent effects of phrasal 
frequency and contingency, the authors also found an interaction between the 
two in first-pass reading time among CSL learners. Specifically, CSL learners read 
higher-frequency sequences faster than lower-frequency ones, but this frequency 
effect was attenuated with the increase in contingency.The authors argued that 
while phrasal frequency and contingency both facilitate reading time, frequency 
information is activated early on in the reading process, while contingency likely 
functions more in later stages of the process as speakers reanalyze and/or integrate 
information to recover from processing difficulties, as suggested by the greater 
fixation counts (a measure reflecting late processes) for higher-MI sequences (e.g., 
Paterson et al., 1999). Overall, the authors interpreted the findings as evidence 
that both L1 and L2 processing are usage-based and that L1 and L2 speakers likely 
share the same statistical learning mechanisms that include both frequency and 
contingency when processing multiword sequences. 
To determine whether the effects of phrasal frequency and contingency can 
be generalized to other language processing tasks and other types of multiword 
sequences,Yi (2018) examined the extent to which L1 and advanced L2 English 
speakers are sensitive to phrasal frequency and contingency when processing 
English adjective-noun collocations along with the moderating effects of cognitive 
aptitudes on such sensitivity. The participants included a group of L1 English 
speakers and a group of Chinese ESL learners enrolled in a university in the 
United States. Each participant completed a phrasal acceptability task designed 
to assess their online processing of English collocations. A total of 180 English 
adjective-noun collocations and 180 filler items (implausible word combinations, 
e.g., religious morning) were presented one by one and the participants were asked 
to judge whether each item is acceptable in English as fast as they could. The 
reaction time and accuracy were recorded for each participant. 
The 180 target collocations were extracted from the BNC through the Phrases 
in English (PIE) database (Fletcher, 2011) in several steps. First, 8,340 adjective-
noun sequences were extracted from the PIE database, the raw frequencies of 
those sequences and their component words in the BNC were normalized 
(per million words) and transformed using natural log, and the MI values of the 
sequences were calculated. Second, a sequence was considered a collocation if it 
occurred one or more times per million words in the BNC with an MI score 
higher than 3.0. Finally, 180 target collocations were selected using a stratified 
sampling procedure to represent different levels of log frequency (high: 2.0–4.0; 
medium: 1.1–1.6; low: 0.1–0.7) and MI (high: 8.0–13.0; medium: 6.8–8.0; low: 
3.0–6.0) (Siyanova-Chanturia & Spina, 2015), with 20 collocations representing 
each of the nine levels of collocation frequency and MI combination (e.g., high

---

<!-- page 76 -->

 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Factors Influencing Second Language Processing and Production 59 
frequency – high MI, high frequency – low MI, etc.). Five advanced Chinese 
ESL learners rated these collocations for familiarity on a five-point scale (with 
5 indicating full familiarity), and an average rating of 4.5 was reported.The filler 
items were constructed by randomly matching a list of adjectives to a list of nouns 
and the author verified that none of them occurred in the BNC.The author also 
measured the participants’ cognitive aptitudes using six aptitude tests, the details 
of which are skipped here. 
Linear mixed-effects modeling revealed significant effects of collocation fre­
quency and contingency on the reaction time and accuracy rates for both L1 
English speakers and advanced Chinese ESL learners, with the ESL learners 
demonstrating stronger sensitivity to phrasal frequency and contingency than 
L1 English speakers. In terms of reaction time, higher-frequency collocations 
required shorter reaction time than lower-frequency ones for both groups, while 
higher-MI collocations required longer reaction time than lower-MI ones for 
both groups; in both cases, the effects were larger for ESL learners than for 
L1 English speakers.The author explained the larger effect size for ESL learn­
ers using the power law of practice in language acquisition (DeKeyser, 2007). 
Specifically, the author argued that because learning effects “follow a logarith­
mic fashion, and progressively smaller learning effects take place with each 
occurrence of language use or practice” (p. 543), stronger phrasal frequency and 
contingency effects can be expected for learners at earlier stages of L2 acquisi­
tion than for L1 speakers. In terms of accuracy, higher-frequency collocations 
resulted in higher accuracy rates for both groups; L1 English speakers processed 
higher-MI collocations more accurately than lower-MI ones, while ESL learners 
processed higher-MI collocations less accurately than lower-MI ones. For both 
groups, the participants’ sensitivity to phrasal frequency and contingency was not 
moderated by their cognitive aptitudes.The author interpreted these findings as 
further evidence that the same statistical learning mechanisms that include both 
frequency and contingency are likely shared by L1 and L2 speakers in processing 
multiword sequences. 
The final study reviewed in this section was conducted by Wolter and 
Yamashita (2018), who focused on L2 processing of English adjective-noun col­
locations as well but with a different set of variables (i.e., word frequency, col-
locational frequency, L1 congruency, and L2 proficiency) and a different learner 
group (i.e., Japanese EFL learners) from those in Yi (2018). Their participants 
included a group of 27 L1 English undergraduate students enrolled at a university 
in the United States, a group of 24 L1 Japanese graduate-level EFL learners, and 
a group of 23 L1 Japanese undergraduate-level EFL learners.They administered 
the Eurocentres Vocabulary Size Test (EVST; Meara & Jones, 1990) to their par­
ticipants and found that the graduate-level EFL learners had a significantly larger 
vocabulary size than the undergraduate-level EFL learners. Four categories of 
adjective-noun collocations were developed with L1 Japanese in mind: 24 con­
gruent collocations (e.g., strong wind), 24 English-only (incongruent) collocations

---

<!-- page 77 -->

 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
60 Factors Influencing Second Language Processing and Production 
(e.g., low speed), 24 Japanese-only (translated) collocations (e.g., tall danger), and 72 
baseline items. For the first three categories of collocations, an initial list of candi­
date items was developed using high-frequency words, i.e., words within the most 
frequent 3,000-level (with few exceptions) in the JACET (Japanese Association of 
College English Teachers) List of 8000 Basic Words (JACET Committee of Basic 
Words Revision, 2003), which was the level appropriate for high school textbooks 
in Japan.A list of baseline items was then created by recombining the words from 
the other three types of collocations to ensure comparability of the lexical fre­
quency of the words used in all categories of items.The baseline items were less 
semantically meaningful and were intended to provide a threshold response time 
that could be used to compare the relative processing speed for the Japanese-only 
collocations (see discussion below).A Japanese language corpus consisting of texts 
from a Japanese daily broadsheet newspaper, the Chunichi Shimbun (Chunichi 
Newspaper) Database (1999 to 2003), was used to verify that the Japanese-only 
collocations were indeed acceptable in Japanese while the English-only colloca­
tions were not. COCA was then used to further verify the appropriateness of the 
classifications of the items. It was confirmed that all congruent and English-only 
items were indeed frequent in COCA and that the Japanese-only and baseline 
items were either absent in the corpus or occurred with a low frequency and a 
negative or low MI score (<1.0), indicating that those expressions were idiosyn­
cratic instead of significant co-occurrences.After confirming the classifications of 
the candidate items, the authors obtained non-lemmatized lexical and collocation 
frequencies of all items from the fiction subsection of COCA and the MI scores 
of all items from the whole corpus.The frequencies were then log transformed 
using natural logs. 
An acceptability judgment task was administered to each participant on a 
computer. The 144 collocations were presented on the screen one by one in a 
randomized order, and the participants were asked to make a binary decision on 
whether each word combination was commonly used in English by pressing a 
key. Response times were assessed for all items that received a “yes” response. Four 
responses faster than 450 milliseconds were excluded, and all remaining response 
times were log transformed.The response times were analyzed using mixed-effects 
modeling and correlations.The results indicated that all three groups of participants 
were sensitive to both lexical frequency and collocational frequency, confirming 
prior findings that L2 learners attend to frequency distributions for multiword units 
in the L2 (e.g., Siyanova-Chanturia et al., 2011) and that even for highly formulaic 
sequences that could be processed as a whole, frequency effects for the component 
words remain intact (e.g., Snider & Arnon, 2012). Meanwhile, L1 English speakers 
and graduate-level EFL learners attended more to collocational frequency than 
undergraduate-level EFL learners, indicating that higher-proficiency learners shifted 
away from reliance on lexical frequency to collocational frequency in collocational 
processing, trending toward a processing system more similar to that of L1 English 
speakers. The authors interpreted these findings as evidence in support of the

---

<!-- page 78 -->

 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Factors Influencing Second Language Processing and Production 61 
applicability of emergentist approaches to L2 learning and processing, which place 
at their core input frequency and probability, in understanding L2 collocational 
acquisition and processing.The results also indicated that both graduate-level and 
undergraduate-level EFL learners processed congruent collocations significantly 
faster than incongruent ones, while L1 English speakers processed congruent and 
incongruent collocations with comparable speed, indicating that congruency 
provides an advantage in collocational processing for L2 learners. The authors 
argued that this advantage could best be explained in terms of the effect of age 
or order of acquisition (Carroll & White, 1973). In other words, at the early stage 
of acquisition, L2 learners rely more on their L1 collocational knowledge and are 
thus able to process congruent L2 collocations or L1 collocations not transferrable 
to the L2 (such as the Japanese-only collocations in this study). With increased 
input exposure and proficiency, congruent collocations are further entrenched, 
incongruent collocations receive more exposure but will not overtake congruent 
collocations in accessibility without disproportionately greater exposure than the 
latter, while non-transferrable collocations become less prominent due to the lack 
of reinforcement in the input. 
Taken together, the studies reviewed in this section have demonstrated sig­
nificant frequency and contingency effects on the processing of several types of 
English and Chinese phraseological units by both L1 speakers and L2 learners 
and the usefulness of corpus analysis in developing experimental stimuli and/or 
obtaining frequency and contingency information about the stimuli. However, 
there appeared to be some differences in the results reported with respect to 
L2 learners’ level of sensitivity to frequency and contingency in comparison 
to L1 speakers. For example, in terms of processing speed, Ellis (2008) found 
that L1 speakers were more sensitive to contingency and less sensitive to fre­
quency than L2 learners, Kim and Kim (2012) found that L1 speakers were 
sensitive to a greater range of frequency than L2 learners,Yi et al. (2017) and Yi 
(2018) reported larger frequency and contingency effects for L2 learners than 
for L1 speakers, while Wolter and Yamashita (2018) reported greater sensitivity 
to collocational frequency for L1 speakers and higher-level L2 learners than 
for lower-level L2 learners.These differences may have arisen from differences 
in the types of phraseological units being processed, the processing tasks used, 
and the proficiency levels of the L2 learners examined. Meanwhile, the studies 
also consistently showed that L1 speakers processed higher-MI phraseological 
units faster than lower-MI ones and that higher-proficiency L2 learners became 
more similar to L1 speakers than lower-proficiency L2 learners in terms of 
their sensitivity to frequency and contingency in processing phraseological units. 
These findings thus confirm the central claims of usage-based constructionist 
approaches to SLA, while also pointing to the need for investigating the specific 
effects of frequency and contingency on different aspects of processing of dif­
ferent types of phraseological units in different languages by learners at different 
proficiency levels.

---

<!-- page 79 -->

 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
62 Factors Influencing Second Language Processing and Production 
3.3.3 L2 Processing and Production of Syntactic Constructions 
Beyond phraseological units, a few studies have also integrated corpus analysis 
to examine frequency and contingency effects in L2 learners’ processing and 
production of syntactic constructions. In this section, we take a close look at two 
studies in this category. In the first study, Ellis et al. (2014) used free association 
tasks to investigate how EFL learners’ access to VACs is sensitive to type-
token frequency distribution, VAC-verb contingency, and verb-VAC semantic 
prototypicality (we return to a discussion of prototypicality in Section 3.4).Their 
participants included 131 L1 English speakers enrolled at a university in the US, 
and 131 EFL learners from each of the following three L1 backgrounds: Czech, 
German, and Spanish, enrolled at research universities in the Czech Republic, 
Germany, and Spain, respectively. Each participant completed a survey online that 
presented a series of sentence frames with a missing verb one by one and prompted 
them to fill in the gap with the first word that comes to their mind.A total of 20 
different sentence frames were used, and each frame was presented twice, once 
with the subject it (e.g., it __ about the…) and once with he/she (e.g., s/he __ about 
the…). In addition to the sentence frame with the aboutVAC, the other 19 frames 
contained across, after, against, among, around, as, at, between, for, in, into, like, of, off, 
over, through, towards, under, and withVACs.The participants’ responses as well as the 
time they took to complete each sentence frame were recorded. 
Based on the assumption that learners acquire constructions using general 
learning mechanisms and that the general principles of cognition are such that the 
relation between frequency of usage and entrenchment follows a power law (e.g., 
Ellis, 2002), the authors predicted that the token frequencies of different verb types 
in a VAC in the learners’ usage experience would affect the accessibility of the verb 
types to the learners as VAC exemplars. For each VAC, the verb types generated 
by all participants in each of the four L1 groups for both prompt variants were 
aggregated, and the token-frequency of each verb type in the VAC was obtained 
from the BNC.The after, at, and inVACs were excluded from the analysis due to 
the difficulty in achieving satisfactory precision in their identification, leaving 17 
unique VACs for further analysis.The generation frequency of the verb types in 
each VAC was then correlated against the token-frequency of the verb types in 
the VAC in the BNC, following a log 10 transformation of all frequency counts. 
The mean correlation for the L1 English respondents was 0.55, and the mean 
correlations for the L1 Czech, German, and Spanish respondents were 0.59, 0.59, 
and 0.65, respectively. In the subsequent regression analyses, verb usage frequency 
in the VAC was also found to be a significant predictor of L1 and L2 speakers’ 
generation frequency of the verb types in the VAC.The authors interpreted these 
findings as meaning that across the 17 VACs analyzed, the responses for each VAC 
from both L1 and L2 English speakers were sensitive to verb usage frequency 
in the VAC. These findings were also taken to provide evidence for the claim 
in usage-based approaches to SLA that frequency effects on language learning,

---

<!-- page 80 -->

 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
  
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
Factors Influencing Second Language Processing and Production 63 
entrenchment, and fluency of linguistic processing are understood in terms of 
Hebbian learning, a type of learning characterized by the learners’ ability to 
extract statistical regularities from the environment, among other features (e.g., 
Ellis, 2002). 
Viewing the contingency of form-function mapping as a driving force of 
language learning, the authors predicted that verbs that are more faithful to 
specific VACs in usage should be more readily accessible to learners in those 
VACs than verbs that are more promiscuous and thus less faithful to those VACs. 
They measured faithfulness using delta P (Allan, 1980), a one-way dependency 
statistic shown to be predictive of cue-outcome learning in the literature of 
associative learning and psycholinguistic studies of form-function contingency 
in construction processing and usage (e.g., Ellis & Ferreira-Junior, 2009; Shanks, 
1995). Delta P is calculated as the probability of the outcome (O) given the cue 
(C) minus the probability of the outcome without the cue (-C). This can be
calculated either with the VAC as outcome and the verb as cue or the other way
around. As the free association task provided the learners with the construction
and asked them to produce the verb, the authors calculated delta P with the VAC
as cue and the verb as outcome (denoted as delta Pcw). Specifically, using the
contingency table in Table 3.1, this can be calculated with the following formula:
delta P cw=P(O|C) – P(O|-C)=(a/(a+b)) – (c/(c+d)).The closer this value is to
1.0, the higher the likelihood of the outcome is given the cue.
To assess whether the EFL learners’ access to VACs is sensitive to VAC-verb 
contingency, the authors correlated the frequency of the verbs generated for each 
VAC by the participants with delta Pcw calculated from the BNC. The mean 
correlation for the L1 English respondents was 0.48, and the mean correlations 
for the L1 German, Czech, and Spanish respondents were 0.53, 0.56, and 0.63, 
respectively. In the subsequent regression analyses, delta Pcw was found to be the 
major predictor of L1 and L2 speakers’ generation frequency of the verb types 
in the VAC, with a higher relative importance than frequency.These results were 
interpreted to mean that across the 17 VACs analyzed, the responses for each VAC 
from both L1 and L2 English speakers were sensitive to VAC-verb contingency. 
The authors further argued that language acquisition involves both the distri­
butional analysis of the language stream and the contingent analysis of percep­
tual activity and that learners acquire abstract constructions from concrete usage 
TABLE 3.1 Contingency table used to calculate delta P with the verb-argument construction 
(VAC) as cue and the verb as outcome (adapted from Ellis et al., 2014) 
Outcome (target verb) 
No outcome (other verbs) 
Cue (target VAC) 
a (target VAC with target verb) 
b (target VAC with other verbs) 
No cue (other VACs) c (other VACs with target verb) 
d (other VACs with other verbs)

---

<!-- page 81 -->

 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
64 Factors Influencing Second Language Processing and Production 
exemplars following statistical learning mechanisms that relate input and learner 
cognition. 
In the second study reviewed in this section, Azazil (2020) integrated two 
experimental studies with a complementary corpus study to investigate frequency 
effects in German EFL learners’ processing of the catenative verb construction 
(CVC) from a usage-based perspective. The CVC has been shown to be chal­
lenging to L2 English learners, who often have trouble choosing the type of 
non-finite complement (i.e., to-infinitive or -ing, e.g., enjoy to read vs. enjoy reading) 
preferred by L1 English speakers (e.g., Gries & Wulff, 2009). The first research 
question asked whether and the extent to which L2 learners’ choice of the target-
like complement type is affected by the following three measures:The frequency 
of the matrix verb, the token frequency of the verb in the CVC, and the relative 
frequency of the verb in the CVC, also referred to as the faithfulness of the verb 
for the CVC (a measure of contingency).The second research question pertained 
to verb-specific frequency effects, asking whether there are differences between 
verbs preferring to-infinitive and -ing complements in the learners’ choice of the 
target-like complement type, given the substantially higher type and token fre­
quency of the CVC with a to-infinitive complement than that of the CVC with 
an -ing complement. 
The first experimental study asked 22 undergraduate German EFL learners to 
perform a sentence completion task.The author consulted the BNC to identify 
six target-to and six target-ing verbs that were used roughly 95% of the time 
with a to-infinitive complement or an -ing complement, respectively. Each group 
consisted of two high frequency (target-to: refuse and manage; target-ing: avoid 
and enjoy), two medium frequency (target-to: serve and offer; target-ing: recall and 
practice), and two low frequency (target-to: proceed and hesitate; target-ing: postpone 
and celebrate) verbs, based on their token frequency in the CVC in the BNC.The 
overall frequency of each verb and the verb’s faithfulness to the CVC (calculated 
as the proportion of occurrences of the verb in the CVC) in the BNC were also 
obtained.The stimuli consisted of 24 test sentences (two per target verb) and 35 
filler items with seven frequent matrix verbs. Each test sentence started with a 
subject, followed by a catenative verb, a gap, and randomized components of the 
complement (with the verb in its base form) in brackets, and the participants were 
required to fill in the gap by putting the complement components in the right 
order and changing the base form of the verb to either a to-infinitive or -ing form. 
Each filler sentence started with a subject, followed by a gap and randomized 
components of the verb phrase, and the participants were required to fill in the 
gap by arranging the verb phrase components in the right order and changing 
the matrix verb to an appropriate inflected form. Of the 521 observations (i.e., 
sentences with target verbs) yielded by the task, 34.9% (182/521) had a non-
target catenative complement, with a greater proportion of target-ing verbs 
produced with non-target complements (132/259 or 50.79%) than target-to 
verbs (50/262 or 19.48%). Mixed-effects models were used to analyze the data

---

<!-- page 82 -->

 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Factors Influencing Second Language Processing and Production 65 
with target-likeness (target vs. non-target-like catenative complement) as the 
dependent variable and the following four predictor variables:Token frequency 
of the verb, token frequency of the verb in the CVC, faithfulness of the verb for 
the CVC, and the expected catenative complement type (to-infinitive vs. ing). The 
two token frequency measures did not significantly predict target-likeness, but the 
last two variables did. Learners were significantly more likely to produce target-
like catenative complements in sentences with more faithful verbs than in those 
with less faithful verbs in the sentence completion task. 
The second experiment examined the same participants’ perception of CVC 
with an acceptability rating ask.The task required the participants to rate a series 
of target (e.g., a sentence containing refused to accept) and non-target-like forms 
(e.g., a sentence containing refused accepting) on a four-point scale, with 4 indicating 
“clearly correct” and 1 “clearly incorrect”. A linear mixed-effects model was 
constructed with the ratings of the sentences as the dependent variable and the 
same predictor variables as in the first experiment, along with the target-likeness 
of the test sentences (i.e., target-like vs. non-target-like) as an additional fixed 
factor. The results showed that the matrix verb frequency was not a significant 
predictor of the ratings. Learners judged target-like sentences with highly faithful 
verbs significantly better than those with less faithful ones. In addition, target-like 
sentences were rated higher than non-target sentences if they contained a target-
to verb, but no difference was found if they contained a target-ing verb. 
To complement the findings from the two experiments, the author analyzed 
the German subcorpus of the EF-Cambridge Open Language Database 
(EFCAMDAT, Geertzen et al., 2013), which at the time of the analysis contained 
about 2.2 million words from 28,555 texts by 5,112 German EFL learners. To 
ensure enough observations in the learner data, five target-ing (i.e., avoid, enjoy, 
finish, mind, and risk) and five target-to verbs (i.e., appear, fail, decide, tend, and seek) 
with a relatively high frequency in the CVC in the BNC were analyzed. Using 
AntConc (Anthony, 2015), the author identified 548 instances of the CVC 
produced by 381 different learners and coded each instance for the matrix verb, 
the form of the catenative complement type, and target-likeness (i.e., whether 
the complement was target- or non-target-like). Different from the experimental 
studies, the token frequency of the matrix verb positively affected target-likeness, 
while the expected catenative complement type did not affect target-likeness. 
Similar to the experimental studies in the corpus study, the faithfulness of the verb 
for the CVC positively affected the target-likeness of the catenative complement 
used by learners in the CVC. 
Across the three studies, the token frequency of the matrix verb showed a 
significant effect on the learners’ choice of target-like catenative complement in 
the corpus study only but not in the two experimental studies.The author argued 
that the verbs used in the CVC in the corpus were highly frequent to begin with, 
as learners appeared to have avoided unfamiliar verbs in the CVC.These findings 
were interpreted as evidence for the existence of a construction between the matrix

---

<!-- page 83 -->

 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
66 Factors Influencing Second Language Processing and Production 
verb and the catenative complement, with the form-meaning mapping resulting 
from the interaction between the two components.This was why the frequency 
of the verb itself could not adequately enable learners to choose target-like non-
finite complements.The faithfulness of the verb for the CVC positively affected 
the learners’ choice of target-like non-finite complements in all three studies.The 
author interpreted these findings as evidence that a higher faithfulness promotes 
target-like entrenchment of the CVC. In terms of verb-specific frequency effects, 
the more frequent CVC with a to-infinitive complement consistently produced 
more target-like complement choices than the less frequent CVC with an -ing 
complement in all three studies.This finding was interpreted to indicate that the 
target-to construction is cognitively more entrenched and consequently better 
acquired than the target-ing construction because of its higher type and token 
frequency in the input.The author further argued that the findings support the 
idea that “the mental representation of language is comprised of item-specific 
as well as more abstract schema knowledge, where frequency determines the 
specificity with which the construction is entrenched” (p. 417). 
Taken together, the two studies showed variable effects of frequency on different 
L2 processing tasks (free association,sentence completion,and acceptability rating) 
for different syntactic constructions (VACs and the CVC) but consistent, robust 
effects of contingency on L2 processing of syntactic constructions. While the 
studies reviewed in Sections 3.3.1 and 3.3.2 focused on either L2 production or 
processing of lexical and phraseological constructions, the study by Azazil (2020) 
showcased the usefulness of complementing findings regarding the frequency and 
contingency effects on L2 processing of constructions with the analysis of these 
effects on L2 production of the same constructions using corpus data. 
3.3.4 L2 Phonological Production: Teacher Talk 
vs. Language Corpora as Input 
In this section, we review a study by Trofimovich et al. (2012), who adopted a 
frequency-based approach to examine the relationship between teacher input and 
learner output in instructed L2 phonology acquisition, focusing on the voiced 
interdental fricative /ð/ in English.While the recognition of the important role of 
input on learner output and the use of the frequency-based approach to examine 
this role are not new, what makes this study uniquely interesting is the authors’ 
argument that previous studies of frequency effects on L2 learning relied primarily 
on frequency counts from language corpora but rarely directly examined the input 
that L2 learners actually received.The teacher input analyzed was sourced from 
a 110,000-word corpus representing more than 40 hours of aural input in sixth-
grade intensive ESL classes collected at four time points with 100-hour intervals. 
Specifically, the authors analyzed a 57,818-word subcorpus comprised of three 
ESL teachers’ speech over two days.The video recordings were transcribed and 
all instances of the English /ð/ were coded into one of eight phonetic contexts:

---

<!-- page 84 -->

 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Factors Influencing Second Language Processing and Production 67 
Following vowels (e.g., do that), following voiceless stops (e.g., get that), following 
voiced stops (e.g., read that), following voiceless fricatives or affricates (e.g., miss 
that), following voiced fricatives or affricates (e.g., is that), following nasals (e.g., 
on that), following liquids (e.g., peel that), or occurring sentence initially or phrase 
internally following a pause (e.g., … that boy). The authors hypothesized that 
higher-frequency contextual instances of the English /ð/ would be easier to learn 
than lower-frequency ones. 
The student corpus contained 120 L1 French sixth-grade ESL students’ speech 
produced on the same two days.All students were taught by the three ESL teachers 
represented in the teacher input subcorpus analyzed.The authors characterized 
the students as beginning-level English learners with little exposure to English 
outside of school. On each of the two days, each student was asked to describe a 
sequence of pictures about two kids going on a picnic.The picture stories were 
recorded and transcribed, and the final student corpus contained 240 picture 
stories with 30,205 words. Each instance of the English /ð/ was independently 
coded into one of the eight phonetic contexts and rated for accuracy (either 
1 or 0, with 1 indicating the production sounds like a good English /ð/ and 0 
indicating the opposite) by two coders (out of a pool of five coders).The token 
counts in each context and the accuracy ratings by the two raters were both 
averaged. 
Input analyses focused on documenting teacher input properties in order 
to explore possible input-output relationships. The authors compared the 
proportion of words containing /ð/ in the teacher input to that in a sample of 
2,500 words randomly selected from the BNC and found these proportions to 
be largely comparable (11.2% in the BNC and between 7.4% and 11.1% in the 
three teachers’ speech samples).The frequency distribution of /ð/ across the eight 
phonetic contexts also followed a largely similar pattern in the teacher corpus 
and the BNC. Six frequent word types covered 95% and 92% of all /ð/ tokens at 
the two time points of the teacher corpus and 94% in the BNC; the sentence/ 
phrase initial context and the voiceless fricative/affricate context accounted for 
the largest and smallest proportion of tokens in both corpora.These properties 
showed that the teacher corpus was fairly representative of English language use. 
Output analyses focused on whether learner output reflected the properties of 
teacher input.Student output contained a higher proportion of tokens containing 
/ð/ (17.4% and 20.9% for the two time points, respectively) than teacher input, 
and the authors attributed this difference to the demands of the task, which 
entailed frequent reference to the characters in the story.As in teacher input, /ð/ 
tokens were also restricted to a few word types in learner output, with six most 
frequent word types covering 98% and 99% of all /ð/ tokens at the two points; 
the frequency distribution of the phonetic contexts in the learner output matched 
that in teacher input as well as that in the BNC. In addition, changes in the 
accuracy of learner output were restricted to the highest-frequency contextual 
instances of /ð/ in teacher input.

---

<!-- page 85 -->

 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
68 Factors Influencing Second Language Processing and Production 
The authors interpreted these findings as evidence that learners are sensitive to 
the distributional properties of teacher input as well as the language experienced 
outside the classroom and that the frequency patterns in large-scale general 
language corpora estimate learners’ language encounters reasonably well. The 
authors further argued that positive frequency effects on the accuracy of L2 
phonological production follow the power law of practice (DeKeyser, 2007), 
with the strongest effects in the early stages of learning, suggesting that providing 
learners with repeated input of targeted phonological features early on could help 
improve L2 production. 
The authors’ comparison of the frequency patterns of the target linguistic 
feature in a large-scale general-purpose corpus and a corpus of teacher input is 
a highly important one. The finding that these frequency patterns largely con­
verged is welcome news to researchers who use large general-purpose corpora to 
derive frequency and contingency information for target constructions in analyz­
ing frequency and contingency effects on L2 processing and production of con­
structions. Meanwhile, additional research verifying the convergence of frequency 
patterns of other constructions would prove useful. 
3.4 Other Factors 
In addition to frequency and contingency, a small number of usage-based studies 
have integrated corpus analysis to examine the effects of other factors on L2 
processing or production of constructions. In this section, we review two studies 
that combined corpus analysis with psycholinguistic experimental methods to 
investigate the effects of recency and semantic prototypicality on L2 processing 
of two different English syntactic constructions and a third study that employed 
corpus analysis to scrutinize the effects of lexical aspect and phonological saliency 
on L2 production of the English past tense. 
Language processing reflects recency effects, a phenomenon known as prim­
ing (McDonough & Trofimovich, 2008). Syntactic priming, for example, refers 
to the phenomenon that learners may use a specific syntactic structure after 
recent exposure to the same structure (Ellis & Collins, 2009). Gries and Wulff 
(2009) examined syntactic priming effects in German EFL learners’ process­
ing of the English gerund and infinitival complement constructions in a study 
designed to combine corpus-linguistic and psycholinguistic evidence to verify 
that these patterns are stored as constructions in the learners’ mental lexicon. 
In the corpus analysis part of the study, the authors retrieved all instances of 
the following from the British subcorpus of the International Corpus of English 
(ICE-GB): 1) One verb immediately followed by another verb, 2) an auxiliary 
followed by a verb, 3) one verb followed by to followed by another verb, and 4) 
one auxiliary followed by to followed by another verb.The 45,933 concordance 
lines retrieved were manually checked, and 480 ing-construction tokens and 
2,863 to-construction tokens were identified, with a total of 120 distinct verb

---

<!-- page 86 -->

 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
Factors Influencing Second Language Processing and Production 69 
TABLE 3.2 Contingency table used to compute the distinctive lexeme analysis 
to-construction 
ing-construction 
Target verb 
lemma 
Other verb 
lemmas 
a (token frequency of the verb 
lemma in the to-construction) 
c (token frequency of the 
to-construction with other verb 
b (token frequency of the verb lemma 
in the ing-construction) 
d (token frequency of the ing­
construction with other verb 
lemmas) 
lemmas) 
lemmas. A distinctive collexeme analysis (DCA; Gries & Stefanowitsch, 2004) 
was then computed to determine which verbs were strongly associated with 
the two complementation constructions, or in other words, best distinguished 
between the two constructions. Specifically, for each of the 120 verb lemmas, 
the p-value of the Fisher-Yates exact test was computed using the contingency 
table in Table 3.2 and then log-transformed to the base of 10.A statistically sig­
nificant (p < .05) value would be 1.3 or higher, with greater values indicating a 
higher level of distinctiveness. Six distinctive verbs for the to-construction (i.e., 
start, stop, avoid, enjoy, consider, finish) and six for the ing-construction (i.e., try, 
manage, attempt, fail, like, continue) were used as stimuli in the psycholinguistic 
experiments. 
The authors designed a questionnaire experiment with an acceptability task 
(18 prime sentences: Six experimental items and 12 distractors) and a sentence 
completion task (18 target sentence fragments: Six experimental items and 12 
distractors).The prime sentences in the rating task represented two independent 
variables, i.e., the structure of the sentence (CX_PRIME; to-construction vs. 
ing-construction) and the construction for which the main verb in the sentence 
is distinctive (V_PRIME_DIST; to-construction vs. ing-construction). The 
dependent variable was the rating, using a scale from -3 (totally ungrammatical) 
to+3 (perfect well-formedness).The sentence fragments in the completion task 
represented the same two independent variables plus a few additional ones: 
The construction for which the verb provided in the fragment is distinctive 
(V_TARGET_DIST; to-construction vs. ing-construction), the construction a 
participant provided to the last experimental item (SELF_PRIME; to-completion, 
ing-completion, or other), the proportion of to-completions out of all to- or ing­
completions a subject had provided in previous items (SELF_TO_RATIO), 
and the logged lemma frequency of the verbs in the fragment in the BNC (V_ 
FREQ).The independent variable was the participant’s response (to-completion, 
ing-completion, or other).The prime sentences for rating and the target sentence 
fragments for completion were presented in alternating order to the participants, 
i.e., 94 German undergraduate EFL learners studying English linguistics at a 
university in Germany.

---

<!-- page 87 -->

 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
70 Factors Influencing Second Language Processing and Production 
The sentence acceptability rating data were analyzed with a linear model, and 
the results showed a significant effect of the interaction of CX_PRIME and V_ 
PRIME_DIST on the ratings. Specifically, for each construction, the ratings were 
higher when the prime sentence contains the construction for which the verb is 
distinctive.The sentence completion data were analyzed with logistic regression. 
The best model showed that V_TARGET_DIST had the strongest influence on 
the learners’ complementation choice: The preferred construction of the verb 
in the target fragment was more likely produced by the participants. A short-
distance production-to-production priming effect (SELF_PRIME) was observed, 
indicating that the participants’ last completion affected the immediately follow­
ing completion. Specifically, the participants were more likely to produce an ing­
construction after producing an ing-construction, and they were equally likely to 
produce a to-construction after producing a to- or other-construction. 
No priming was observed from the rating task to the sentence completion task. 
V_FREQ had a significant main effect as well, with more frequent verb lemmas 
used more often with the to-construction; however, the effect of its interaction 
with V_TARGET_DIST indicated that as the overall frequency of a verb lemma 
increases, it becomes more likely to admit both constructions more equally. 
The authors speculated that the absence of rating-to-production priming 
could be due to a task effect, arguing that the rating task may prompt advanced 
EFL learners to weigh different constructional alternatives in completing the 
target.With respect to production-to-production priming, the authors explained 
that to-constructions were equally frequently produced after to-primes and other-
primes likely because the close translational equivalent of to-constructions in 
German served as a quasi-default when no specific complementation construction 
was evoked by the prime and because the advanced learners may have attempted 
to demonstrate a command of diverse patterns. The strong production-to­
production priming effect for ing-constructions arose because such constructions 
have no translational equivalent in German; they were also learned later in the 
acquisition process and were therefore more likely to benefit from priming. 
As mentioned in Section 3.3, Ellis et al. (2014) examined the ways in which 
EFL learners’ access to VACs is sensitive to verb-VAC semantic prototypicality, 
in addition to type-token frequency distribution and VAC-verb contingency. 
The authors understood the prototype of a category as the best exemplar that 
summarizes the most representative features of the category and predicted that 
verbs that are more prototypical of the VAC semantics should be produced 
more frequently by the learners in the generative tasks. They determined the 
prototypicality of a verb-VAC combination by assessing how central the verb 
is in the semantic network connecting the verb types that occur in the VAC. 
The semantic network is built using all verb types that occur in the VAC in 
the BNC, with the nodes representing verb types and the edges representing 
the semantic similarity between pairs of verb types. The semantic similarity of 
a pair of verbs reflected the distance between their conceptual categories in the

---

<!-- page 88 -->

 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Factors Influencing Second Language Processing and Production 71 
hierarchical structure of the WordNet (Pedersen et al., 2004) and was computed 
with the WordNet Path Similarity in the Natural Language Tool Kit (NLTK; Bird 
et al., 2009).The specific measure used to assess prototypicality was betweenness 
centrality, which equals the number of shortest paths from all vertices to all others 
passing through a node (McDonough & De Vleeschauwer, 2012). 
To determine whether the EFL learners’ access to VACs is sensitive to verb-
VAC semantic prototypicality, the authors correlated the frequency of the verbs 
generated for each VAC by the participants with the betweenness centrality values 
of the verbs in the semantic network of the verb types occurring in the VAC in 
the BNC.The mean correlation for the L1 English respondents was 0.46, and the 
mean correlations for the L1 German, Spanish, and Czech respondents were 0.44, 
0.43, and 0.35, respectively. In the subsequent regression analyses, betweenness 
centrality was found to be a significant predictor of L1 and L2 speakers’ generation 
frequency of the verb types in the VAC, but with a lower relative importance than 
both contingency and frequency.The authors interpreted the effects of semantic 
prototypicality in terms of Anderson’s (1983) spreading activation theory of 
semantic memory. The prototype has a frequency advantage: It tends to have a 
high token frequency, which contributes to its strong association with the VAC in 
its own right. It also has the network centrality advantage: Any response spreads 
activation of other members in the network; sitting at the center of the network, 
the prototype receives the most spreading activation from other responses. 
In the final study reviewed in this section, Zhao and Shirai (2018) adopted the 
usage-based perspective to explore the effects of lexical aspect and phonological 
saliency on Arabic EFL learners’ acquisition of the English past tense, testing 
in particular whether this acquisition followed the predictions of the Aspect 
Hypothesis (AH; Bardovi-Harlig, 2000) and the Phonological Saliency Hypothesis 
(PSH;Wolfram, 1985).The primary prediction of the AH is that simple past and 
perfective marking will start with telic verbs (i.e., verbs whose semantic feature 
encodes an inherent endpoint, e.g., finish) and gradually expand to atelic verbs 
(i.e., verbs encoding states, e.g., have, or activities, e.g., walk), while the opposite is 
true for imperfective marking (e.g., Bardovi-Harlig, 2000).The PSH accounts for 
the potential effect of phonological saliency on the L2 acquisition of tense-aspect 
morphology, predicting, for example, that past tense marking is more likely for 
irregular verbs than for regular verbs because the present and past-tense forms 
of irregular verbs are more distinct than those of regular verbs (Wolfram, 1985). 
Their participants were 20 Arabic high-school EFL learners studying in 
a summer program at a Qatar campus of an American university. The learners 
were divided into a basic-level group and an intermediate-to-advanced group 
based on their scores on the reading comprehension and grammar sections of 
a multi-choice English proficiency test. Each participant completed a written 
cloze test in which they were asked to read a story and provide the appropriate 
tensed forms (primarily the past tense) of 59 base-form verbs. The verbs were 
distributed across three lexical aspectual categories: 16 states (e.g., want, think), 11

---

<!-- page 89 -->

 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
72 Factors Influencing Second Language Processing and Production 
activities (e.g., walk, sit), and 32 telic events (e.g., decide, catch). In addition, they 
also completed a personal narrative task in which they were asked to freely tell 
a personal story.The authors audio-recorded the narratives, transcribed them in 
the CHAT format, and then uploaded the transcribed narratives to the TalkBank 
database and extracted verb tokens marked for simple past from them using the 
CLAN software (MacWhinney & Wagner, 2010). 
The authors coded each target verb token in the cloze test and each past-
tense verb form supplied by the learners in the narratives for correctness (correct 
vs. incorrect), lexical aspect (telic, states, activities), and morphological regularity 
(regular vs. irregular).Accuracy of usage was calculated as the ratio of the number 
of past-tense forms produced to the number of obligatory contexts for simple 
past-tense forms. They examined the AH by comparing the accuracy of past-
tense marking in different lexical aspectual classes and the PSH by comparing the 
accuracy of past-tense marking for regular and irregular verbs. 
Overall, the results showed that lexical aspect better predicted Arabic EFL 
learners’ use of past tense markers than phonological saliency.Arabic EFL learners’ 
acquisition of English past-tense marking followed the prediction of the AH 
in the oral personal narratives, with low-proficiency learners showing higher 
accuracy for telic verbs than atelic verbs, while high-proficiency learners showing 
comparable accuracy for all verbs. However, the AH was not supported by the 
results from the cloze task, in which all learners achieved comparable accuracy for 
state verbs and telic verbs.The authors proposed three possible explanations for this 
finding: Morphological features of the learners’ L1 (i.e.,Arabic), the distributional 
bias in the input, and the high frequency of the copula/auxiliary BE (which 
was frequent in the input and therefore easier to acquire) and negative sentences 
(in which past tense was marked on the auxiliary verb rather than the main 
verb) in the case of state verbs in the data. Phonological saliency demonstrated a 
marginal effect (p =.056) on the accuracy of past-tense marking in the narrative 
task, with low-proficiency learners marking irregular verbs more accurately than 
regular verbs, but no effect in the cloze task. However, the authors noted that 
other more refined categorizations of phonological saliency within regular and 
irregular verbs should be investigated. The authors interpreted their findings as 
confirming the notion that L2 acquisition is determined by a variety of input and 
psycholinguistic factors. 
Collectively, the studies reviewed in this section highlight the importance of 
investigating the effects of input factors other than frequency and contingency on 
L2 processing and production and showcased the usefulness of corpus analysis in 
such investigations. Recency, semantic prototypicality, and saliency all had either 
significant or close to significant effects on L2 processing or production of syntactic 
or grammatical constructions.Their effects appeared to be either less ubiquitous 
or weaker than the effects of frequency and contingency, but such a finding needs 
to be validated with more diverse or fine-grained operationalizations of these 
factors and a wider range of constructions.The authors of two of the three studies

---

<!-- page 90 -->

 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Factors Influencing Second Language Processing and Production 73 
explicitly discussed the methodological contributions of their research. Gries 
and Wulff (2009) pointed out that the method of DCA proved instrumental in 
identifying verbs that produced significant differences in the experiments.They 
further argued that the use of corpus data in their study painted a substantially 
more varied picture of the distribution of verbs in to- and ing-constructions than 
is usually reported in theory-oriented research or described in instructional 
materials. Zhao and Shirai (2018) also concluded with an elaborate discussion of 
the usefulness of accessible high quality learner corpora,such as those that control 
for proficiency, task, and L1, for advancing our understanding of SLA. 
Gries and Wulff’s (2009) comment regarding the differences between the 
distributional patterns observed in large-scale corpus data and the description of 
such patterns in instructional materials deserves further attention.This comment 
is also supported by Anderson’s (2007) finding that the rules of word order related 
to adjective positions in French textbooks used in the US are incongruent with 
the word-order frequency patterns found in corpora of authentic French-language 
texts or oral input in American university classroom settings. In the backdrop of 
usage-based claims of language acquisition and processing, it is desirable that the 
limited attention to comparing frequency patterns in large-scale general-purpose 
corpora and teacher input be extended to include descriptions of such patterns 
in instructional materials. 
3.5 Summary 
In this chapter, we started with a brief overview of the basic claims of usage-based 
approaches to SLA regarding the nature of the SLA process and factors that may 
affect L2 acquisition, processing,and production.We then dedicated the bulk of the 
chapter to a review of empirical studies that integrated corpus analysis to examine 
the effects of various factors on L2 processing and/or production.The findings of 
these studies indicate that frequency and contingency are particularly important 
determinants of L2 processing and production of various types of constructions, 
but other factors such as recency, semantic prototypicality, and saliency play a 
role as well.These studies have revealed the importance to consider a wide range 
of constructions and L2 processing tasks in order to arrive at a comprehensive 
understanding of the effects of various input factors.They have also demonstrated 
the usefulness of corpus analysis in this line of research, particularly for obtaining 
usage statistics about target constructions, selecting experimental stimuli, and 
analyzing L2 production. 
Several issues are worthy of further exploration. First, the effects of the 
various factors reported in the studies reviewed should be interpreted with their 
operationalizations in mind. For instance, various measures have been used to 
operationalize contingency (as discussed in Section 3.3), semantic prototypicality 
has been operationalized in ways other than betweenness centrality (e.g., the use 
of MI by Abugaber, 2020), and diverse operationalizations of saliency exist (e.g.,

---

<!-- page 91 -->

 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
74 Factors Influencing Second Language Processing and Production 
Goldschneider & DeKeyser, 2005). Researchers have argued for the need for 
more valid operationalizations (e.g., Gries & Ellis, 2015) as well as the use of 
more comprehensive operationalizations (e.g., Zhao & Shirai, 2018) of different 
input factors, and continued expansion and validation of the repertoire of their 
operationalizations will further enrich our understanding of their effects on L2 
processing and production. Second, existing research has focused primarily on 
L2 processing and production in the general language domain using large-scale 
general-purpose corpora as reference corpora. It would be useful to explore how 
usage-based claims play out in L2 processing and production in highly specialized 
domains and genres, e.g., in terms of whether they are affected more by usage 
patterns in general language input or by those in specialized language input. 
Finally, from the usage-based perspective, it would appear desirable to align the 
distributional patterns of target constructions or the descriptions of such patterns 
in pedagogical materials with those in the general language input to maximize the 
benefit of L2 instruction to L2 learners.The attention by Trofimovich et al. (2012) 
to distributional patterns in teacher input and the observations by Anderson 
(2007) and Gries and Wulff (2009) of the discrepancy between the descriptions 
of distributional patterns in instructional materials and those in general-purpose 
corpora directly pertain to this issue. Pedagogy-oriented usage-based research 
employing large-scale corpus analysis to systematically profile the distributional 
patterns of different constructions in the input is emerging (e.g.,Tracy-Ventura 
& Cuesta Medina, 2018). Such research will prove instrumental in assessing and 
facilitating the alignment between authentic language input and instructional 
materials.
