<!-- page 96 -->

  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
4 
SECOND LANGUAGE 
DEVELOPMENT TRAJECTORIES 
AND INFLUENTIAL FACTORS 
4.1 Introduction 
In Chapter 3, we reviewed the main tenets of usage-based approaches to 
second language acquisition (SLA) and a body of usage-based research that 
integrated corpus linguistic analysis in investigating the effects of various input 
factors on second language (L2) processing and production. In this chapter, we 
review empirical studies that employed corpus linguistic methods to examine 
the trajectories of L2 development and/or the input, learner, and task factors 
that influence such trajectories. Many of these studies were conducted within 
the usage-based linguistics (UBL) theoretical framework with a focus on L2 
development of different types of constructions,but studies from other theoretical 
perspectives and those focusing on other aspects of L2 development, such as the 
development of complexity, accuracy, and fluency (CAF) and the development 
of academic stance, are included as well. Some studies in the L2 development 
literature adopted a quasi-longitudinal design to track L2 development from low 
to high proficiency levels, using cross-sectional corpora consisting of language 
samples produced by learners at different proficiency levels (e.g., Alexopoulou 
et al.,2015;De Clercq & Housen,2019;Murakami & Alexopoulou,2016;Römer, 
2019;Thewissen, 2013).We will not cover such studies in this chapter as they are 
to some extent similar in spirit to studies reporting variation in L2 production 
among learners at different language proficiency levels, which we discussed in 
Section 2.3.1. Additionally, it is often the case that patterns of cross-proficiency 
differences do not match trajectories of longitudinal development (e.g., Kyle 
et al., 2021). As such, we focus on studies that adopted a genuine longitudinal 
design here, i.e., those that used longitudinal corpora consisting of language 
samples collected at different time points from the same group of L2 learners to 
DOI: 10.4324/9781003054948-4

---

<!-- page 97 -->

 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
80 Second Language Development Trajectories and Influential Factors 
track learner development. Additionally, we focus on results pertaining to group 
developmental trends in this chapter and do not include studies that focused on 
one or two individual learners (e.g., Broeder, 1995; Eskildsen, 2009; Li et al., 
2014), even though the authors of those studies may have sourced the data of their 
focal learners from a larger longitudinal learner corpus.The discussion of learner 
variation in L2 development is reserved for Chapter 5. 
4.2 L2 Development of Complexity, Accuracy, and Fluency 
Let us start our review by taking a look at four studies that employed longitudinal 
learner corpora to examine L2 development of complexity, accuracy, and fluency. 
Most studies in this area have focused on the longitudinal development of syntactic 
complexity in L2 writing, while research on the development of accuracy and 
fluency is relatively scant. In Section 2.2, we discussed a range of ways in which 
these constructs have been operationalized. For example, for syntactic complexity, 
holistic and fine-grained structural complexity measures as well as usage-based 
indices of syntactic sophistication have all been used in L2 writing syntactic 
complexity research.The differential levels of attention paid to different constructs 
as well as the diverse ways in which these constructs have been operationalized are 
reflected in the four studies reviewed.The patterns of significant changes in CAF 
as a function of time spent learning the L2 have been the primary focus of this 
line of research, and some attention has been paid to the effects of task variables 
(e.g., genre) and their interactions with time as well. 
In Section 2.4, we discussed Yoon and Polio’s (2017) study of English as a 
second language (ESL) learners’ linguistic development in two written genres 
with respect to their exploration of the possible reasons for genre differences in 
complexity. This is also the only study among the four reviewed here that has 
examined L2 learners’ longitudinal development in all three CAF constructs, and 
we discuss the results they reported on this development here.As a reminder, their 
learner data consisted of 111 narrative essays and 111 argumentative essays written 
by 37 adult ESL students studying at a US university at six different time points 
over a 15-week semester. Each essay was analyzed using a set of CAF measures. 
Syntactic complexity was assessed using 12 of the 14 measures (all but clauses 
per sentence and complex T-units per T-unit) in the L2 Syntactic Complexity 
Analyzer (L2SCA; Lu, 2010); lexical complexity was assessed using three measures 
from Coh-Metrix (Graesser et al., 2004), namely, average word length, word 
frequency index (i.e., the mean logarithmic frequency of all words in an essay, 
using frequency norms derived from Baayen et al.’s (1995) CELEX database), 
and vocd-D; accuracy was measured as the number of syntactic, morphological, 
preposition, and spelling errors per 100 words; and fluency was measured by the 
number of words in the essay produced within a 30-minute time limit.The authors 
conducted a series of two-way repeated-measures ANOVAs with time and genre 
as within-subjects variables to examine how the CAF measures developed over

---

<!-- page 98 -->

 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Second Language Development Trajectories and Influential Factors 81 
the course of one semester in the ESL learners’ writing and whether there was 
any interaction between time and genre on any of the measures. Among the 
12 syntactic complexity measures, mean length of sentence increased significantly 
over time with no interaction effect, while mean length of T-unit and clauses per 
T-unit both increased significantly in narrative essays but not in argumentative 
essays.The lexical complexity, accuracy, and fluency measures, however, showed 
no significant change over time. 
These findings showed that, for the ESL learners at the proficiency level 
examined in Yoon and Polio’s (2017) study, limited development in syntactic 
complexity could be expected in a semester, but lexical complexity, fluency, 
and accuracy would likely take a longer time to develop. As we will see in the 
next section, the longitudinal development of lexical complexity has also been 
examined in the body of research on L2 lexical development. The results on 
fluency and accuracy reported here call for additional research on these constructs 
across longer observation periods and preferably using richer operationalizations 
as well.The significant effects of genre on the CAF measures and of the interaction 
between genre and time on the development of syntactic complexity measures 
reported in this study highlight the need to control for genre in L2 writing 
development research. 
While Yoon and Polio (2017) operationalized syntactic complexity using 
holistic indices of complexity at diverse structural levels, Kreyer and Schaub 
(2018) narrowly focused on the development of noun phrase (NP) complexity 
of German English as a foreign language (EFL) learners. Their data consisted 
of 95 expository and argumentative texts (28,188 words total) produced by 
15 German EFL learners over their last three years of secondary school (39 texts 
in Grade 10, 30 in Grade 11, and 26 in Grade 12) sampled from the Marburg 
Corpus of Intermediate Learner English (MILE; Kreyer, 2015), a longitudinal 
database of German secondary-school EFL learners’ writing. The proficiency 
levels of the learners were estimated to be level B1 in the Common European 
Framework of Reference (CEFR) for languages when entering Grade 10 and 
CEFR level B2 or C1 in Grade 12.The authors manually extracted all NPs that 
were either “immediate constituents of the clause” (Pallotti, 2015, p. 123) (e.g., 
the subject or object) or part of another non-NP structure (e.g., a prepositional 
phrase), excluding NPs that were embedded within another NP. NPs consisting 
of pronouns and proper names and those copied verbatim from task descriptions 
were filtered. 
Two holistic measures of NP complexity were calculated, i.e., NP length and 
the number of NP modifiers per 1,000 words.The authors included all remain­
ing 4,077 NPs in the analysis of NP length but excluded an additional 155 NPs 
that were partially copied from task descriptions in the analysis of NP modifica­
tion.The types of NP modification analyzed were based on the developmental 
sequence of NP modification hypothesized by Biber et al. (2011), with adapta­
tions from Parkinson and Musgrave (2014) as well as the authors themselves. In

---

<!-- page 99 -->

 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
82 Second Language Development Trajectories and Influential Factors 
these hypothesized stages, NP modification would start in Stage 2 with attribu­
tive adjectives. In Stage 3, learners would start using participial premodifiers, 
relative clauses, nouns as premodifiers, of-phrase as postmodifiers, and postmodi­
fying prepositional phrases other than -of. In Stage 4, they would start using -ed 
and -ing participial clauses as postmodifiers. Finally, in Stage 5, they would start 
using preposition+nonfinite complement clause, that-noun complement clause, 
appositive noun phrases as postmodifiers, to-clauses as postmodifiers, and other 
types of NP modifiers. For each NP, all occurrences of all modification types 
were counted. 
Repeated-measures ANOVAs revealed no significant difference in either NP 
length or the number of NP modifiers per 1,000 words among the three time 
points, although both measures trended higher from Grade 10 to Grades 11 and 
12. To test Biber et al.’s (2011) hypothesized developmental stages, the authors 
further analyzed the frequencies of individual modification types. Parkinson and 
Musgrave (2014) put forth two expectations regarding the frequency distribution 
of the NP modification types based on Biber et al.’s (2011) hypothesized stages. 
The first was that lower proficiency learners would use a greater proportion of 
earlier-stage NP modification types than higher proficiency learners. The sec­
ond was that higher proficiency learners would better approximate expert writers 
than lower proficiency learners in terms of the frequency distribution of the NP 
modification types used. Kreyer and Schaub (2018) compared the normalized fre­
quencies (per 1,000 words) of each NP modifier type across the three grade levels; 
they further compared the frequencies for all their learners combined (represent­
ing intermediate learners) to the frequencies reported for English for Academic 
Purposes (EAP) learners and Master of Arts (MA) students (representing advanced 
learners) by Parkinson and Musgrave (2014) and for first language (L1) English 
speakers (representing expert writers) reported by Biber and Gray (2011) and 
Biber et al. (1999). Although the overall amount of NP modification increased 
steadily from intermediate learners to EAP learners, MA students, and L1 English 
speakers, Parkinson and Musgrave’s (2014) first expectation was not supported, as 
the proportions of most NP modification types remained relatively stable across 
the three grades.The second expectation did not clearly materialize in the data, 
either, as for most features, the frequencies for intermediate learners were either 
already close to the frequencies of expert writers (e.g., relative clauses) or did not 
change over the three years (e.g., participial premodifiers). Meanwhile, results of an 
analysis of the emergence of different NP modification types in five intermediate 
learners in terms of whether they used each individual feature at each grade level 
offered some tentative support for Biber et al.’s (2011) stages of development, with 
features in Stages 2 and 3 used by more learners than features in Stages 4 and 5. 
The study’s focus on NP complexity in adolescent EFL learners is a highly 
important one.The lack of significant growth in the two holistic indices of NP 
complexity in the data might not be surprising, given that high levels of NP com­
plexity have generally been found to be more characteristic of advanced rather

---

<!-- page 100 -->

 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Second Language Development Trajectories and Influential Factors 83 
than intermediate L2 English proficiency (e.g., Lu, 2011).The detailed analysis of 
different NP modification types undertaken by the authors to verify the devel­
opmental stages hypothesized by Biber et al. (2011) has high theoretical value, as 
these stages were proposed as a “radically new approach for the study of complex­
ity in student writing development” (Biber et al., 2011, p. 5). However, it should 
be noted that the approach taken for this part of the analysis was not longitudinal 
but cross-sectional, and it would be desirable to carry out such validation utilizing 
larger and longer-time longitudinal learner corpora that would allow less frequent 
NP modification types to emerge. 
While the first two studies examined the development of L2 writing syntactic 
complexity at different structural levels, the types of indices they used were of 
the same nature and shared the limitation that they “do not take into account 
the relative frequency of the syntactic forms or the relationship between the 
syntactic forms and the lexical items with which they are used” (Kyle et al., 2021, 
p. 782). In light of this limitation, Kyle et al. (2021) examined the longitudinal 
development of a number of usage-based indices of verb-argument construction 
(VAC) sophistication in a group of L1 Dutch EFL learners.The authors sourced 
their data from a larger corpus of English essays written by 20 secondary-school 
Dutch students in the Netherlands over two academic years (Verspoor & Smiskova, 
2012) and included in their analysis essays from nine students who completed 
all written assignments in those two years. The students were aged 12–13 and 
were characterized as A2 level learners at the beginning of the study based on 
scores of their essays provided by certified CEFR raters. Each learner submitted 
an untimed essay of up to 1,000 characters with no reference to external resources 
three times a year (October, February, and June), and the data included a total of 
54 essays with a mean length of 181.722 words.The six topics used in the study 
included four short stories (e.g., “Write a short story about your new school, 
friends, and teachers”), one describing a favorite holiday and explaining why it 
is special, and one writing a letter to the school principal to explain the student’s 
position toward a school uniform rule.The authors pointed out that these topics 
had ecological validity as they were relevant to the students and that they should 
not require specialized language. 
All essays were analyzed using five syntactic complexity indices from the L2SCA 
(Lu, 2010), namely, mean length of T-unit (MLT), mean length of clause (MLC), 
dependent clauses per clause (DC/C), coordinate phrases per clause (CP/C), 
and complex nominals per clause (CN/C). They were also analyzed using six 
VAC sophistication indices from the Tool for the Automatic Analysis of Syntactic 
Sophistication and Complexity (TAASSC; Kyle, 2016), including three related 
to frequency (main verb frequency, VAC frequency, and verb-VAC frequency) 
and three related to association strength (namely, delta P VAC to verb, delta P 
verb to VAC, and bidirectional verb-VAC association strength). These indices 
were computed using frequency and association strength information derived 
from the written sections of the Corpus of Contemporary American English

---

<!-- page 101 -->

 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
˘
84 Second Language Development Trajectories and Influential Factors 
(COCA; Davies, 2009).We described the delta P measures in Section 3.3.3.The 
bidirectional verb-VAC association strength measure was computed as follows 
using the contingency table presented in Table 3.1.

The authors first confirmed that the learners’ writing scores increased signifi­
cantly over time. They then built a separate linear mixed-effects (LME) model 
in R for each syntactic complexity and VAC sophistication index to examine 
whether it developed over time, with the index as the dependent variable, time 
as a fixed effect, and participant as a random effect. Among the five syntactic 
complexity indices, DC/C and MLT both increased significantly over time with 
moderate to large effect sizes, MLC and CN/C did not demonstrate a clear rela­
tionship with time, while CP/C was removed due to its lack of a normal distri­
bution. Controlling for the influence of DC/C and MLT on each other, Kyle 
et al. (2021) reported DC/C as “the clearest and most accurate indicator of lon­
gitudinal growth in syntactic complexity in this dataset” (p. 798).Among the six 
VAC sophistication indices, main verb frequency and verb-VAC frequency both 
decreased significantly over time, while the other four indices did not demon­
strate a clear relationship with time. Controlling for the influence of main verb 
frequency and verb-VAC frequency on each other, Kyle et al. (2021) reported 
main verb frequency as exhibiting a meaningful relationship with time. Finally, 
entering the two indices demonstrating unique meaningful relationships with 
time into a linear regression model to predict the time point at which a text was 
written, the authors reported significantly main effects for both DC/C and main 
verb frequency, which jointly explained 58.3% of the variance. 
Kyle et al.’s (2021) findings indicated that as the adolescent EFL learners 
spent more time learning English over the two-year period, they tended to 
produce more dependent clauses (thus also longer T-units) and lower-fre­
quency main verbs (thus also lower-frequency verb-VAC combinations).The 
result on DC/C aligns with findings from cross-sectional studies (e.g., Lu, 
2011) but differs from that reported in Yoon and Polio’s (2017) longitudinal 
study, which found no significant increase in DC/C over time.This discrep­
ancy in results may have arisen from the differences in the proficiency levels of 
the learners examined and/or those in the length of the observation periods 
in the two longitudinal studies.The result on main verb frequency aligns with 
Bulté et al.’s (2008) result on frequency-based lexical sophistication measures 
(see Section 4.3), supporting UBL accounts of L2 learning. However, the 
lack of longitudinal development in VAC frequency or verb-VAC associa­
tion strength measures countered expected UBL accounts of L2 learning (see 
Section 3.2). One possible explanation offered by the authors for the absence

---

<!-- page 102 -->

 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Second Language Development Trajectories and Influential Factors 85 
of development in these measures was the potential discrepancy between the 
input received by the learners and the types of language present in the writ­
ten sections of COCA. This is a highly relevant point, as usage-based stud­
ies have often used large-scale general-purpose corpora such as COCA and 
the British National Corpus (BNC; Burnard, 2007) to derive frequency and 
association strength measures (see also the studies reviewed in Chapter 3), 
with the assumption that they reasonably represent L1 and L2 learners’ lan­
guage input. In Sections 4.3–4.5, we will examine several studies that used 
the language produced by the L2 learners’ L1 instructors or interlocutors to 
represent L1 input. 
The three studies reviewed so far all focused on the CAF development of L2 
English learners. Notably, studies using longitudinal learner corpora to examine 
the CAF development of L2 learners of languages other than English are still 
rare. In the final study reviewed in this section,Vyatkina et al. (2015) examined 
syntactic complexity development of beginning level L2 German learners from a 
dynamic usage-based perspective, focusing in particular on syntactic modification. 
The authors sourced their data from the Kansas Developmental Learner Corpus 
(KANDEL;Vyatkina, 2016), comprised of L2 German writing samples written by 
multiple cohorts of college students enrolled in a basic German language program 
at a university in the US over four 16-week-long semesters.The study used data 
from 12 students (five male and seven female) who completed the full four-semes­
ter-long sequence. Among the 12 students, eight were ab initio learners and four 
were beginning learners who had taken German courses in high school.With the 
exception of one learner who was in her 30s, all other learners were aged between 
18 to 22 years.The writing samples were essays written for actual curricular tasks 
every three to five weeks, and the writing genres progressed from narratives and 
descriptions in the first year to formal letters and essays requiring explanatory and 
interpretive elements in the second year.With the exception of essays written at 
time points 11–12 and 14–16, which were written at home, all other essays were 
written during a 50-minute class with no access to external resources.The writing 
samples were corrected for morpho-syntactic and orthographic errors and part-of­
speech (POS) tagged using the TreeTagger (Schmid, 1994).The authors searched 
for prenominal (attributive) adjectives (ADJA), cardinal numbers (CARD), predic­
ative and adverbial adjectives (ADJP), and adverbs (ADV) by their respective POS 
tags; identified verbal and sentential prepositional phrases (PREP) by searching 
for phrases headed by a preposition and then manually filtering those modifying 
nouns; and searched for relative clauses (REL) and adverbial subordinate clauses 
(SUB) using relative pronouns and a set of subordinating conjunctions as proxies. 
The first analysis focused on the development of the size and range of the 
L2 German learners’ syntactic modification system. For size, the authors plotted 
the cumulative frequency counts for all modifier types used by each learner on 
the timeline and observed no clear development for either the group or indi­
vidual learners. For range, the authors plotted the mean frequency counts for

---

<!-- page 103 -->

 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
86 Second Language Development Trajectories and Influential Factors 
each modifier type (normalized by the token counts in each text) on the time­
line and observed no uniform developmental pattern of the different modifier 
types as a global syntactic category.The second analysis examined group devel­
opmental trends in the frequency of different modifier types.The authors built 
a separate generalized LME model for each of the seven modifier types, with its 
frequency as the dependent variable, time as a fixed effect, and task and writer as 
random effects. Different types of modifiers exhibited different group develop­
ment trends:ADJA, SUB, and REL increased significantly over time, CARD and 
ADJP decreased significantly over time, PREP increased significantly in the first 
half but then decreased significantly in the second half, while ADV did not change 
significantly over time. 
The authors concluded that, viewed as a whole, the size and range of the L2 
German learners’ syntactic modification system remained relatively stable over 
the four semesters. This stability was explained in terms of the adult mature 
learners’ need to express complex ideas despite their limited L2 resources. 
Meanwhile, the specific components of the system (i.e., different types of 
modifiers) exhibited varied group developmental trends.The authors explained 
that features demonstrating an upward trend are associated with greater cognitive 
difficulty (e.g., ADJA involves the rather complex German inflection system), 
while those demonstrating a downward trend are less cognitively difficult 
(e.g., CARD and ADJP are both uninflected). This interpretation aligns with 
the view of the dynamic usage-based approach that development manifests as 
growth of more developmentally advanced features along with the decline of less 
developmentally advanced ones (Verspoor et al.,2012).The authors attributed the 
lack of change in the frequency of adverbs to the learners’ reliance on a few high 
frequency adverbs. They further attributed the mixed picture for prepositional 
phrases to the learners’ reliance on locational and temporal prepositional phrases, 
suggesting that prepositional sentence and nominal modifiers may emerge at a 
later stage. 
The four studies reviewed in this section have made a number of important 
contributions to our understanding of L2 learners’ CAF development, while also 
leaving much room for further research. The development of L2 English writ­
ing syntactic complexity and sophistication featured most prominently in this 
body of research, operationalized using indices gauging complexity at different 
structural levels and usage-based indices of VAC sophistication. Future research 
can use large-scale longitudinal learner corpora to examine the development of 
L2 writing syntactic complexity in languages other than English, the develop­
ment of L2 speaking syntactic complexity, and the development of fluency and 
accuracy in both L2 speaking and writing.The studies reviewed have convinc­
ingly shown the need to control for or model genre and task effects, collect data 
over longer observation periods, and carefully consider the potential effects of the 
instructional foci and the reference corpus used to represent learner input on the 
patterns of development observed.

---

<!-- page 104 -->

 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Second Language Development Trajectories and Influential Factors 87 
4.3 L2 Development of Lexical Knowledge 
In this section, we move on to the body of research that analyzed longitudinal 
learner corpora to specifically examine L2 learners’ lexical development. The 
studies reviewed here were selected to represent research on the development of 
vocabulary breadth and depth knowledge as well as the effects of various input, 
learner, and task variables on this development.We should note that the part of 
Yoon and Polio’s (2017) study on lexical complexity development is relevant to 
this body of research as well.While most studies again focused on L2 English, we 
included one study that focused on L2 French. Several studies were conducted 
by Crossley and colleagues, giving us the added benefit of seeing how the same 
longitudinal learner corpora may be used in multiple studies to replicate or 
validate earlier studies with methodological improvements and to incrementally 
broaden the scope of or enhance the depth of developmental research in the same 
area (in this case L2 lexical development). 
In the first study reviewed, Bulté et al. (2008) explored the lexical proficiency 
development of Dutch-speaking L2 learners of French, as part of a larger research 
project on the development of L2 proficiency in various educational contexts. 
The authors noted that most L2 vocabulary studies up to the time they were 
conducting their research had adopted a synchronic design and that this gap 
necessitated their longitudinal study on lexical proficiency. Their participants 
were 19 Dutch-speaking learners of French as well as 19 L1 French students 
from 10 Dutch-medium secondary schools in Brussels, Belgium, with the L1 
speaker group included for benchmarking purposes. At the time of initial data 
collection, all L2 French learners had experienced at least four years of instruction 
in French as a foreign language (FFL) and different levels of natural exposure to 
French. Each learner performed the frog-story task (retelling a story based on 
a wordless picture book) orally once a year in Years 1 to 3, and each L1 French 
speaker performed the same task once in Year 2, resulting in a total of 76 speeches 
in their corpus. Each recorded oral speech was transcribed in the CHAT format 
so that the transcriptions could be analyzed using the Computerized Language 
Analysis (CLAN) programs (MacWhinney, 2000). The authors lemmatized all 
content words and coded all nouns, verbs, and adjectives for part of speech.They 
then computed 42 quantitative measures of lexical diversity, sophistication, and 
productivity on each transcript but reported results on 22 of those measures in 
this particular study only.The 22 measures fell under three categories. Seven were 
standard measures that distinguished between word types and tokens, i.e., number 
of word types, number of content word types, Uber’s index (Dugast, 1979) for all 
word types and for content word types only, Guiraud’s index (Guiraud, 1960) for 
all word types and for content word types only, and the D measure (Malvern et al., 
2004). Nine were lexical class measures that focused on a specific lexical class, i.e., 
number of tokens in each content word class (i.e., nouns, verbs, and adjectives), 
and Guiraud’s index for each content word class (i.e., nouns, verbs, and adjectives)

---

<!-- page 105 -->

 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
88 Second Language Development Trajectories and Influential Factors 
in two versions – once within the content word class and once across all word 
classes. Finally, there were six frequency measures that relied on three external 
word frequency lists to characterize words as basic or sophisticated, i.e., number 
of words on each list, and the Guiraud’s index for words on each list. 
A series of repeated-measures ANOVAs along with post hoc Bonferroni 
comparisons were conducted to examine how the lexical proficiency of L2 
learners progressed across the three time points. The results showed significant 
increases over time in all but two frequency measures based on one word 
frequency list. Among those 20 measures that increased significantly over time, 
12 grew significantly from Year 1 to Year 2, but only two frequency-based lexical 
sophistication measures continued to grow significantly from Year 2 to Year 3. A 
series of paired-samples t-tests were used to compare the L2 learners’ scores at each 
time point with the scores of the L1 French speakers.The results showed that the 
L1 French speakers outperformed the L2 French learners on all measures and at 
all three testing times, with the exception of two measures of lexical productivity 
and diversity of adjectives. 
Bulté et al. (2008) concluded that the L2 French learners progressed 
significantly in terms of lexical diversity, sophistication, and productivity but that 
this development slowed down after the first year.This was especially the case for 
the development of lexical diversity and productivity.They tentatively attributed 
the lack of continued growth from Year 2 to Year 3 to the methodological 
aspects of their study, i.e., the L2 learners may have “experienced diminished 
task motivation the third time around and invested less effort in creating a 
lexically productive and diverse narrative” (Bulté et al., 2008, p. 293).They further 
concluded that, despite their growth over time, the L2 French learners did not 
obtain the lexical proficiency level of their L1 French peers.The lack of difference 
in adjectival diversity and diversity between the two groups was also attributed to 
the task used, which elicited limited adjectives from both groups of participants. 
Bulté et al. (2008) argued that their findings indicate that there are limits to 
the contributions of both formal/explicit and informal/incidental vocabulary 
learning and that one needs to be “patient, and realistic in what one can be 
expected from L2 vocabulary development in educational contexts” (p. 295). 
Two methodological issues are particularly worth noting in this study. 
The authors’ discussion of the potential task effects on their results invites the 
interesting question of how researchers can best control for task effects on the 
one hand and avoid repetition effects and issues regarding generalizability on the 
other hand in longitudinal research in general. Second, the authors also reported 
very strong correlations among the 22 measures used in their study, suggesting 
that they may tap into similar dimensions of lexical proficiency and therefore 
jeopardizing their construct validity as indicators of different dimensions of lexical 
proficiency.These issues notwithstanding, as one of the earliest longitudinal studies 
of L2 lexical proficiency development on an L2 other than English, the study has 
shed useful light on adolescent L2 French learners’ (significant albeit limited)

---

<!-- page 106 -->

 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Second Language Development Trajectories and Influential Factors 89 
lexical proficiency growth in FFL contexts. While this study differs from Yoon 
and Polio (2017) in several aspects (e.g., secondary-school FFL learners vs. adult 
ESL learners), the longer observation period may have been the crucial factor in 
allowing significant growth in lexical diversity, sophistication, and productivity. 
The measures of lexical proficiency used in Bulté et al.’s (2008) L2 lexical 
development study were fairly broad, in the sense that they do not gauge specific 
properties of the words other than their frequency.The same could be said about 
the lexical complexity measures used in Yoon and Polio’s (2017) study. In the 
second study reviewed in this section, Salsbury et al. (2011) set out to employ 
measures that gauge the lexical psycholinguistic properties of words to investigate 
lexical growth in spontaneous speech data of six adult L2 English learners in a one-
year period.The learner data used in this study was derived from a longitudinal 
corpus of oral language samples produced by ESL learners enrolled in a six-level 
intensive English program at a university in the United States, with most learners 
in the first level of the program.The learners were interviewed by an L1 English 
speaker bi-weekly for a total of 18 sessions (30–45 minutes per session) over a 
year using diverse elicitation materials (e.g., emotion, picture description tasks, 
questions, and topics for discussion) and some free-choice topics generated by 
the learners.The authors used data from a cohort of six ESL learners in this study. 
Each recorded interview for each of the six learners was transcribed and saved 
as a single text file.The six learners produced a total of 99 texts over the year of 
observation, with 13 to 18 texts per learner, as not all learners participated in all 
interviews. 
The authors removed interjections and punctuation marks (with the exception 
of periods and question marks) and checked the files for spelling errors. Each 
file was then automatically analyzed using Coh-Metrix (Graesser et al., 2004) to 
obtain four lexical psycholinguistic scores: Concreteness (how abstract or concrete 
a word is), imageability (how easy can a mental image be constructed for a word), 
familiarity (how familiar a word is to native speakers), and meaningfulness (how 
strongly a word is associated with other words in meaning). For each lexical 
psycholinguistic property, the score of each text was calculated as the average rating 
of the words in the text with available ratings for that property in the Medical 
Research Council (MRC) database (Wilson, 1988), which contains concreteness, 
imageability, familiarity, and meaningfulness ratings of a set of words provided by 
native English speakers.The number of words rated for the four properties in the 
MRC database varied, ranging from 2,627 words for meaningfulness to 4,920 
words for familiarity. Among the 3,690 word types found in the learner corpus, 
the percentage of words with ratings for the four properties ranged from 30.0% 
for familiarity and imageability to 37.9% for meaningfulness. 
The authors conducted a series of repeated-measures ANOVAs using time as 
the independent variable and the four psycholinguistic scores as dependent vari­
ables, along with post hoc comparisons, to see if these scores increased linearly 
over time. For these analyses, the authors used data from Weeks 2, 4, 16, 32, 50,

---

<!-- page 107 -->

 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
90 Second Language Development Trajectories and Influential Factors 
and 52 to capture the early, mid, and late periods of the year of observation and 
accommodate missing data points due to participant absences.The learners’ con­
creteness, imageability, and meaningfulness scores all decreased significantly over 
time.These results showed that as learners spent more time learning English, their 
ability to use more abstract language increased (e.g., using more abstract words 
such as weird and bother), their oral discourse became less dependent on visual 
contexts (e.g., using less imageable words such as control and theory), and they 
employed more specialized words with fewer semantic associations with other 
words (e.g., using more words with lower meaningfulness scores such as link, 
as opposed to words with higher meaningfulness scores such as pet). Familiarity 
scores, however, did not decrease significantly over time.The authors explained 
that the ESL learners in their study were unlikely to use many words unfamiliar to 
native speakers and it may take longer than one year to observe significant changes 
in familiarity scores with L2 learners. 
A notable strength of Salsbury et al.’s (2011) study lies in their employment of 
features pertaining to the lexical psycholinguistic properties of words. Combined 
with a longitudinal approach, these features allowed them to generate valuable 
insights into the development of the depth of L2 learners’ lexical knowledge, 
usefully complementing previous findings on the development of L2 learners’ 
vocabulary breadth knowledge. The authors also performed some analysis to 
examine the potential effect of topic on their findings. For example, they analyzed 
whether abstract topics (e.g., politics, love) elicited more abstract language in the 
early period of their study.Their conclusion was that topic was not a factor in the 
L2 learners’ scores. Nevertheless, they acknowledged that some topics would still 
lend themselves better to different types of language and it would be helpful for 
future research to control for topic more systematically and validate their findings. 
While the first two studies reviewed represent research that integrates learner 
corpus data to examine the longitudinal development of breadth and depth of L2 
productive lexical knowledge, the third study reviewed in this section is chosen to 
illustrate the value of replication research that builds upon and validates previous 
findings pertaining to L2 lexical development. Crossley and Skalicky (2019) 
pointed out several limitations of Salsbury et al.’s (2011) study, including the 
small sample size (six learners), the lack of control for some potentially important 
learner variables (e.g., age, proficiency, and gender), and the absence of attention 
to individual participant variation. In light of these limitations, they conducted an 
approximate replication of Salsbury et al.’s (2011) study to ascertain whether their 
findings could be generalized to a new population in a new setting (Porte, 2012). 
The data used in their replication study consisted of transcripts of naturalistic 
conversations between 50 ESL learners attending an intensive English program 
(non-matriculated) and 50 students (25 L1 English speakers and 25 L2 English 
speakers) enrolled in undergraduate or graduate TESOL courses (matriculated) 
at a university in the US. Conversations between one non-matriculated ESL 
learner and one matriculated student took place once a month every four months

---

<!-- page 108 -->

 
 
 
 
 
  
  
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Second Language Development Trajectories and Influential Factors 91 
and lasted about 30 minutes each time. A portion of each conversation was 
transcribed, and the transcript was then split into two files, each representing 
the oral production of one of the two interlocutors.The analysis focused on the 
oral production of the 50 non-matriculated ESL learners only. The number of 
learners analyzed was over eight times more than the six participants analyzed 
by Salsbury et al. (2011). In terms of learner variables, the authors considered 
gender (35 male vs. 15 female), age (mean=26.1, SD=8.26, range =18 to 55), 
language distance score (1 to 3 based on the distance of the learner’s L1 to English; 
six L1 backgrounds represented), and proficiency level (high beginning, low 
intermediate, high intermediate, or advanced based on the learner’s score on an 
ACT ESL Compass test).The inclusion of these variables was informed by studies 
that reported positive correlations between these variables and lexical proficiency 
development (e.g., Birdsong & Molis, 2001; DeKeyser, 2000; Roquet et al., 2016; 
Schepens et al., 2013; Sunderland, 2000;Van der Slik, 2010). The authors used 
the Tool for the Automatic Analysis of Lexical Sophistication (TAALES; Kyle 
& Crossley, 2015) to obtain the same four features of lexical psycholinguistic 
properties (i.e., concreteness, imageability, familiarity, and meaningfulness) used by 
Salsbury et al. (2011) for each text. 
To examine linear growth trends, the authors constructed a separate LME 
model for each lexical feature in R using the lme4 package (Bates et al., 2015), 
with the lexical feature as the dependent variable and the following variables as 
fixed effects: Gender, age, language proficiency, interlocutor match (whether the 
ESL learner talked to an L1 or L2 English speaker), session order, and language 
distance. In addition, the authors entered participants as random effects and 
session order as a random slope for participants to capture individual variation. 
Interactions between session order and proficiency and session order and 
language distance were retained in the models if significant. The results of the 
study revealed a significant main effect for session order in the LME models 
predicting concreteness, meaningfulness, and imageability, but not in the LME 
model predicting familiarity. Additionally, the results indicated that proficiency 
significantly predicted concreteness, imageability, and meaningfulness scores, with 
higher level learners producing less concrete, imageable, and meaningful words 
than lower level learners.Age, gender, and language distance did not significantly 
predict any of the four scores, which was somewhat surprising as previous studies 
reported language advantages for younger learners (DeKeyser, 2000; Birdsong 
& Molis, 2001), female learners (Sunderland, 2000; Roquet et al., 2016), and 
learners with an L1 closer to the L2 (Schepens et al., 2013;Van der Slik, 2010). No 
interaction effect was found in any of the LME models. 
Crossley and Skalicky’s (2019) study offers robust support for the findings 
reported by Salsbury et al.’s (2011) pertaining to L2 learners’ longitudinal growth 
of lexical psycholinguistic features. Specifically, L2 English learners used less con­
crete, imageable, and meaningful words as they spent more time learning English, 
regardless of their age, gender, and L1 background. Such lexical acquisition

---

<!-- page 109 -->

 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
92 Second Language Development Trajectories and Influential Factors 
occurred within an even shorter period of observation than in Salsbury et al.’s 
(2011) study (four months vs. one year). As one of the first replication stud­
ies in the domain of L2 lexical development research, this study exemplifies the 
value of such studies in validating previous research findings with methodologi­
cal enhancements. The findings of the study also suggest a potentially interest­
ing tradeoff between sample size and observation period in longitudinal studies, 
which deserves further exploration. 
The next study reviewed differs from the studies examined above in that it 
explored the effect of an important input factor, i.e., absolute word frequency, 
on L2 lexical output over time. Using the longitudinal data from the same six 
ESL learners examined by Salsbury et al. (2011) as L2 output data and the speech 
produced by the 13 L1 English interviewers as L1 input data, Crossley et al. (2014) 
examined this effect from a usage-based perspective. The 13 interviewers were 
all recruited from a graduate-level SLA course, and each ESL learner interacted 
with three or more interviewers during the one-year period. Only 89 of the 
99 transcripts of learner production were used, as the other ten contained data 
that were not characteristic of free conversation (e.g., picture description), along 
with 89 L1 English speaker transcripts that matched those learner transcripts.The 
authors computed four word frequency indices for each transcript.Two indices 
based on Baayen et al.’s (1995) CELEX database were computed from Coh-
Metrix (Graesser et al., 2004), i.e., mean logarithm (base 10) frequency for all 
words in the text found in the CELEX database, and mean logarithm frequency 
for all words in the text found in the spoken component of the CELEX database. 
The other two were computed from Vocabprofiler (Cobb, 2002) based on the 
BNC, i.e., percentage of level one words (i.e., words among the top 1,000 most 
frequent words in the BNC) and percentage of words beyond the second band 
(i.e., words beyond the first 2,000 most frequent words in the BNC). 
An analysis of the 50 most common words used by both L1 and L2 speakers 
in Weeks 1, 27, and 50 of the observation period showed that the type-token 
frequency distribution of those words for each week was Zipfian in the L1 input 
but not in the L2 output. In addition, the rank order of those 50 words in the L1 
input and L2 output was dissimilar and showed only weak to moderate correlations 
(.285 to .495) for the three time points.The authors noted that the L1 and L2 
speakers used first-person personal pronouns (e.g., I) and question forms (e.g., 
what) quite differently.A second analysis of the 50 most commonly shared words 
excluding those forms yielded largely comparable results as the first analysis. 
Using linear curve estimates, the authors found significant negative linear trends 
for the L1 input but significant positive linear trends for the L2 output over time 
for the two CELEX indices.These results indicated that, as the ESL learners spent 
more time learning English, the L1 interviewers likely modified the frequency of 
their words (i.e., they used more low frequency words), but the learners produced 
more high frequency words instead. Among the four word frequency indices, 
only percentage of words beyond the second band exhibited a significant, weak

---

<!-- page 110 -->

 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Second Language Development Trajectories and Influential Factors 93 
correlation in the L1 input and L2 output (r=.245), confirming at best a weak 
relationship between the frequency scores of the words produced by the L1 and 
L2 speakers.An analysis of word types showed that both L1 interviewers and L2 
learners produced more types with lower frequency over time; however, the ESL 
learners repeated infrequent word types less often but held their rate of repetition 
of frequent words over time, while the L1 interviewers repeated both infrequent 
and frequent words less over time. 
In sum, Crossley et al. (2014) found that for their ESL learners, the frequency 
values of the words in the L1 input were not correlated to those in the L2 output, 
“indicating that the L2 learners in this study did not produce frequent words at 
a similar rate as their interlocutors” (p. 325).The authors argued that this finding 
did not necessarily contradict the frequency effect of language learning posited 
in UBL in general, but demonstrated that absolute word frequency in the input 
alone could not explain the production of individual words in L2 output, which 
may be affected by other factors such as phonological salience and cross-linguistic 
influence. 
In the final study reviewed in this section, we discuss how Crossley et al. 
(2019) re-examined the developmental patterns in L2 learners’ word frequency 
production using the same dataset from Crossley and Skalicky (2019). Taking 
advantage of the variables coded in the dataset, the authors also examined the 
relationships between word frequency production and four learner variables 
(i.e., L1 background, age, gender, and proficiency level) as well as the potential 
interactions between word frequency production and proficiency level. The 
authors used TAALES (Kyle & Crossley, 2015) to obtain word type and word 
token frequency features based on the spoken section of COCA (Davies, 2009), 
representing L1 English norms, and the TOEFL11 corpus (Blanchard et al., 2013), 
a corpus of 11,000 TOEFL iBT essays representing L2 English norms.Word type 
and token frequency scores based on COCA were computed by dividing the sum 
of the logarithmically transformed frequency scores for all types (or tokens) in a 
text by the number of types (or tokens) in the text found in the COCA spoken 
section. Word type and token frequency scores based on the TOEFL11 corpus 
were computed in the same way after excluding tokens with a frequency of one 
to prevent neologisms and misspellings. 
Similar to Crossley and Skalicky (2019), the authors constructed a separate 
LME model for each of the four word frequency features with the frequency 
measure as the dependent variable and the same independent variables (i.e., 
gender, age, language proficiency, interlocutor match, session order, and language 
distance), random effects (i.e., participants), and random slope (i.e., session order). 
For COCA spoken token frequency, age and proficiency exhibited significant 
negative main effects while language distance exhibited a significant positive main 
effect; session order did not exhibit a significant main effect but a significant 
interaction effect with proficiency. In particular, high intermediate learners’ 
COCA spoken token frequency was significantly lower than advanced learners

---

<!-- page 111 -->

 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
94 Second Language Development Trajectories and Influential Factors 
in earlier sessions but increased significantly over time. For TOEFL11 token 
frequency, proficiency and language distance exhibited significant positive main 
effects, but session order did not exhibit a significant main effect or interaction 
effect. For COCA spoken type frequency and TOEFL11 type frequency, no 
significant main effects or interactions emerged. Post hoc analyses examining 
content and function words separately for the COCA spoken token and type 
indices yielded a significant main effect for age for content word tokens and a 
significant main effect for language distance for function word tokens only, with 
no other main effects or interactions. 
Similar to Crossley et al. (2014), Crossley et al. (2019) summarized that their 
findings demonstrated a trend for L2 learners to produce more frequent words 
as they spend more time learning English, indicating that “frequency effects are 
not strong predictors of acquisition for individual linguistic items like words” 
(p. 740) and that other usage-based factors such as salience likely play a critical 
role. Essentially, this study reuses the longitudinal learner corpus and much of 
the same statistical procedures from a prior study (Crossley & Skalicky, 2019) to 
re-examine the effect of frequency on L2 lexical output over time explored in 
another prior study (Crosley et al., 2014) from a different perspective, i.e., using 
frequency norms derived from external L1 and L2 reference corpora instead of 
those derived directly from the input provided by L1 interlocutors. In addition, 
the study also expanded the scope of research on the developmental patterns of 
L2 learners’ word frequency production by considering the effects of multiple 
learner variables.As we mentioned at the beginning of this section, this collection 
of studies exemplifies an efficient and productive way to validate, enhance, and 
expand relevant previous research on L2 lexical development. 
Taken together, the five studies reviewed in this section have shown how the 
breadth and depth of L2 learners’ productive lexical knowledge may develop over 
time and how this development may be affected by various input,learner,and task 
variables.All five studies employed a combination of longitudinal learner corpus 
data, carefully selected quantitative lexical features, efficient automated tools for 
lexical analysis, and statistical analytical procedures appropriate for the research 
questions to reveal trajectories of L2 learners’ lexical development. Several of 
these studies also usefully built on and complemented each other to provide more 
robust evidence for the developmental patterns and effects observed.A number of 
issues and potential directions for future research are worth mentioning. First, the 
set of lexical proficiency features can be further enriched. For example, existing 
features have generally not accounted for the fact that polysemous words are 
used with different senses in different contexts and that it may be useful to model 
word sense diversity and sophistication (e.g., Lu & Hu, 2022). Second, assessing 
a particular lexical psycholinguistic property in L2 production by computing 
the mean ratings for that property of the words in L2 production assumes that 
learners at different proficiency levels perceive the degree of that property of the 
same words in the same way. In reality, however, they may perceive the properties

---

<!-- page 112 -->

 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Second Language Development Trajectories and Influential Factors 95 
of the same words quite differently and/or use them in very different ways in 
different contexts, partly because many words have many different meanings and 
uses. Recent research has thus started to experiment with dynamic approaches to 
estimating the lexical psycholinguistic properties of L2 production (e.g., Sun & 
Lu, 2021).Third, while the second and third studies showed development in the 
depth of L2 learners’ lexical knowledge in one year and four months, respectively, 
the first study showed that the development of the breadth of L2 learners’ lexical 
knowledge slowed down after the first year. It would be useful for future research 
to use longer periods of observation to examine longer-term development of the 
depth of L2 learners’ lexical knowledge. Finally, future research in this area should 
carefully consider the potential effect of topic on the findings, an issue brought up 
in multiple studies reviewed, and how this factor can be best treated. For example, 
it may be helpful to identify a set of topics that do not elicit significantly different 
types of language and use such a set of topics to replicate the first study to validate 
whether the stalled growth in the second year resulted from the use of the same 
topic throughout the study. 
4.4 L2 Phraseological Development 
In this section, we move on to studies that integrated longitudinal corpus analysis 
to investigate the trajectories of L2 learners’ phraseological development.These 
studies have generally taken a quantitative approach, with notable variation in 
the types of phraseological unit examined and the quantitative measures used 
to measure L2 learners’ phraseological competence. In the first study reviewed, 
Crossley and Salsbury (2011) examined the development of lexical bundle 
accuracy of the same six adult ESL learners analyzed in Salsbury et al.’s (2011) 
study discussed in Section 4.3.They understood lexical bundles as phraseological 
or multi-word lexical units (McCarthy, 2006) and focused specifically on bigrams 
to bypass the data sparseness problem.The learner corpus they employed consisted 
of the same 99 transcripts used by Salsbury et al. (2011).To prepare the transcripts 
for lexical bundle analysis, the authors converted contractions and phonological 
blends to their full forms (e.g., I’m to I am, gonna to going to).The learner corpus 
was compared against the Santa Barbara Corpus of Spoken American English (Du 
Bois et al., 2000–2005), a corpus consisting of 200,000 tokens of natural speech 
produced by L1 American English speakers in casual conversation in natural 
settings. 
Two types of analyses were conducted on the data.The first analysis was based 
on the authors’ argument that growth in L2 learners’ lexical bundle accuracy 
should be reflected in the extent to which their lexical bundle production 
approaches that of L1 English speakers, in terms of both the types of bundles 
produced and the frequency of those bundles. For each quarter of the 12-month 
learning period, the authors identified the bigrams with a minimum frequency of 
two that were shared between the learner corpus and the L1 English corpus and

---

<!-- page 113 -->

 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
96 Second Language Development Trajectories and Influential Factors 
correlated the normalized frequency counts (per 1,000 words) of those bigrams 
in the learner corpus with those in the L1 English corpus. This was done to 
see whether there was growth in accuracy across the four quarters.The authors 
also examined the difference between the first and fourth quarter correlations, 
calculated using z-score conversion, to see if any significant growth took place. 
The same analyses were also performed for each individual learner.At the group 
level, the correlation increased linearly from .60 in the first quarter to .81 in 
the fourth quarter, and the difference between the first and fourth quarter 
correlations was statistically significant, indicating that the learners’ lexical bundle 
accuracy increased as they spent more time learning English. At the individual 
level, four learners exhibited growth in lexical bundle accuracy over time, among 
whom three showed significant growth between the first and fourth quarters. 
The TOEFL scores of these four individuals were also significantly correlated to 
their time spent studying English.Two learners did not show growth over time, 
for whom the authors found no significant correlation between their TOEFL 
scores and time spent studying English, indicating that their lack of growth in 
lexical bundle accuracy could be attributed to their lack of growth in language 
proficiency in general. 
In the second analysis, the authors qualitatively examined the bigrams produced 
by two or more L2 learners that were shared with L1 speakers in the first and fourth 
quarters.These bigrams were categorized based on their pragmatic and syntactic 
functions.The authors found that the L2 learners produced bigrams with several 
functions that distinguish L1 English spoken and written texts, including filler 
phrases (e.g., I mean, kind of), expressions of opinion (e.g., I think, I like), question 
bigrams (e.g., What is, What I), and bigrams containing coordinating conjunctions 
(e.g., and I, so I). In addition, they also produced bigrams for expressing self (e.g., 
I am, I want) or discussing possibilities (e.g., maybe I, you can). For most categories, 
the frequency of the bigrams produced by L2 learners was either on par with 
or trended over time toward that of L1 speakers, with the exception that the 
frequency of L2 learners’ production of self-expression bigrams increased over 
time in a trend opposite of L1 speakers. 
A particularly interesting theoretical contribution of the study is the 
conceptualization of L2 learners’ lexical bundle accuracy in terms of the 
frequency of the lexical bundles in L1 usage. This conceptualization links to 
the notion of frequency effects in L2 learning in general (see Chapter 3) and 
that of phraseological learning as resulting from the “graduate strengthening of 
association between co-occurring” words in particular (Ellis, 2002, p. 173).The 
authors’analysis of the frequency and functions of the bigrams shared between the 
learner and L1 corpora revealed a developmental trajectory of the learners’ lexical 
bundle accuracy characterized by growth in form, frequency, and function toward 
L1 usage.These findings provide evidence for the author’s conceptualization and 
has important implications for implicit learning. Meanwhile, given the small 
number of focal learners and the narrow focus on bigrams only, it would be

---

<!-- page 114 -->

 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
   
   
  
   
  
   
  
     
  
 
   
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Second Language Development Trajectories and Influential Factors 97 
highly useful to see how the findings can generalize to larger samples and longer 
lexical bundles. 
While Crossley and Salsbury (2011) used reference-corpus frequency of 
bigrams as a measure of L2 learners’ lexical bundle accuracy, Bestgen and Granger 
(2014) proposed the use of the reference-corpus association strength of contigu­
ous bigrams, measured using mutual information (MI) and t-score, to quantify the 
development of L2 learners’ phraseological competence.They termed this method 
the “CollGram method”. Bestgen and Granger (2018) used this method to ana­
lyze a section of the Longitudinal Database of Learner English (LONGDALE) 
consisting of 178 argumentative essays (slightly over 100,000 words) written by 
89 L1 French EFL learners at the University of Louvain, 89 written in their 
first year (T1) and 89 in their third year (T3), to track the learners’ phraseo­
logical development over time. Each text was first orthographically corrected and 
then POS-tagged using the CLAWS7 tagger.The authors extracted all bigrams 
as well as bigrams of the following three POS categories: Noun-noun (NN), 
adjective-noun (JN), and adverb-adjective (AJ) from the T1 and T3 corpora. 
They then computed the mutual information (MI) score and t-score for each 
of those bigrams that occurred five or more times in the BNC. The bigrams 
extracted from the learner corpus were then classified as below-threshold (fre­
quency<5),non-collocational (MI<3;t-score<2),collocational-low (3≤ MI<5; 
2≤ t-score<6), collocational-medium (5 ≤ MI<7; 6≤ t-score<10), and colloca­
tional-high (MI≥ 7; t-score ≥ 10). Finally, the percentage of bigrams in each of the 
five categories was calculated separately for the two association strength measures. 
These percentages were obtained for each individual text and calculated for each 
bigram category (i.e., NN, JN,AJ, and all). 
Using randomization tests for matched samples (an equivalent of Student’s 
t-tests that do not assume data normality), the authors identified a number of 
significant changes from T1 to T3. 
Considering all bigram POS categories together yielded the largest number 
of significant changes: Non-collocational bigram types and tokens were consist­
ently fewer at T3 for both association strength measures, while high-MI and high 
t-score bigram types and tokens were all consistently higher at T3.The results for 
the three specific POS-bigram categories differed. NN bigrams were used the 
least frequently at both time points and showed no significant change from T1 
to T3. JN and AJ bigrams showed similar results to those observed for all bigram 
POS categories, with the exception of the percentage of high-MI AJ bigrams, 
which was significantly lower at T3 than T1.The authors noted that this excep­
tion aligned with previous findings that lower-proficiency learners tended to use 
more adjective intensifiers such as most (e.g., most important) (Lorenz, 1998). 
Overall, the results of this study showed that over the three-year period, the 
L2 learners produced fewer non-collocational bigrams and more bigrams that 
were lower in frequency but higher in association strength. Given the heavy 
methodological focus of the study, the authors did not explicitly explain these

---

<!-- page 115 -->

 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
98 Second Language Development Trajectories and Influential Factors 
findings from a particular perspective. Nonetheless, the findings align with the 
UBL hypotheses pertaining to the effects of frequency and contingency discussed 
in Chapter 3.The authors argued that the CollGram method presented a fine-
grained picture of the development of L2 phraseological competence by capturing 
the degrees of appropriateness of the bigrams produced by L2 learners rather 
than categorizing such bigrams as either correct or erroneous. Meanwhile, they 
pointed out two caveats that are worth noting.The first had to do with the density 
of the data collection: The study collected data from two data points only and 
thus fell short of revealing the full developmental trajectory of the L2 learners’ 
phraseological competence.They also pointed out that topic would likely affect 
the learners’ lexical usage and it was not feasible to ask learners to repeatedly 
write on the same topic.The second caveat had to do with the lack of attention 
to individual variability. 
Bestgen and Granger’s (2018) argument that phraseological competence is 
better assessed in terms of appropriateness using association strength measures 
than in terms of absolute correctness judgments was echoed in a study by 
Yoon (2016), who examined the development of a group of ESL learners’ 
phraseological competence in terms of changes in the association strength of 
verb-noun combinations in their writing over a semester. He compiled a short-
term longitudinal learner corpus with data from 51 adult ESL learners enrolled 
in an intensive English program at a university in the US over one 15-week­
long semester. The learners were considered to be at the high-intermediate 
level as they were placed in the two highest levels of the six-level program.The 
learners received six to eight hours of instruction on English writing per week. 
Each ESL learner wrote six essays over one semester, with two- to three-week 
intervals.The essays written at the first, third, and fifth time points were narratives, 
while those written at the other three time points were argumentative essays. 
The author also compiled an L1 English comparison corpus by recruiting 46 L1 
English undergraduate students at the same university.The L1 English speakers 
each wrote one narrative and one argumentative essay. Each text was cleaned of 
spelling errors, POS-tagged with the TreeTagger (Schmid, 1994), and manually 
checked for tagging accuracy.The verb-noun combinations were then extracted 
from the texts by manually examining the noun lemmas occurring before and 
after verb lemmas in the concordance lines using AntConc (Anthony, 2014).After 
removing verb-noun combinations found in the prompts (e.g., carry laptop), the 
author identified a total of 6,457 combinations (e.g., make research, spend time). The 
frequency and MI scores of each combination were obtained from COCA. 
To assess the development of collocational strength in the ESL learners’ 
writing over time, the author performed repeated-measures ANOVAs for each 
genre separately. The results showed no significant change in the MI scores of 
the combination types and tokens over time for both genres.The author further 
compared the MI scores of the combinations in the last narrative and argumentative 
essays produced by the ESL learners with those produced by the L1 English

---

<!-- page 116 -->

 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
  
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
Second Language Development Trajectories and Influential Factors 99 
speakers. Between-subjects ANOVAs revealed significantly higher MI scores in 
the argumentative essays produced by the L1 English speakers and comparable 
MI scores in the narrative essays produced by the ESL learners and L1 English 
speakers. A close analysis of the top 20 most frequent combinations produced 
by the two groups in the two genres of writing suggested that the L1 English 
speakers used fewer general verbs (e.g., have, make, take, do) in argumentative essays 
than in narrative essays, while the ESL learners used such verbs with comparable 
frequency in both genres of writing. Further analysis of the high-MI combinations 
produced by the two groups indicated that the L1 English speakers used more 
infrequent collocations characteristic of academic writing (e.g., address dilemma, 
cause misunderstanding). 
The author offered two possible reasons for the absence of change in association 
strength over one semester. First, one semester may be an insufficient period of 
time for investigating longitudinal development in phraseological competence. 
Second, the length of the texts analyzed may be too short for significant changes 
to emerge.The significant changes in association strength over a three-year period 
reported by Bestgen and Granger (2018) lend support to the first explanation. 
The author further argued that the ESL learners likely had attained different levels 
of phraseological competence across the two genres and were more capable of 
manipulating genre-appropriate combinations in the narrative genre than in the 
argumentative genre. Methodologically, the author highlighted the same notion 
of Bestgen and Granger (2018) that association strength measures constitute a 
useful way to measure phraseological accuracy as a gradual degree to which the 
learners’ phraseological usage in specific contexts resembles that of L1 speakers. 
In the final study reviewed in this section, Siyanova-Chanturia and Spina (2020) 
represented a longitudinal learner corpus study of L2 Italian learners’ development 
of a particular type of phrasal vocabulary, namely, Noun+Adjective (N+Adj) 
combinations, in L2 writing over a six-month period.This study filled several gaps 
in the learner corpus literature pertaining to phrasal vocabulary development, 
including in particular the use of a large-scale longitudinal corpus and the focus 
on an understudied L2 (i.e., L2 Italian) and learners at lower proficiency levels. 
The data used in this study came from the Longitudinal Corpus of Chinese 
Learners of Italian (LCCLI; Spina & Siyanova-Chanturia, 2018), comprised of 
350 essays written by 175 adult Chinese learners of Italian (about 97,000 words 
total). Each learner wrote two essays, one at the beginning and one at the end 
of a six-month full-time intensive course of Italian at a university in Italy. The 
learners’ proficiency levels were characterized as A1 or beginner (n=39), A2 or 
elementary (n=86), and B1 or intermediate (n =50) based on the CEFR.The 
learners were offered three comparable essay topics (i.e., “My first impression 
of Italy and Italians”,“My hobbies:What do I usually do in my free time?”, and 
“My last holidays”) and were instructed to write on two different topics. This 
approach to control for topic and repetition effect is advisable, as we discussed in 
Section 4.3. The corpus was POS-tagged using the TreeTagger (Schmid, 1994)

---

<!-- page 117 -->

 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
˘
100 Second Language Development Trajectories and Influential Factors 
followed by a semiautomatic post-tagging revision procedure.The authors’ focus 
on N+Adj combinations was motivated by the challenge such combinations pose 
to L2 Italian learners, as one or more adjectives may either precede or follow a 
noun in Italian, with adjectival position determined by syntactic and semantic 
constraints (Spina, 2019). The authors extracted 1,550 N+Adj combinations 
from the corpus by simply searching for nouns followed by an adjective. They 
removed 49 combinations found in the essay topics and retained all other 1,401 
combinations for further analysis. 
The authors employed five measures, each of which captures a unique aspect 
of the L2 learners’ development of lexical combinations: Phrasal frequency was 
used to account for overall word combination repetition; MI was used to measure 
the association strength between the constituent words in the combination; Delta 
P
