Similar to Crossley and Skalicky (2019), the authors built five different LME 
models using R to investigate whether and the extent to which each of the five 
measures changed as a function of time along with other variables. In each model, 
the dependent variable was the feature in question, the fixed effects included time, 
proficiency, topic, the frequency and length of the noun, the frequency and length 
of the adjective, and lexical diversity calculated using the Guiraud index (i.e., the 
number of word types divided by the square root of the number of tokens).The 
authors also entered learners nested within class as random effects and time as a 
random slop for learners to capture individual variation. Particularly relevant to 
our focus on L2 phraseological development in this section, the results showed 
that time significantly negatively affected four of the five features (i.e., all but MI). 
In addition, time also interacted with proficiency significantly for frequency and 
both delta P features:The negative effect of time was stronger for A1 learners and 
weaker for A2 learners, and it actually turned positive for B1 learners in the case 
of delta P 
. Several other predictors were found to significantly affect some
backward 
or all of the five features, including topic, noun frequency, adjective frequency, 
noun length, and adjective length, and significant interaction effects on several 
features were found between noun and adjective frequency and length as well.

---

<!-- page 118 -->

 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
Second Language Development Trajectories and Influential Factors 101 
Little individual variance in how the 175 learners within the 17 different classes 
produced N+Adj combinations emerged from the analysis of the random effects 
of the five models. Overall, the fixed and random effects included in the models 
predicted phrase frequency the best, explaining 21% of the variance. 
These results show that after six months, the L2 Italian learners produced 
more lower frequency combinations and combinations whose constituent words 
were less “strongly associated, mutually attracted, or predictive of each other” 
(Siyanova-Chanturia & Spina, 2020, pp. 446–447). Meanwhile, time affected A1, 
A2, and B1 learners differently. In particular, the decrease in the use of frequently 
and mutually attracted N+Adj combinations was the strongest for A1 learners, 
indicating that these learners were the most likely to experiment with new, 
unusual combinations as they spent more time learning Italian. These results 
led the authors to conclude that the learning of such combinations is a process 
“fraught with difficulties” in which “more exposure and higher proficiency may 
not necessarily lead to a more idiomatic and target like output and may, indeed, 
result in lower levels of idiomaticity and greater reliance on lower frequency 
combinations” (p. 452). 
A close look at the differences and commonalities in the research design, 
analytical methods, and findings of the studies discussed in this section reveals 
several noteworthy issues and potential trends. First, all researchers called for 
the use of larger-scale longitudinal learner corpora with denser data collection 
over longer time periods in order to allow for phraseological growth to fully 
manifest itself. Topic and repetition effects need to be carefully controlled in 
building such corpora in pedagogical contexts. Second, the interaction of time 
with other variables (e.g., genre and learner proficiency, among others) as well 
as the potential variation among individual learners need to be considered in 
delineating the trajectory of L2 learners’ phraseological development, and mixed 
effects models have been recognized as particularly effective for modeling such 
interaction effects and variation. Third, phraseological competence needs to 
be conceptualized as a multidimensional construct and operationalized using 
diverse features that tap into different dimensions in order to fully understand L2 
learners’ development of this competence. Finally, the different results reported 
for L2 English and L2 Italian learners by the studies reviewed in this section 
highlight the importance to examine phraseological development of L2 learners 
of languages other than English. 
4.5 L2 Development of Grammatical and 
Morphosyntactic Constructions 
In an effort to test Goldberg’s (2006) proposal that the Zipfian frequency 
distributions of the types and tokens of constructions (Zipf, 1935) are optimal 
for language learning by providing high-frequency, semantically prototypical 
exemplars, Ellis and Ferreira-Junior (2009a) investigated the effects of construction

---

<!-- page 119 -->

 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
102 Second Language Development Trajectories and Influential Factors 
frequency, form, function, and prototypicality on L2 learners’ development of 
the English verb-argument constructions. The authors sourced their data from 
the European Science Foundation (ESF) project, which collected spontaneous 
speech data in five target L2s from adult immigrants with six different L1s residing 
in five different European countries every four to six weeks over a roughly 
30-month period.The corpus is available in the CHAT format in the TalkBank 
(MacWhinney, 2000, 2007). The specific dataset used in their analysis included 
234 sessions between seven ESL learners (four L1 Italian and three L1 Punjabi) 
living in Britain and their L1 English conversation partners.These sessions involved 
a range of activities, such as free conversation, interviews, role-play, and picture 
description, among others, and the data produced by the L1 English speakers was 
used to represent naturalistic input for the ESL learners. 
The authors used various CLAN tools to separate utterances produced by 
the ESL learners and L1 English speakers and to perform a rough POS tagging 
to identify potential verbs in those utterances. One researcher then analyzed 
the 405 potential verb forms to identify utterances containing examples of the 
following three types of VACs, i.e., verb locative (VL, e.g., come out of my house), 
verb object locative (VOL, e.g., put it inside the cup), and ditransitive (VOO, e.g., 
give me some money) constructions.The identified constructions were checked for 
accuracy by an L1 English coder. Altogether, the L1 English speakers produced 
900 verb tokens (33 types) in VL, 303 tokens (33 types) in VOL, and 139 tokens (12 
types) in VOO constructions; the ESL learners produced 436 tokens (39 types) in 
VL, 224 tokens (24 types) in VOL, and 36 tokens (9 types) in VOO constructions. 
The authors found that for each of the three VACs, one exemplar accounted 
for a substantial proportion of the tokens produced by L1 English speakers (i.e., go 
for 42% of VL tokens,put for 35% of VOL tokens, and give for 53% of VOO tokens) 
and an even greater proportion of the tokens produced by ESL learners (go for 
53% of VL tokens, put for 68% of VOL tokens, and give for 64% of VOO tokens). 
These results confirmed that the type-token distributions of the constructions 
produced by L1 English speakers and ESL learners were both Zipfian. Notably, 
for both L1 English speakers and ESL learners, the fewer types there were in a 
construction, the greater the proportion of tokens were accounted for by the 
exemplar type, indicating “the generalized implications of Zipf ’s law for learning” 
(Ellis & Ferreira-Junior, 2009a, p. 373). 
The rank order of verb types in the three constructions was largely comparable 
in the learner data and the L1 English speaker data; the frequency of lemma 
use by the learners also correlated significantly and strongly with that by the L1 
English speakers for all three constructions (.97 for VL, .89 for VOL, and .93 for 
VOO constructions, p <.001).These results were taken to indicate that learner use 
matched the relative input frequencies.An analysis of the cumulative frequencies 
of different verb types as a function of learning time (in months) showed learner 
progression from few verb types in each construction in the early months to 
more types later on. Notably, one exemplar seeded each construction and led its

---

<!-- page 120 -->

 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Second Language Development Trajectories and Influential Factors 103 
development (go for VL, put for VOL, and give for VOO).This result was confirmed 
by analyses of variance conducted for all three constructions, which revealed verb 
type use as a significant factor both by subjects and by time. For example, in VOO, 
give was used significantly more frequently than all other verbs for all learners and 
in all four quarters of the learning period.An analysis of the ESL learners’ uses of 
the most frequent verb type in each construction showed that 17 out of 233 go 
tokens in the VL, 8 out of 152 put tokens in VOL immediately followed from L1 
speaker uses, indicating that interactional priming (see Section 3.4) accounted for 
some but not the majority of learner uses. 
The authors also obtained prototypicality ratings of the 80 verb types that were 
used in the constructions in the dataset from five L1 English-speaking volunteers. 
The volunteers were given a set of instructions describing the core meanings of 
the three VACs and asked to rate each verb in terms of the degree to which it fol­
lowed the core meaning of each VAC on a nine-point scale. Interrater reliabilities 
assessed using Cronbach’s alpha ranged from .86 to .91 for the three constructions. 
A significant positive association was found between the prototypicality rating of 
a verb and the log frequency of learner use (.44 for VL, .29 for VOL, and .34 for 
VOO; p <.001). However, it was not the case that the most prototypical verb in a 
construction was also the most frequently used. For example, go was the most used 
in VL with a fairly high prototypicality rating of 7.4, but ten other verbs used in 
VL had higher prototypicality ratings, i.e., walk, move, run, travel, come, drive, arrive, 
jump, return, and fall.The authors argued that these verbs had higher prototypical 
ratings because they are less generic and fit the change of location schema well, 
but they were lower in frequency because their specific semantic requirements 
limit their general use. As such, these results confirm that the frequency of the 
verbs used by the ESL learners in different VACs is associated with the prototypi­
cality of VAC meaning but that this association is moderated by the generality or 
specificity of the semantics of the verbs. 
In a subsequent study, Ellis and Ferreira-Junior (2009b) used the same dataset 
to examine the effect of contingency on the ESL learners’ acquisition of the 
verb islands in the VACs.The authors measured contingency using a distinctive 
collexme analysis (Gries & Stefanowitsch, 2004; see discussion in Section 3.4), 
delta P VAC to verb, and delta P verb to VAC.The results showed that, for each 
of the three constructions, the frequency of verb use by the learners correlated 
strongly with collexeme strength (.96 for VL, .97 for VOL and VOO) and delta P 
VAC to verb (.95 for VL, .89 for VOL, .93 for VOO) and to a lesser extent delta P 
verb to VAC (.26 for VL, .18 for VOL, and .75 for VOO).These results were taken 
to support the hypothesis that learners acquire verbs that are more distinctively 
associated with each construction in the input earlier. 
Ellis and Ferreira-Junior (2009b) further extended the same analyses performed 
on verb islands of the VACs to examine the effects of frequency, frequency 
distribution, prototypicality, and contingency on the ESL learners’ acquisition 
of non-verb islands in the VL (i.e., Subject, Preposition, Locative), VOL (i.e.,

---

<!-- page 121 -->

 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
104 Second Language Development Trajectories and Influential Factors 
Subject, Object, Preposition, Locative), and VOO (i.e., Subject, Object 1, Object 
2) constructions. Many of the findings pertaining to the verb islands in each VAC 
were replicated for the non-verb islands.The frequency distribution of the types 
occupying all non-verb islands was also Zipfian both in the input and in learner 
production. The learners learned more frequent types in each island in each 
construction earlier; no single exemplar dominated any island, but there was high 
overlap between the top five to ten types in the input and learner production. 
As was the case with verb islands, the types in each non-verb island in each 
construction acquired earlier by the learners were those that were prototypical and 
generic.With the exception of the subject slot, which was frequently occupied by 
pronouns, the first-learned types in each non-verb VAC island also tended to be 
more distinctively associated with that construction. 
The two studies by Ellis and Ferreira-Junior (2009a, b) offered valuable 
insights into the effects of frequency, frequency distribution, prototypicality, 
semantic generality, and contingency on L2 learners’ development of VACs.The 
conclusion drawn from their findings is that L2 learners’ development of VACs 
is optimized by the Zipfian type-token frequency distributions of the occupants 
of these VAC islands, with more generic exemplars that are more frequent in, 
prototypical for, and distinctively associated with a construction generally 
acquired earlier. Meanwhile, Ellis and Ferreira-Junior (2009b) identified strong 
positive correlations among these factors and recommended the use of computer 
simulations to investigate their independent and joint contributions. Ellis and 
Larsen-Freeman (2009) presented two connectionist computational models to 
investigate the dynamic interactions of these factors in the simulated emergence 
of the three types of VACs.We will not delve into the details of the simulations 
here, as they did not directly involve corpus analysis per se, although the frequency 
information derived from the input data generated by the L1 English speakers 
was used to inform the input to the models. Nevertheless, we note in passing 
that the same order of emergence of the VACs observed in the ESL learners was 
replicated in the simulations, with the same factors conspiring in this emergence. 
The authors, however, left it to future simulation research to tease out the unique 
contributions of individual factors. 
Still on the topic of the effects of key input factors on L2 development, we 
now turn to a study by Collins et al. (2009), which compared the opacity profile 
of a construction acquired earlier and more easily (i.e., progressive aspect) against 
those of two harder-to-acquire constructions (i.e., simple past and the possessive 
determiners his/her) in terms of the type-token distribution of the forms, their 
lexical properties, and their perceptual salience. The authors did not analyze a 
longitudinal learner corpus to examine L2 learners’ development of these con­
structions but drew on acquisition findings from previous longitudinal studies 
that the progressive emerges early and is easier to acquire while the simple past 
and possessive determiners his/her both emerge later and are more difficult to 
acquire (e.g., Bardovi-Harlig, 2000). The authors used a 110K-word corpus of

---

<!-- page 122 -->

 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Second Language Development Trajectories and Influential Factors 105 
transcriptions of aural instructional input (over 40 hours) collected longitudi­
nally in three sixth-grade intensive ESL classes at schools in Quebec, Canada. 
Each class was recorded four times at 100-hour intervals during the 400-hour 
program. Only the speech of the three L1 English classroom teachers was used 
in the analysis. 
The authors analyzed the corpus in three ways. First, they examined the type 
and token distributions of the forms of the three constructions in relation to other 
relevant forms.The two target possessive determiners his and her accounted for 
6% and 3% (150 and 79 out 2,398) of the possessive determiners in the corpus, 
and verbs in the simple past tense and progressive aspect accounted for 9% and 5% 
(1,413 and 693 out of 15,130) of the finite verbs in the corpus.These distribu­
tion profiles did not suggest that any of the three constructions would be easily 
acquired from input alone. 
Second, the authors analyzed the lexical properties of the words the forms 
were attached to, namely, lexical frequency and lexical aspect profiles for verbs 
and collocates of his/her. The lexical frequency profiles showed that 98% of all 
irregular past tense verb types were among the top 1,000 most frequent English 
word families, while only 58% of the regular past tense verb types were in this 
band, with only four of them repeated eight or more times. Meanwhile, about 
70% of the progressive marked verbs were in this band, with 26 of them repeated 
eight or more times. In terms of the lexical aspect profiles, 72% of the verb types 
in simple past tense were telic verbs, while 59% of the verb types in progressive 
aspect were activity words. These results suggest that regular simple past tense 
is relatively less frequent and largely restricted to telic verbs, while progressive 
occurs across a wider range of frequent verbs and is less restricted to a single 
class of verbs. An analysis of the 233 noun collocate tokens of the possessive 
determiners his/her revealed that they fell into four semantic categories: Inanimate 
(53%), animate kin-same (14%), animate kin-different (14%), and body parts 
(19%).The animate kin-same and kin-different categories refer to cases where the 
grammatical gender of the possessive determiner matches (e.g., his father) or does 
not match (e.g., his mother) the natural gender of the noun referent.The authors 
argued that the most informative contexts for learning in the case of francophone 
ESL learners are kin-different, which is a small lexical set, with 15 of the 31 tokens 
accounted for by one type (i.e., his wife). Overall, the results of the lexical analyses 
were taken to indicate that the progressive is semantically less restricted than 
the simple past tense and possessive determiners, and the authors concluded that 
“frequency-based lexical factors do appear to distinguish between easy and hard 
constructions” (Collins et al., 2009, p. 345). 
Third, the authors examined the phonetic environment of the forms in order 
to determine their phonetic and perceptual salience. A phonetically trained 
judge was asked to listen to every token of the three constructions, transcribe 
it in its immediate phonetic context, and record all instances with additional 
emphasis (stress, repetition, pausing). It was found that all progressive markers

---

<!-- page 123 -->

 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
106 Second Language Development Trajectories and Influential Factors 
occurred in phonetically salient environments, while more than 80% of the two 
possessive determiners and the regular past occurred in phonetic environments 
that were hard to perceive, with 75% of the regular past in contexts modified by 
the following sound. 
The authors concluded that the following three input factors reliably dis­
tinguished constructions acquired earlier and later: Type frequency (i.e., the 
overall frequency of inflected verbs and of noun collocates of his/her), semantic 
scope (i.e., semantic classes of nouns or verbs and the distribution of the con­
struction across those classes), and perceptual salience. These factors worked 
together “to make the progressive more available and accessible in the put than 
either the simple past or his/her possessive determiners” (Collins et al., 2009, 
p. 347). 
While not a study of the trajectories of L2 longitudinal development, Collins 
et al.’s (2009) study contributed valuable insights into the effects of additional 
input factors in addition to those considered by Ellis and Ferreira-Junior (2009a, 
b), exemplifying the usefulness of “an integrated perspective of phonological, 
morphosyntactic, and lexicosemantic properties of the forms” in aural input. 
Together with Ellis and Ferreira-Junior (2009a, b), the study also highlights the 
value of examining the actual input received by the learners in instructional and 
naturalistic contexts. 
Zdorenko and Paradis (2008) examined the role of L1 transfer in child ESL 
learners’ development of articles.The data consisted of a longitudinal corpus of 
narratives produced by 17 ESL children from new families in Edmonton, Canada. 
The children were studied five times over a two-year period (once every six 
months).At the start of the study, their mean age was five years and four months, 
and their mean time of English exposure was nine months.The 17 children were 
divided into a [+article] L1 group (3 L1 Arabic, 1 L1 Romanian, and 3 L1 Spanish; 
7 total) and a [-article] L1 group (1 Japanese, 1 Korean, and 8 Mandarin and/or 
Cantonese).The authors used two picture books, each containing three series of 
line drawings, to elicit narratives from the children.At each data collection point, 
each child was asked to tell a story looking at one set of pictures.The elicitations 
were videotaped and transcribed in CHAT format (MacWhinney, 2000). 
The analysis focused on instances of a, the, and Ø (null articles) before singular 
common nouns referring to the four animate characters and three concrete 
objects crucial for the storyline in each picture book. The articles were coded 
as “correct a”, “incorrect the” or “null” in indefinite contexts, and “correct the”, 
“incorrect a” or “null” in indefinite contexts. A three-way ANOVA with L1 
background ([+article] and [-article]) as a between-participants factor and round 
(five time points) and article type (definite and indefinite) as within-participants 
factors revealed significant main effects for round and article type but not for L1 
background.The children’s article use became more accurate over time, and they 
were more accurate with the definite than indefinite articles.The ANOVA also 
revealed a significant effect of the interaction of the three factors. However, post

---

<!-- page 124 -->

 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Second Language Development Trajectories and Influential Factors 107 
hoc comparisons showed significant differences in only one pairwise comparison: 
The [-article] group was less accurate with the definite article at time point one 
than the [+article] group. In terms of error types, the misuse was the dominant 
type for both L1 groups; null article errors were found in the [-article] group 
only but disappeared after the second time point. Independent samples t-tests 
confirmed that the only significant difference in the proportion of different error 
types between the L1 groups was for null article use in definite and indefinite 
contexts, with the [-article] group showing a higher proportion of these errors. 
The authors concluded that overall, L1 background had a very limited effect 
on the developmental patterns and rates of article acquisition for their child 
ESL learners, while article type had a much stronger effect. Comparing their 
findings to previous findings on adult L2 learners’ article acquisition (Ionin 
et al., 2008), they further concluded that the child ESL learners, even those in 
the [-article] group, approximated the target system faster than adult L2 learn­
ers, pointing to differential effects of L1 background on child and adult L2 
learners’ acquisition of articles. 
We conclude this section with a look at a study by Ivaska (2015), one of the 
few studies that employed a longitudinal learner corpus to examine the trajec­
tory of L2 learners’ development of constructions in an L2 other than English. 
Specifically, the author traced the morphological forms exhibiting clear frequency 
changes in a longitudinal corpus of L2 Finnish writing from a usage-based per­
spective. In addition to the focus on L2 Finnish, another unique feature of this 
study lies in its data-driven approach, as the target constructions were not chosen 
before the study but were allowed to emerge from the learner data.The data used 
in the study was sourced from the Corpus of Advanced Learner Finnish (Ivaska, 
2014), comprised of 196 essays (86,372 tokens) written by 37 advanced Finnish 
as a second language writers from 12 L1 backgrounds in the first 16 months of 
their MA program in Finnish studies at a Finnish university.The learner essays 
were handwritten for exams that required the learners to demonstrate knowl­
edge of a range of topics based on lectures and/or readings, such as culture, litera­
ture, morphosyntax, and sociolinguistics, to name but a few.A non-longitudinal 
corpus of 101 texts (58,333 tokens) written by 101 L1 Finnish speakers was used 
as reference data.The topics of the L1 essays were less varied and included three 
of those in the L2 corpus, namely, language acquisition, language policies, and 
semantics. 
In the first step of the analysis, the author extracted frequencies of mor­
phological 1-word, 2-word, and 3-word skip-grams from the learner corpus. 
Each morphological 1-gram contained all morphological tags associated with 
a single typographic word (e.g., the morphological 1-gram<pl nom>, derived 
from the word lapset “children”, denotes a plural nominative form).The 2-grams 
and 3-grams did not need to occur consecutively but only needed to be in the 
same order within a clause to account for word order variation. A clause with 
four different words represented by four different morphological 1-grams (say

---

<!-- page 125 -->

 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
108 Second Language Development Trajectories and Influential Factors 
A, B, C, and D, in that order) would thus generate six morphological 2-grams 
(i.e., AB, AC, AD, BC, BD, CD) and three morphological 3-grams (i.e., ABC, 
ABD, BCD). Next, the author used the random forest algorithm (Breiman, 2001) 
to rank the top 100 most frequent morphological skip-grams in terms of how 
well their frequencies predicted longitudinal changes in the L2 data, using the 
number of months since the learner’s first essay as the response variable. The 
preterite form of the indicative singular third-person verb emerged as the best 
predictor of longitudinal change in this step. To ensure that the longitudinal 
change observed was not due to topic or inter-learner variation, the author used 
the random forest algorithm to confirm that among time, topic, and learner, the 
best predictor of the frequency of this feature was indeed time.The L2 writers’ 
use of the preterite tense decreased over the 16-month period as their use of 
the present tense increased, gradually approximating the L1 reference data. To 
ascertain whether the change was due to any specific constructions or semantic 
domains, the authors investigated the predictiveness of 19 co-occurring features 
or contextual variables for longitudinal change using the random forest algo­
rithm.This procedure yielded person marking and the lemma of the main verb 
as the best predictors. Distinctive collexeme analyses identified specific person 
markings and main verb lemmas that were distinctively associated with the first 
and last five months of the observation period or that proportionally increased or 
decreased from the first to the last five months.The L2 writers’ use of the singular 
third and passive forms decreased over time, becoming more similar to the L1 
reference data. However, the use of the singular first person and the plural third 
person still diverged from the L1 reference data towards the end of the observa­
tion period. A qualitative analysis of the L2 writers’ use of the forms over time 
suggested that “the decrease in narrative features such as the preterite tense” may 
be attributed to their increasing familiarity with the expected linguistic behavior 
for the academic Finnish written exam response genre and thus reflective of their 
“becoming members of the relevant discourse community” (Ivaska, 2015, p. 233). 
Ivaska’s (2015) study has showcased the potential of the data-driven approach, 
one that is not frequently adopted by L2 developmental studies, for detecting 
significant developmental changes without preconceived hypotheses. Such an 
approach may be especially useful for revealing ongoing changes among highly 
advanced L2 learners or within shorter observation periods, as hypothesized 
changes may not clearly materialize in such learners or observation periods. 
The author’s effort in linking the ongoing changes observed to the emergence 
and maturing of L2 learners’ familiarity with and competence in specific new 
registers and genres is also a highly plausible one. The author discussed the 
uneven distribution of the data across the observation period and the variation 
in the topics used to elicit the learner essays as two methodological limitations 
of the study.This discussion once again echoes the recurrent themes of corpus 
design and task effects in the use of longitudinal learner corpora in L2 develop­
ment research.

---

<!-- page 126 -->

 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Second Language Development Trajectories and Influential Factors 109 
4.6 L2 Development of Stance 
Crosthwaite and Jiang (2017) examined the L2 longitudinal development of 
stance features (e.g., hedges, boosters, attitude markers, and self-mention) in a 
one-semester EAP course in Hong Kong. The participants for this study were 
87 freshman undergraduate students (aged 18–19) enrolled in five different 
sections of a 14-week EAP course at a university in Hong Kong. All students 
were either L1 Cantonese or L1 Mandarin speakers with an upper-intermediate 
level of L2 English proficiency (IELTS scores ranging from 6.5 to 8 with a mean 
of 7). Data was collected at three data points (DPs;Weeks 1, 10, and 14).At each 
DP, the participants could choose to write either an academic report (e.g., on 
campus sustainability) or an essay (e.g., on the topic of whether death penalty 
should be restored in Hong Kong), with the restriction that they must write 
in two different genres at DP1 and DP2. The authors indicated that the two 
genres had similar social functions, that the EAP course materials emphasized 
the structural differences between the two genres, and that the students were 
motivated to develop and support their stance in writing in both genres. Stance 
features (i.e., hedging, boosters, self-mention, and attitude markers) were covered 
at various points in the course and constituted a heavily weighted component of 
the program’s standardized assessment rubric. 
The writing samples were converted into plain text files and then into a 
searchable corpus using UAM CorpusTool (version 3.3h, O’Donnell, 2008).The 
authors annotated the samples for stance features using Hyland’s (2005) list of 
hedges, boosters, attitude markers, and self-mentions. Two L1 English speakers 
then each checked ten essays and ten reports at each DP (60 texts total) to 
assess the appropriateness of the annotations. Rater agreement, measured using 
Intraclass Correlation Coefficient (ICC), reached 0.738, which was considered 
good quality (Fleiss, 1981).The raw frequencies of the annotated items in each 
text were normalized to per 1,000 words in UAM CorpusTool for further analysis. 
The data showed an increase in the use of hedges and a decrease in the use 
of boosters, self-mention, and attitude markers across the three DPs.The authors 
built a separate mix-effects linear regression model for each feature, using the 
standardized z-score for the normalized frequency of that feature per text as 
the dependent variable, DP and task type as fixed effects, and the participants’ 
demographic information (major, L1 background, medium of instruction of 
secondary school, languages other than English and Chinese, and time lived 
abroad) as crossed random effects (with DP as the repeated-measures variable). DP, 
taken as reflecting the impact of instruction, was found to be a significant predictor 
of the use of all four features; Bonferroni-corrected pairwise comparisons showed 
that hedging increased significantly from DP1 to DP2, the other three features 
all decreased significantly from DP1 to DP3, and attitude markers also decreased 
significantly from DP2 to DP3.Task type was a significant predictor for hedging, 
boosting, and self-mention, and its interaction with DP had a significant effect on

---

<!-- page 127 -->

 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
110 Second Language Development Trajectories and Influential Factors 
hedging and boosting.A follow-up analysis of the distribution of stance features 
in the two task types across the three DPs indicated a significant longitudinal 
effect on the use of stance features specific to essays, in which such features 
were both more frequent and diverse than in reports. In particular, boosters 
such as indeed, believe, and obvious were prevalent in essays at DP3, while self-
mentions reduced substantially in essay introductions over time. None of the 
learner variables emerged as a significant covariance predictor. Comparisons of 
the frequencies of specific words at different DPs using log likelihood ratio tests 
revealed longitudinal variation in the specific wordings of all four stance features 
selected. For example, for hedging, the use of claim/claimed was more frequent 
at DP1, while that of usually was more frequent at DP2 and DP3. Overall, the 
authors judged the wordings selected at DP2 and DP3 to be better aligned with 
the conventions of academic discourse than those at DP1. The authors also 
reported that the increased frequency of hedges may have had a major influence 
on the learners’ improvement in their letter grades from DP2 and DP3. 
This short-term course-based longitudinal study was designed to shed light 
on the effectiveness of EAP instruction for helping L2 learners develop the abil­
ity to employ a more register-appropriate academic stance in academic writing. 
The findings highlighted the importance for teachers to systematically explain 
the communicative and rhetorical effects of stance markers in academic writ­
ing to L2 EAP learners. The authors mentioned a number of specific design 
limitations pertaining to the comparability of the writing samples collected at 
the three DPs (e.g., DP1 was not graded, and DP3 was timed with a longer 
word limit). In addition to attending more carefully to such design issues, future 
research could integrate learner response data from each DP to supplement the 
results from pure textual analysis, analyze whether and how L2 EAP learners’ use 
of stance markers becomes more appropriate and effective over time (see, e.g., Lu 
et al., 2021), and explore how the development of stance continues as learners 
obtain even richer exposure to and immersion in EAP writing instruction and 
their disciplinary fields. 
4.7 Summary 
In this chapter, we have reviewed a large body of studies that used longitudinal 
learner corpora to examine the trajectories of various aspects of L2 devel­
opment, including that of CAF, productive lexical knowledge, phraseological 
competence, grammatical and morphosyntactic constructions, and stance fea­
tures in academic writing. Some of these studies considered whether and how 
the interaction of different learner and task variables with time spent learn­
ing the L2 affect such trajectories, using information coded in the longitudi­
nal learner corpora at hand. Others utilized either longitudinal L1 corpora or 
large-scale synchronous L1 reference corpora to examine the effects of vari­
ous input and linguistic factors on L2 development. Collectively, these studies

---

<!-- page 128 -->

 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Second Language Development Trajectories and Influential Factors 111 
have yielded highly valuable insights into the extent and direction of the var­
ied aspects of L2 development over observation periods ranging from 14 or 
15 weeks or six months on the shorter end to one year in the mid-range to 
two or three years on the longer end.The scope of this line of research can be 
expanded in many theoretically and pedagogically meaningful ways, with many 
underrepresented and understudied constructs, constructions, competences, 
L2s, learner groups, and task variables on the plate. Several recurring meth­
odological concerns emerged in this body of research, including in particular 
the selection of L1 reference corpora representing L1 input; the control for 
topic and repetition effects in pedagogically feasible ways in longitudinal stud­
ies; the size, scale, density, and duration of longitudinal L2 corpora that would 
allow significant linguistic development to fully emerge; and systematic quan­
titative operationalizations of multidimensional linguistic constructs and their 
subconstructs. To break new ground in this line of research, the field would 
benefit from several promising methodological advances. These include con­
tinued efforts in building substantially larger-scale, longer-term longitudinal 
learner corpora with richer learner and task variables than currently available 
in diverse L2s, further development of data-driven approaches to dynamically 
identify and profile linguistic features and constructions undergoing significant 
growth in longitudinal L2 corpora, and systematic attempts to rigorously col­
lect L1 input corpora that maximally capture the instructional, incidental, and/ 
or naturalistic interactive input experienced by the L2 learners represented in 
the longitudinal L2 corpora.
