<!-- page 134 -->

  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
5 
VARIABILITY AND VARIATION IN 
SECOND LANGUAGE DEVELOPMENT 
5.1 Introduction 
In Chapter 4, we reviewed a body of research that utilized longitudinal corpora 
to examine group-level trajectories of second language (L2) development and 
the input, learner, and task factors that affect such developmental trajectories. In 
this chapter, we examine the use of corpus linguistic methods in investigating 
variability and variation in L2 development. We distinguish variability and 
variation following the distinction of these two terms in the Complex Dynamic 
Systems Theory (CDST) literature (e.g., Lowie & Verspoor, 2015; van Dijk et al., 
2011; van Geert & Steenbeek, 2005; Verspoor et al., 2008). Specifically, inter-
learner variability is used to refer to differences in the longitudinal developmental 
trajectories among individual learners, intra-learner variability is used to refer 
to when and how change happens within an individual learner’s development 
trajectory, and individual variation is used to refer to inter-learner variation in 
language use or performance at one moment in time during the developmental 
process. We should also clarify that the discussion of variation in L2 use in 
Chapter 2 focused on factors such as first language (L1) background giving rise to 
variation among different subgroups of learners (e.g., L1 Chinese vs. L1 Spanish 
learners of English), while the discussion of variation here focuses on variation at 
the individual level. 
The chapter starts with an overview of the major tenets and investigative 
concerns of the CDST approach to second language acquisition (SLA).We then 
systematically review a small body of empirical studies from this and other related 
approaches that integrated corpus linguistic techniques to investigate inter- and 
intra-learner variability and individual variation in L2 development.We conclude 
with a discussion of the methodological choices made in the studies reviewed, 
DOI: 10.4324/9781003054948-5

---

<!-- page 135 -->

 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
118 Variability and Variation in Second Language Development 
the specific contribution of corpus linguistic methods, and the research gaps and 
limitations that exist in this body of research. 
5.2 A Complex Dynamic Systems Approach 
to Second Language Acquisition 
The Complex Dynamic Systems Theory unifies two independent SLA innovations 
with shared theoretical roots, namely, Complexity Theory, a theoretical paradigm 
in the natural sciences introduced into the SLA field by Larsen-Freeman (1997), 
and Dynamic Systems Theory, a spin-off of the Dynamical System Theory in 
mathematics introduced into the SLA field by de Bot et al. (2007). CDST is 
a meta-theory that provides the principles for understanding and theorizing 
language and its use and development in complex and dynamic terms, with object 
theories for language (e.g., corpus linguistics and construction grammar) and 
language learning (e.g., usage-based theories and emergentism) that align with 
those principles and that address questions regarding the processes and outcomes 
of language development (de Bot et al., 2013; Larsen-Freeman, 2017). CDST 
researchers have put forth a number of key assumptions and principles underlying 
the complex, dynamic systems view of language and language development. 
Importantly, language is seen as a dynamic system that evolves in the dynamics 
of use by and among its speakers and that adapts to local communities and usage 
contexts, allowing patterns of language use to emerge from the bottom up 
(Larsen-Freeman,2006,2017;Verspoor et al.,2011).This dynamic, adaptive nature 
of language aligns with the view of language as not only a cognitive resource 
but also a social resource, highlighting the fact that it is used for social action 
in context (Atkinson, 2014). Because language is a complex system consisting 
of many subsystems that are each complex and dynamic, language development 
cannot be explained by performance in any one linguistic subsystem but involves 
the interaction of different subsystems (Larsen-Freeman, 2006, 2017; Lowie & 
Verspoor, 2015).There are no discrete developmental stages in which a learner’s 
performance is invariant, nor is there a single, fixed end state of development; 
rather, learner development is characterized by fluctuation and variability, 
individual developmental trajectories may be very different from one another, 
and a learner’s interlanguage may never converge to the target language (Larsen-
Freeman, 1997, 2006, 2017;Verspoor et al., 2008). Since its inception, CDST has 
made important contributions in a wide range of areas of applied, educational, 
and theoretical linguistics research, including not only language development, 
attrition, and pedagogy (e.g., Han, 2021; Lowie et al., 2010; Schmid et al., 2013) 
but also language ecology, evolution, and planning (Bastardas-Boada, 2013; 
Cowley, 2011; Mufwene et al., 2017), among many others. 
In a comprehensive review of 25 years of research adopting the CDST approach 
to language learning, Hiver et al. (2021) systematically analyzed 158 research reports 
with respect to their empirical contributions to L2 development, methodological

---

<!-- page 136 -->

 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
Variability and Variation in Second Language Development 119 
trends, and potential areas for improvement. These studies have examined the 
development of various aspects of the L2 system and provided empirical support 
for the dynamic and complex nature of the L2 system and the L2 development 
process. In particular, they offered substantive evidence of dynamic regularities 
in L2 development, interaction between different elements in the development 
process, nonlinearity of the developmental trajectory, the role of context in devel­
opment, the emergent patterns of development, inter- and intra-learner variability 
in development, and system adaptation, among others. Some studies also addressed 
the compatibility of CDST with other research paradigms and discussed the com­
plementarity of group and individual level analysis. Methodologically, most studies 
were exploratory and featured small samples (50 participants or fewer) of college 
students or adult learners; about half of the studies were longitudinal in design 
(as opposed to cross-sectional or other) or focused on the individual (as opposed 
to the group).The authors noted that some studies relied on data or analyses that 
appeared inappropriate for examining development and/or connections in con­
text (e.g., cross-sectional test data or very sparse longitudinal data) or that were 
limited in scope or subjected to sample selection bias, resulting in limited transfer­
ability or generalizability of the conclusions drawn. 
Our focus in the next two sections will be on longitudinal studies that utilized 
learner corpus data and/or corpus linguistic techniques to examine variability 
and variation in L2 development. In the Chomskyan paradigm (Chomsky, 1965), 
variation in language performance has been largely overlooked, given the primary 
concern with linguistic competence. In sociolinguistic and psycholinguistic 
approaches to L2 development, researchers have attempted to identify linguistic, 
contextual, and psychological factors that may lead to individual variation in 
linguistic performance in order to shed light on different stages of L2 development 
(e.g., Ellis, 1994;Verspoor et al., 2008). Recent L2 development studies of group 
developmental trends have increasingly used modern mixed-model designs to 
account for the effect of individual variation on such trends by including it as a 
random effect. CDST researchers have argued that group developmental trends 
“may not be representative of any of the individual learners in the group” (Lowie 
& Verspoor, 2015, p. 67) and that individual variation at one moment in time in 
the development process cannot be equated with individual variability over time. 
Seeing variability as an intrinsic property of the language development process, they 
have highlighted the importance to examine variability in order to understand how 
the interaction of developmental variables shapes the developmental trajectories 
of individual learners over time (Lowie & Verspoor, 2015; van Dijk et al., 2011). 
5.3 Inter- and Intra-Learner Variability in 
Second Language Development 
In this section, we review a set of studies that focused on variability in L2 develop-
ment.The first subsection discusses Larsen-Freeman (2006), a seminal study that

---

<!-- page 137 -->

 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
120 Variability and Variation in Second Language Development 
laid out the theoretical rationale for attending to variability in L2 development 
and illustrated the importance of uncovering inter- and intra-learner variability in 
understanding the complex dynamic nature of L2 learning, along with an exam­
ple learner corpus study of variability in L2 development inspired by Larsen-
Freeman (2006).The second and third subsections then focus on methodological 
advances in and research findings of learner corpus studies of inter- and intra-
learner variability in L2 development, respectively. 
5.3.1 Variability in Focus: A Starting Point 
and A Further Example 
Let us start our review with a look at a study by Larsen-Freeman (2006), who 
examined the emergence of complexity, accuracy, and fluency (CAF) in the spoken 
and written production of five L1 Chinese ESL learners.While the study did not 
explicitly mention the use of learner corpora or corpus linguistic techniques, it is 
important to include it here because it constituted “a first attempt to grapple with 
the conceptual apparatus and tools to understand complex systems in action in 
second language learning” (Larsen-Freeman, 2006, p. 595), in addition to its heavy 
focus on inter- and intra-learner variability in the ESL learners’ development 
from the CDST perspective.The participants were five high intermediate female 
ESL learners aged 27 to 37 in a class that met weekly for two and a half hours 
over a ten-month period. Each learner also received private tutoring from the 
instructor once a month, lasting one hour. In the first six months, each participant 
was asked to write a narrative about a past experience once every six weeks (four 
times total) and to tell the story orally three days after writing each story.There 
was no time limit for either task, and the learners did not receive feedback on 
their performance. 
Four measures recommended by Wolfe-Quintero et al. (1998) to be the best 
measures of L2 writing development were calculated for each writing sample: 
Fluency (number of words per T-unit), syntactic complexity (number of clauses 
per T-unit), accuracy (proportion of error-free T-units), and lexical complexity 
(corrected type-token ratio, i.e., number of word types divided by the square root 
of two times the number of word tokens).While the group as a whole improved 
consistently over time in all four measures, there was a high degree of inter- and 
intra-learner variability.With respect to inter-learner variability, for each measure, 
some learners regressed and progressed over time, while others remained largely 
unchanged across the four time points.To examine intra-learner variability, the 
author transformed the measures to z-scores to make them comparable. For each 
learner, not only did the performance on the same measure vary from one time 
point to the next, but also the level of performance at each same time point var­
ied across the four measures.The author interpreted these results as pointing to 
“attractors or preferred paths within individual performances” (Larsen-Freeman, 
2006, p. 601), or, more specifically, what each learner focused on at each time

---

<!-- page 138 -->

 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Variability and Variation in Second Language Development 121 
point and over time. Further analysis indicated that the rate of change of the 
measures fluctuated for different learners at different time points.The author also 
performed a qualitative analysis of the idea units, defined as “a message segment 
consisting of a topic and comment that is separated from contiguous units syn­
tactically or intonationally” (Ellis & Barkuizen, 2005, p. 154), to examine how the 
narratives were constructed and how narrative construction varied over time.This 
analysis showed that for each learner, changes occurred over time at variegated 
linguistic levels, including lexical, phrasal, clausal, utterance, and semantic, among 
others.The author argued that some changes may have resulted from social fac­
tors, while others from the learners’ internal reorganization of their L2 system. 
Larsen-Freeman (2006) concluded that “individual developmental paths, 
each with all its variation, may be quite different from one another” (p. 615). 
Methodologically, she argued that the time-series design with participants with 
a homogeneous L1 background and comparable proficiency level, while not 
new, allows for the examination of the micro-level details that are crucial for 
understanding the variable and context-dependent nature of linguistic behavior 
and the variable developmental trajectories of individual learners.All in all, using 
relatively straightforward analytical procedures, Larsen-Freeman (2006) provided 
initial empirical findings that reinforced the theoretical rationale for attending 
to variability in L2 development research, i.e., such variability is an inherent 
property of and abundant in the complex, dynamic process of L2 development. 
For future research, she raised the question of whether improvement on one 
task can be generalized to other tasks and argued that macro-developmental 
studies of individual learners are necessary for understanding how the variability 
and behavior at one time point may impact the variability or stability of learner 
development over time, a central concern in L2 development research. 
Inspired by Larsen-Freeman’s (2006) theoretical positioning and empirical 
analysis, Zhang and Lu (2013) examined inter- and intra-learner variability in 
the development of the Chinese numeral classifier system among four college-
level higher intermediate learners of Chinese as a foreign language (CFL) over a 
30-week period from the CDST perspective.The data of the four focal learners 
were sourced from a larger longitudinal corpus that consisted of 657 essays 
written by college-level CFL learners at the lower intermediate level and higher 
intermediate level at a university in the United States. As part of a 30-student 
cohort in the higher intermediate group, all four focal learners enrolled in the 
fifth Chinese course of a six-semester sequence in the fall semester of 2009 and 
subsequently the sixth course in the same sequence in the spring semester of 2010. 
The four focal participants were all L1 English non-heritage CFL learners in their 
early 20s and were selected to represent high-, average-, and low-achieving learners 
within the higher intermediate group. Each focal learner also completed ten out 
of the 11 required essays assigned at 11 different time points over the 30-week 
period. The writing tasks ranged from descriptions of personal experience and 
pictures to stories and diaries, to comments on assigned readings.To alleviate the

---

<!-- page 139 -->

 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
122 Variability and Variation in Second Language Development 
potential effects of writing tasks and topics on the learners’ classifier usage, the 
essays were grouped by month (i.e., September, October, November, December, 
February, and April), resulting in six uniform data points for each learner. 
All essays were word-segmented and part-of-speech (POS) tagged using the 
NLPIR-ICTCLAS Chinese Lexical Analysis System (http://ictclas.nlpir.org 
/index_e.html). Inappropriate classifiers and missing classifiers in obligatory 
environments in each essay were then manually identified and annotated. The 
authors retrieved instances of classifiers and missing classifiers in obligatory 
environments using the POS and error tags with AntConc 3.2.2.1 (Anthony, 
2011) and then manually examined those instances to filter false positives resulting 
from tagging errors. Each learner’s classifier use at each data point was measured 
for fluency, diversity, and accuracy. Fluency was calculated as the number of 
classifier tokens per 100 characters as well as the number of classifier types per 
100 characters, diversity was calculated as the type-token ratio of classifiers, and 
accuracy was calculated as the accuracy rate of all classifiers produced by the 
learner. The accuracy rate of classifiers in all obligatory environments was also 
computed but not considered due to the rarity of omitted classifiers among the 
focal learners. Following Larsen-Freeman (2006), the authors transformed these 
measures to z-scores to make them comparable. 
The four focal learners exhibited varied, nonlinear developmental trajectories 
for all three dimensions. For example, for Learner S, both token and type fre­
quencies increased over the first four months and then decreased in the last two 
months; diversity decreased over the first three months, increased in the next two 
months, and declined again in the last month; accuracy remained high throughout 
the observation period. For Learner J, token frequency increased over the first 
three months, followed by fluctuations in the last three months; type frequency 
remained stable; diversity and accuracy both fluctuated back and forth throughout 
the period.Additionally, fluency, diversity, and accuracy of classifier use interacted 
in varied ways among the four learners as well. For example, for Learner S, the 
three dimensions co-developed in the first three months; in the last three months, 
diversity and accuracy continued to co-develop, but both competed with flu­
ency. For Learner J, fluency and accuracy largely developed together (with the 
exception of one data point, i.e., December) and both competed with diversity 
throughout the observation period. A close analysis of the actual classifiers used 
by the four focal learners further revealed substantial variability in the degrees to 
which they reduced the tendency to overgeneralize the general classifier ge (个) 
to concrete and abstract nouns, expanded their repertoire of unique classifiers, 
and mastered the use of unique classifiers over the observation period. For each 
learner, intra-learner variability was visible at two levels:Across different dimen­
sions at each time point and across different time points for each dimension. 
The authors concluded that the four focal learners’ developmental trajectories 
of the three dimensions were characterized by nonlinearity (with varying degrees 
of progression, fluctuation, and regression) and inter- and intra-learner variability.

---

<!-- page 140 -->

 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Variability and Variation in Second Language Development 123 
They further argued that variability should be treated as an intrinsic rather than 
a marginal property of L2 development and that assistance tailored to individual 
learners’ emerging needs at different time points should be an integral component 
of group-level instruction.This line of thinking partially motivated the authors’ 
subsequent research on assessing and supporting L2 learners’ development 
using computerized dynamic assessment (e.g., Poehner et al., 2015; Zhang & 
Lu, 2019). Meanwhile, the authors also identified the expansion of classifier-
noun collocations as a common challenge facing all four focal learners in their 
acquisition of the Chinese classifier system and advocated a collocation approach 
to presenting and teaching Chinese classifiers.The lack of adequate control for the 
potential effects of writing tasks and topics was discussed as a potential limitation. 
The two studies reviewed in this section delineated inter- and intra-learner 
variability in the development of general CAF measures in L2 English (Larsen-
Freeman, 2006) and that of CAF measures of a specific language subsystem in 
L2 Chinese (Zhang & Lu, 2013). We turn to studies that focused exclusively 
on intra-learner variability in Section 5.3.2 and on inter-learner variability in 
Section 5.3.3. 
5.3.2 Intra-Learner Variability in L2 Development: 
Methods and Findings 
Van Geert and Van Dijk (2002) presented several methods specifically for 
visualizing and describing intra-learner variability in individual time-series data 
of repeated observations and demonstrated how those techniques could be used 
to systematically examine intra-learner variability in child language acquisition. 
The usefulness of some of these and other related techniques for analyzing intra-
learner variability in L2 development was subsequently demonstrated by Verspoor 
et al.(2008) and Spoelman and Verspoor (2010).We briefly discuss some of the key 
techniques used in one or both of these studies before turning to their findings 
pertaining to intra-learner variability in this section. 
First, moving-window min-max graphs can be used to visualize the patterns 
of longitudinal change for individual developmental variables. At each time 
point, the min-max graph of a particular developmental variable plots the 
minimum and maximum values of that variable within a predetermined moving 
window. With a window size of 5, for example, the mix-max range for time 
point 1 corresponds to the minimum value and maximum value from time point 
1 to time point 5. In this way, the data are presented in a bandwidth of values, 
allowing one to visually examine whether the moving-window minimums and 
maximums show fluctuations over time. Second, raw and detrended correlations 
can be used to examine the interaction between developmental variables. Raw 
correlations are calculated using the actual data. However, the general upward 
or downward trend of the variables may lead to overestimation of the degree of 
their interaction, as the small local increases or decreases are not accounted for.

---

<!-- page 141 -->

 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
124 Variability and Variation in Second Language Development 
To eliminate the influence of such general trends, the data can be detrended. 
Verspoor et al. (2008), for example, used linear regression for this purpose, and a 
more detailed discussion of the suitability of different functions for detrending 
developmental data can be found in Van Geert and Van Dijk (2002). A moving 
window of correlations (e.g., with a window of 5) can also be used to better 
reveal the pattern of interaction between two developmental variables over 
time. Third, the Progressive Maximum (Progmax) and Regressive Minimum 
(Regmin) can be calculated to determine possible developmental jumps in the 
developmental process. The Progressive Maximum is calculated by identifying 
the maximum values in the first n (n >4) data points (e.g., the first five, first six, 
first seven, etc.), and the Regressive Minimum is calculated by identifying the 
minimum values in the last n (n>4) data points (e.g., the last five, last six, last 
seven, etc.).These values are then plotted in Progmax-Regmin graphs, and the 
point at which maximal instances between those values are observed can be taken 
to indicate a possible developmental jump. Finally, Monte Carlo simulations can 
be conducted to determine whether the correlations between developmental 
variables and the developmental jumps are statistically significant or coincidental, 
e.g., using the Poptools add-in in Microsoft Excel for random sampling (Hood, 
2004). More detailed hands-on illustrations of these and other techniques can be 
found in Verspoor et al. (2011). 
Verspoor et al. (2008) demonstrated how such methods can be used to 
describe and visualize intra-learner variability in individual longitudinal data to 
better understand the dynamic nature of L2 development.The dataset they used 
for this demonstration consisted of 18 academic writing samples produced by 
an advanced L1 Dutch college-level English learner over a three-year period. 
They used the following measures to assess vocabulary use:Average word length 
(WL),type-token ratio (TTR), the degree of use of words on the Academic Word 
List (AWL; Coxhead, 2000), and average sentence length (SL). To measure the 
complexity of sentence constructions, they used two fine-grained measures:The 
length of the noun phrase (NP) and the number of words per finite verb (W/FV). 
The authors first illustrated the importance of variability with the development 
of individual variables. While almost all of the variables increased overall over 
time, all measures developed nonlinearly with different degrees of variability. 
For example, the frequency of AWL words across the academic writing samples 
fluctuated quite dramatically (e.g., 4 in Assignment 1, 0 in Assignment 5, 12 in 
Assignment 10).Word length, however, appeared to increase more steadily, with a 
smoothing function (i.e., a polynomial function of the second degree) indicating 
a general increasing trend (from 4.5 letters per word to 5.5).A moving min-max 
graph for word length showed a period with relative stability among the first five 
texts (narrow range), followed by a highly variable period from text 6 to text 
12 (large range) and again a relatively stable period from text 13 onward. The 
authors argued that the period of enlarged variability constituted a transition 
phase between the two more stable periods.

---

<!-- page 142 -->

 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Variability and Variation in Second Language Development 125 
Next, the authors illustrated how examining combinations of variables with 
a meaningful relationship to each other can yield further insights into dynamic 
developmental processes, particularly with respect to how different subsystems 
interact with each other (e.g., they may support or compete with each other’s 
growth). For example, average SL and TTR both oscillated with a slight upward 
trend, but the directions of their oscillations tended to go in opposite directions. 
The two variables had a trivial and insignificant correlation of -.03. Detrending 
the dataset with a linear regression confirmed this largely competitive relationship 
between the variables:When one measure went up, the other measure went down. 
A moving window of correlation between the two measures (with a window 
size of five) further revealed that they were positively correlated with each other 
in the early and later stages but negatively correlated with each other between 
time point 4 and time point 15.The competitive relationship between the two 
measures in that period indicated a tradeoff between longer sentences and more 
diverse vocabulary use in the learner’s academic writing during that stage of the 
developmental process. Finally, the authors illustrated how plotting average SL in 
relation to TTR in an X-Y scatterplot offers an additional view of the interaction 
between the two variables. Each data point in an X-Y scatterplot represents the 
values of the two variables at a particular time point, and the trajectory drawn 
through the sequence of data points is taken to indicate how the two variables 
jointly develop over time. Instead of a linear increase from the lower left to the 
upper right of the graph, which would indicate a straightforward co-growing 
relation between the two variables, the authors noted many diagonals in the 
opposite direction, suggesting a competitive relationship between the two.Towards 
the end of the trajectory (from data point 14), the amount of variation appeared 
to have slowed down, but the authors noted that more data would be needed 
to determine if this was the beginning of a stabilization process. Similar analyses 
conducted for average SL and the complexity measure of W/FV showed a lack of 
relationship between the two.While native speakers often employ more complex 
structures in longer sentences, the advanced learner demonstrated substantial 
variability and no recognizable pattern in the strategies she used for making her 
sentences longer.The two complexity measures, i.e., the length of NPs and W/ 
FV, however, showed positive correlations with coinciding high and low points, 
indicating that they were connected growers. 
Verspoor et al.’s (2008) study made two distinct contributions. Methodologically, 
they illustrated two ways to examine intra-learner variability as well as several 
analytical procedures for each of those, including the use of a smoothing function 
and a min-max graph for examining variability in the development of individual 
variables and the use of correlation analyses (including raw and detrended 
correlations and a moving window of correlations) and an X-Y scatterplot for 
examining the interaction of the variability of meaningfully related developmental 
variables. Theoretically, their analysis of intra-learner variability has yielded 
abundant patterns confirming the CDST assumption that “variability appearing

---

<!-- page 143 -->

 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
126 Variability and Variation in Second Language Development 
in L2 development behaves according to principles of dynamic systems”(Verspoor 
et al., 2008, p. 229). 
Spoelman and Verspoor (2010) further utilized some of the same and additional 
techniques to trace the dynamic patterns in the development of morphological 
accuracy and lexical and syntactic complexity in the longitudinal data of an L2 
Finnish learner. The learner was an L1 Dutch speaker majoring in theoretical 
linguistics with a minor in Finnish at the University of Groningen for three years, 
starting from the age of 19 as a true beginning Finnish learner.The data consisted 
of 54 writing samples written on academic topics as take-home assignments 
over the three-year period.The authors sampled 100 words from each text and 
normalized the frequency counts to per 100 words for texts with fewer than 
100 words.The samples were transcribed in the Codes for the Human Analysis 
of Transcripts (CHAT) format and analyzed using the Computerized Language 
Analysis (CLAN) software (MacWhinney, 2000). 
Each sample was analyzed using several accuracy and complexity measures. 
In light of the rich morphology of Finnish, the accuracy analysis focused on the 
use of cases. Specifically, the authors calculated an overall case accuracy rate (i.e., 
the number of correct cases divided by the total number of cases) as well as an 
error rate for each of the 11 most frequent cases. Complexity was assessed at the 
word, NP, and sentence levels. For word-level complexity, the numbers of one-
morpheme, two-morpheme, three-morpheme, and four-or-more-morpheme 
words were counted, and a word complexity ratio was calculated as the difference 
between the average sentence length in morphemes and the average sentence 
length in words. For NP complexity, the numbers of one-word, two-word, 
and three-or-more-word NPs were counted, and an NP complexity ratio was 
calculated as the average NP length in words. For sentence-level complexity, the 
numbers of simple, compound, complex, and compound-complex sentences 
were counted, and a sentence complexity ratio was calculated as the average 
number of dependent clauses per text. For the interaction between accuracy and 
complexity, the authors compared the development of case errors with that of 
word complexity. 
The min-max graphs revealed changing degrees of variability in the devel­
opmental patterns of the accuracy and complexity measures. For the overall case 
accuracy rate, the degree of variability was higher in the earliest texts, decreased 
after the 11th text, and appeared to stabilize from the 28th text onward. For word 
complexity, one-morpheme and two-morpheme words started competing with 
each other from the seventh text onward, and three-morpheme were used more 
regularly from the seventh text onward as well. Four-or-more-morpheme words 
did not appear frequently until the 52nd text. For NP complexity, two-word NPs 
were very infrequent until the 11th text, started competing with one-word NPs 
after the 11th text with an increased degree of variability, and increased more sta­
bly from the 42nd text onward. For sentence-level complexity, simple sentences

---

<!-- page 144 -->

 
 
 
 
 
  
 
  
  
 
 
 
  
 
 
  
 
  
 
 
  
 
 
  
 
  
 
  
  
 
  
 
 
  
  
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
Variability and Variation in Second Language Development 127 
decreased over time with a large degree of variability until the 28th text, at which 
point the decrease stabilized; complex sentences began to be used more widely 
from the 9th text and the increase saw smaller variability after the 12th text. 
Compound and compound-complex sentences were both used very infrequently, 
with the latter emerging in the 15th text and becoming more regular from the 
30th text. 
A detailed analysis of the development of four-or-more-word NPs using a 
Progmax-Regmin graph indicated a possible developmental jump between the 
44th and the 45th text, and a Monte Carlo simulation with 10,000 simulations 
revealed that the jump was significant (p=.015). The authors argued that such 
detailed analyses of less frequently used categories could be useful. 
Moderately strong negative raw (r=–.368) and detrended (r=–.357) 
correlations were found between one-morpheme words and two-morpheme 
words, and a Monte Carlo simulation with 10,000 iterations showed that the 
competition between the two was significant (p =.005). The raw correlation 
between the NP and sentence complexity ratios was weak (r=.185), but the 
detrended correlation was strong and negative (r=–.451). A Monte Carlo 
simulation showed that the correlation was significant (p =.000), confirming a 
strong competitive relationship between the two. Strong positive raw correlations 
were found between the word and sentence complexity ratios (r=.605) and 
between the word and NP complexity ratios (r=.604), although the detrended 
correlations were weaker (r =.228 and r=.226, respectively), and Monte Carlo 
simulations showed strong trends only (p=.054 for both). Finally, for the 
interaction between accuracy and complexity, the detrended correlation between 
the case accuracy rate and word complexity ratio was very weak (r=–.022). A 
moving window of correlations showed alternations between periods of negative 
(texts 1–5 and texts 15–23) and positive (texts 5–15 and text 23 onward with 
some exceptions).A Monte Carlo simulation suggests that the pattern is random 
(p=.5704). 
Spoelman and Verspoor’s (2010) analysis of intra-learner variability in the 
development of case accuracy and several levels of complexity yielded important 
insights into the L2 development process. Their findings confirmed the non­
linear nature of L2 development, as the developmental patterns of accuracy and 
complexity were characterized by progressions and regressions as well as complex 
interactions between different variables.They argued that “intra-learner variability 
behaves according to the principles of Dynamic Systems” (Spoelman & Verspoor, 
2010, p. 551), with periods of variability near a developmental jump and periods 
of increased variability between developmental stages. They further concluded 
that complexity and accuracy should be examined across the full developmental 
trajectory and that the variability in the competition between developmental 
variables should be understood within individual developmental trajectories 
instead of solely in terms of inter-learner differences.

---

<!-- page 145 -->

 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
128 Variability and Variation in Second Language Development 
5.3.3 Inter-Learner Variability in L2 Development: 
Methods and Findings 
The two studies reviewed in Section 5.3.2 showcased the usefulness of several 
methods for analyzing intra-learner variability in L2 development. In this section, 
we take a look at three learner corpus studies of inter-learner variability in L2 
development, with attention to the methods they used to analyze inter-learner 
variability as well as the implications of their findings for understanding the L2 
development process. 
In Section 4.4, we discussed Crossley and Salsbury’s (2011) study of six adult 
ESL learners’ development of lexical bundle accuracy. In addition to group-level 
developmental trends, the authors also considered inter-learner variability in their 
analysis. As a reminder, their learner data consisted of 99 transcripts of the ESL 
learners’ biweekly oral interviews with L1 English speakers on diverse topics in a 
one-year period (13 to 18 transcripts per learner).The transcripts were organized 
into four time points, each representing a quarter of the one-year learning time. 
The authors operationalized lexical bundle accuracy as the extent to which the 
normalized frequency counts of bigrams produced by the learners correlated with 
those produced by L1 English speakers in the Santa Barbara Corpus of Spoken 
American English (Du Bois et al., 2000–2005).To assess inter-learner variability 
in lexical bundle accuracy development, they compared the rate of growth in 
these correlations over time among the six learners. 
At the individual level, three learners exhibited significant growth in lexical 
bundle accuracy between the first and fourth quarters. In other words, the 
correlations between the normalized frequency counts of the bigrams produced 
by these learners and L1 English speakers became significantly stronger over time. 
The magnitude of growth varied among these learners, with the learner with the 
highest starting accuracy exhibiting the smallest growth (.61 to .81), and the learner 
with the second highest starting accuracy exhibiting the greatest growth (.48 to 
.86).A fourth learner showed some growth over time, although the growth from 
the first to the fourth quarter was not significant (.41 to .63).Two learners did not 
show growth over time, with minimal changes in the correlations from the first 
quarter to the fourth quarter (.69 to .71 and .27 to .31, respectively). Crossley and 
Salsbury (2011) also computed the correlations between the six learners’TOEFL 
scores and their time spent studying English.They found significant correlations 
for the four learners whose lexical bundle accuracy improved over time but no 
significant correlation for the two learners whose lexical bundle accuracy showed 
no growth. They explained the inter-learner variability observed mainly from 
the perspective of general language proficiency. Specifically, they interpreted 
their findings as supporting “the notion that L2 learners develop lexical bundle 
accuracy as a function of increasing English proficiency” (Crossley & Salsbury, 
2011, p. 15) and attributed the two learners’ lack of lexical accuracy growth to 
their lack of general language proficiency growth over the observation period.

---

<!-- page 146 -->

 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Variability and Variation in Second Language Development 129 
In a study of the development of linguistic complexity in the writing of 
beginning L1 English learners of German, Vyatkina (2012) examined the 
differences in the developmental pathways of two focal learners as well as their 
differences from cross-sectional trendlines.The author collected a corpus of L2 
German writing samples from a cohort of learners enrolled in a German language 
program at a university in the United States over four 16-week semesters (from 
the spring of 2008 to the fall of 2009). While data collection occurred in four 
consecutive semesters, the authors treated the cohort data as cross-sectional rather 
than longitudinal because the actual participant constituency varied across the 
four semesters.The author selected two focal learners with comparable language 
learning history who completed all four semesters, one 19-year-old male (Braden) 
and one female learner in her early 30s (Cassie). Braden and Cassie shared the 
same L1 (i.e., American English) and were both true beginners, non-German 
majors, and diligent learners.The writing samples were essays written for actual 
curricular tasks every three to five weeks (four to five per semester and 19 total). 
The writing genres were personal narratives in the first two semesters (time points 
1–10), personal narratives with some reasoning elements in the third semester 
(time points 11–14), and reflections and interpretations of the meanings of the 
stories in the textbook (time points 15–18) and a book review (time point 19) in 
the fourth semester.With the exception of essays written at time points 15–18, 
which were untimed, all other essays were written during a 50-minute class. 
The number of essays ranged from 16 to 40 and the mean length ranged from 
68 words to 244 words across the 19 time points.The writing samples were tagged 
with part-of-speech (POS) information using the TreeTagger (Schmid, 1994). 
The samples were analyzed using six measures tapping into different dimensions 
of linguistic complexity. Specifically, mean sentence length (SL) was used to 
measure general complexity; finite verb units per sentence (FV/S) and words per 
finite verb unit (W/FV) were used to measure clause complexity; normalized 
frequencies of coordinating conjunctions (CC) and of subordinating conjunctions 
(SC) per 100 words were used to measure complexification by coordination and 
subordination, respectively; the corrected type-token ratio (CTTR) was used to 
measure lexicogrammatical variety. 
The author first examined the correlation between each measure and 
time for the cohort and for each of the two focal learners. For each measure, 
three scatterplots were presented to facilitate cross-sectional to longitudinal 
comparisons. The first scatterplot presented the cohort average of the measure 
for each assignment along with a least-squares regression trendline for the average 
and two curved lines representing the 95% confidence interval of the predicted 
average (Draper & Smith, 2001).The second scatterplot presented Braden’s values 
of the measure for each assignment along with a least-squares regression trendline 
and 95% confidence intervals for the trendline; these were superimposed on the 
cohort trendline to facilitate comparison.The third scatterplot presented the same 
information for Cassie.

---

<!-- page 147 -->

 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
130 Variability and Variation in Second Language Development 
The cohort’s average sentence length increased steadily from six to ten words 
per sentence over time, and the two focal learners’ developmental pathways for 
this measure were largely close to the average cohort trendline.The cohort and 
both focal learners used more FV-units per sentence over time as well; however, 
Braden’s increases surpassed the cohort average, while Cassie’s values were mostly 
below the cohort average with more upward and downward oscillations. W/ 
FV showed no significant correlation with time for either the cohort or the 
two focal learners, with no discernable developmental pattern for Braden and an 
incrementally increasing pattern with oscillations for Cassie.The frequency of CCs 
decreased significantly over time for the cohort but did not change significantly 
for the two focal learners, both of whom showed very large variations in their 
CC use.The frequency of SCs was strongly positively correlated with time for 
both the cohort and the two focal learners; while both focal learners showed 
large variations in their SC use, Braden’s development was close to the cohort, 
while Cassie’s development lagged behind the cohort. CTTR was also strongly 
positively correlated with time for both the cohort and the two focal learners; 
however, while Braden’s CTTR mostly stayed below the cohort’s trendline, 
Cassie’s CTTR consistently surpassed the cohort’s trendline with the exception 
of two data points. 
The author also analyzed the correlations among the measures. The general 
complexity measure SL was found to be significantly correlated to four specific 
complexity measures: FV/S, CC, SC, and CTTR for the cohort. For Braden, SL 
was correlated very strongly with FV/S and moderately with CC; for Cassie, SL 
was correlated very strongly with CTTR and moderately with FV/S and W/ 
FV. Among the specific syntactic complexity measures, FV/S, SC, and CC were 
significantly correlated with each other for the cohort; for the two focal learners, 
however, the only significant correlation was found between FV/S and SC for 
Braden. 
Taken together, while the two focal learners’ developmental pathways were 
largely close to the average cohort trendline for the general complexity measure 
(i.e., sentence length), the analyses revealed rich variability in their developmental 
pathways relative to the cross-sectional developmental trend for the more specific 
complexity measures. In particular, Braden made his sentences progressively 
longer primarily by using more finite-verb units, while Cassie’s sentence length 
was correlated most strongly with CTTR. A qualitative analysis also confirmed 
that Cassie used a wider range of words and constructions in collocation with 
subordinating conjunctions. Different from the cohort and Braden, Cassie’s 
sentence length was also correlated significantly with W/FV, suggesting that her 
syntactic complexification reached a higher stage than her classmates.Additionally, 
while coordinating conjunction competed with subordinating conjunction and 
FV/S for the cohort, this was not the case for the two focal learners. 
Vyatkina’s (2012) comparative analysis of the two focal learners’ developmental 
pathways against the developmental trendlines of the cohort they were part of

---

<!-- page 148 -->

 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Variability and Variation in Second Language Development 131 
yielded convincing evidence for inter-learner variability in L2 development and 
for the need to attend to both group developmental trends and individual 
developmental trajectories (Byrnes, 2009). Methodologically, Vyatkina (2012) 
showed that the frequencies of words of certain POS categories could be used to 
approximate syntactic complexity measures in learner corpus analysis, particularly 
in languages without automated syntactic complexity analysis tools.Additionally, 
the study also illustrated how using scatterplots along with trendlines and 
confidence interval lines could effectively facilitate cross-sectional to longitudinal 
comparisons. 
Vyatkina (2013) further compared the same two focal learners’ developmental 
pathways in a set of more specific syntactic complexity measures. Methodologically, 
she combined individual developmental profiling techniques (Byrnes et al., 2010) 
and longitudinal learner corpus analysis with semi-automatic corpus annotation. 
The individual developmental profiles of the two learners were established through 
frequency and contextual analyses of a set of specific syntactic structures in the 
two learners’ writing samples.The specific syntactic structures examined covered 
four types of coordinate structures (nominal phrases, modifier phrases, predicate 
phrases, and coordinate clauses), four types of complex nominal structures 
(attributive adjective phrases, prepositional phrases extending nominal phrases, 
nominal clauses, and relative clauses), and three types of nonfinite verb structures 
(infinitive phrases governed by modal and auxiliary verbs, zu (“to”)+infinitive 
phrases, and past participle phrases). 
The author and one independent annotator manually checked the POS 
tagging output from the TreeTagger (Schmid, 1994), reported F-scores of .95 
to 1 for different POS categories, and resolved all discrepancies by discus­
sion. Past participles of full verbs were captured using the corresponding POS 
tags directly. Coordinate structures were extracted by manually examining the 
concordance lines obtained using the POS tag for coordinating conjunctions 
with WordSmith Tools (Scott, 2008).All other structures were manually anno­
tated by two annotators with inter-rater reliability, measured using F-scores, 
ranging from 0.8 to 0.98. 
Two developmental profiles emerged from the frequency and contextual anal­
yses of the two focal learners’ use of the specific syntactic structures over time. 
They developed comparably in the first half of the observation period, gradually 
increasing their use of complex structures from about 0.4 per clause at time point 
one to about 1.5 at time point nine.They also used comparable proportions of 
certain types of complex structures at multiple time points (e.g., more nonfinite 
verb phrases at time point seven), likely due to the instructional focus on those 
structures at those time points. However, their developmental paths diverged 
in the final third of the observation period. Cassie’s use of complex structures 
increased linearly from time point 13 onward, reaching 1.8 structures per clause 
at time point 19, while Braden’s use of complex structures showed a less stable, 
declining trend in this period. Additionally, Cassie’s use of complex structures

---

<!-- page 149 -->

 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
132 Variability and Variation in Second Language Development 
was also more evenly distributed among the three types, while Braden did not 
use nonfinite verbs at some time points. In terms of the specific structures used, 
Cassie used more nonfinite verbs and complex nominals per clause, while Braden 
used more coordinate structures.These results indicated that while Braden made 
his sentences progressively longer by using more coordinate and simple clauses, 
Cassie did so by using more complex clausal structures.The contextual analyses 
also indicated that in the second half of the timeline, Braden’s use of coordinate 
clauses tended to be repetitive, while Cassie engaged with coordination through 
using more complex coordinate structures, particularly more coordinate predi­
cates.The fact that Cassie’s use of the three types of complex structures became 
more balanced also indicated that she could be closer to a stabilization period 
than Braden. 
These findings offered additional insights into inter-learner variability in 
beginning L2 German learners’ development of specific syntactic complexity 
features.Vyatkina (2013) highlighted the usefulness of combining developmental 
profiling with corpus analysis methods, including the use of semi-automatic 
corpus annotation methods and dense longitudinal data collection. The focus 
on true beginners and specific syntactic complexity features allowed for the 
observation of when the target features emerged, whether they disappeared or 
stayed following focused instruction, and how they were distributed at different 
time points in relation to instructional focus and task prompts. Based on the 
finding that Cassie balanced previously learned and new features while Braden 
abandoned some features in the following task, Vyatkina (2013) argued that 
“learners should be explicitly taught how to use level-appropriate and task-
appropriate linguistic features” (p. 25). 
In sum, the three studies reviewed in this section have offered convincing 
evidence of inter-learner variability in L2 development. These three studies 
situated learner corpus analysis of inter-learner variability in different ways, 
namely, parallel to the analysis of the longitudinal developmental trends of 
the same small group (Crossley & Salsbury, 2011), in comparison to cross-
sectional developmental trends of a larger cohort (Vyatkina, 2012), and as 
a detailed comparative analysis of the developmental profiles of individual 
learners (Vyatkina, 2013). These different orientations have allowed the studies 
to offer complementary insights into how individual learners’ developmental 
trajectories and profiles may vary from one another and how they may vary 
from the developmental trends of either a longitudinal or cross-sectional cohort. 
The studies demonstrated the instrumental role of dense longitudinal learner 
corpus data and automated or semi-automated annotation and analysis of such 
data in examining inter-learner variability. As was the case with intra-learner 
variability analysis, quantitative procedures are necessary for revealing variability, 
while qualitative analyses shed light on the specific ways in which such variability 
arises and/or manifests.

---

<!-- page 150 -->

 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Variability and Variation in Second Language Development 133 
5.4 Individual Variation in L2 Development 
As briefly mentioned in Section 5.2, recent L2 development studies have 
increasingly utilized modern mixed-model designs to account for individual 
variation while examining group developmental trends. This methodological 
advancement helps address the well-known problem that statistical averaging in 
the search for group patterns conceals individual patterns that often differ from 
averaged patterns (Dörnyei, 2009).As Lowie and Verspoor (2015) noted,“mixed­
model designs can neutralize individual variation within the group by including 
it as a random effect” (p. 69), although it is important to not equate individual 
variation as studied using such designs with individual variability over time as 
discussed in Section 5.3. 
In a methodological review article for Language Learning, Murakami (2016) illus­
trated how generalized linear mixed-effects models (GLMMs) can be utilized to 
investigate systematic effects and quantify individual variation at the same time. 
The demonstration was carried out with an analysis of the amount of individual 
variation in L2 accuracy development of English grammatical morphemes as well 
as the potential effect of L1 type (in terms of whether the learners’ L1s have an 
equivalent morpheme) on cross-sectional and longitudinal development patterns. 
The author used longitudinal and cross-sectional data from the pre-release version 
of the EF-Cambridge Open Language Database (EFCAMDAT; Geertzen et al., 
2014), which was described in Section 2.3.1.The following three morphemes were 
considered in the analysis: Articles, past tense –ed, and plural –s, and the teach­
ers’ corrections of erroneous grammatical morphemes were used to count obliga­
tory contexts and errors and calculate accuracy.The author selected 10 L1 groups 
with the largest amount of data in EFCAMDAT (i.e., Brazilian, Chinese, French, 
German, Italian, Japanese, Korean, Portuguese, Russian, Spanish, and Turkish). For 
each L1 group, learners with fewer than ten obligatory contexts and overgeneraliza­
tion errors for any of the three morphemes were eliminated. Four L1 groups had 
20 or fewer learners left, and those learners were all included. For each of the other 
six L1 groups, the 20 learners with the largest number of writings in that group 
were included to reduce the computational cost of some analyses. Altogether, 3, 
323 writing samples from 159 learners were included in the analysis. 
The author calculated accuracy as the ratio between correct uses and errors. 
Correct uses were counted as the number of obligatory contexts minus all omis­
sion and misformation errors, and errors were counted as the total number of 
omission, misformation, and overgeneralization errors. Obligatory contexts were 
counted as the number of morphemes in teacher-corrected texts.The frequency 
of obligatory contexts and each type of error in error-tagged texts were counted 
using R scripts.The author included four independent variables included in the 
analysis: Proficiency (the mean Englishtown level at which a learner submitted all 
writings, capturing cross-sectional development), writing number (within-learner

---

<!-- page 151 -->

 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
134 Variability and Variation in Second Language Development 
writing order, capturing longitudinal development), morpheme (a categorical 
variable with one level for each morpheme, with articles as the reference level), 
and L1 type (a dichotomous variable with two levels: ABSENT for L1 groups 
without the equivalent morpheme in their L1s and PRESENT for L1 groups 
with the equivalent morpheme in their L1s). 
The author employed a mixed-effects logistic regression model to analyze 
the relationship between accuracy, proficiency, longitudinal development, and 
morpheme type, with L1 type and learner as random-effects factors. Writings 
were nested within learners, and learners were nested within L1 groups. As 
such, the model had a nested random-effects structure, in which variance was 
partitioned into between-L1, between-learner, and between-writing levels. The 
author used a forward selection approach to identify the best model, starting with 
a model that included by-L1 and by-learner random intercepts without any fixed-
effects predictor and adding one predictor at a time that resulted in the greatest 
reduction of the model’s AIC (Akaike information criterion, an information-
theoretic measure for model comparison).The statistical analyses were carried out 
using the lme4 package (Bates et al., 2015) in R. 
The final model selected by the author included the following fixed-effects 
predictors: Morpheme, L1 type, writing number, proficiency, and the interaction 
between morpheme and proficiency, and the following random-effects factors: 
By-L1 and by-learner intercepts, by-morpheme random-contrasts at learner 
level, and by-writing number random-slope at learner level. The effects of the 
predictors in this model were tested against a reference model that included the 
same random-effects factors but only morpheme and writing number as fixed-
effects predictors. 
In the reference model, the standard deviation of the by-L1 random intercept 
indicated the dispersion of L1 groups in accuracy (.300 in the logit scale).The 
by-learner random intercept indicated the amount of individual differences in 
article accuracy within each L1 group (.495).The standard deviations of the by-
morpheme random contrasts indicated the individual differences in the accuracy 
differences between articles and past tense –ed (.716) as well as between articles 
and plural –s (.582).The by-writing number random slope indicated the amount 
of individual variation in the overall learning rate (.192).The model selected by 
the author showed a 20% reduction in the by-learner random intercept, indicat­
ing the amount of individual variation in article accuracy that can be explained 
by proficiency and L1 type. The by-morpheme random contrast also decreased 
by 16.8% for past tense –ed and by 21.1% for plural –s, indicating the degree 
to which proficiency could explain individual variation in between-morpheme 
accuracy difference. 
In terms of fixed effects, the author reported significant effects of morpheme 
(better accuracy for plural –s than for articles), L1 type (PRESENT group 
outperforming ABSENT group), writing number (increase over time), 
proficiency (increase by proficiency), and the interaction between morpheme

---

<!-- page 152 -->

 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
  
  
 
  
  
 
  
 
 
  
 
  
 
  
 
 
 
 
Variability and Variation in Second Language Development 135 
and proficiency (smaller increase over proficiency for plural –s than for articles). 
The author further noted that comparing random effects with the corresponding 
fixed effects would shed further light on the magnitude of individual variation. 
For example, the random contrast for past tense –ed was .613, while its estimate 
in the fixed-effects structure was much smaller (.141), indicating that the standard 
deviation of individual variation in the accuracy difference between articles and 
past tense –ed was substantially larger than the mean accuracy difference between 
those morphemes. In other words, although on average past tense –ed was more 
accurate than articles, the order of accuracy between these two morphemes varied 
substantially by learner. 
Overall, Murakami’s (2016) analysis adeptly demonstrated how individual 
variation can be quantified through random effects in GLMMs. The author 
highlighted that variance in random effects is informative in three ways:The extent 
to which individual variation is present in an effect, whether it is smaller or larger 
than that in another effect, and the degree to which variation can be accounted for 
by predictors. GLMMs have been employed by an increasing number of recent L2 
development studies, several of which were discussed in Chapter 4. For example, 
in examining a group of ESL learners’ longitudinal development of four features 
of lexical psycholinguistic properties (i.e., concreteness, imageability, familiarity, 
and meaningfulness), Crossley and Skalicky (2019) entered participants as random 
effects and session order as a random slope for participants to capture individual 
variation in the GLMMs they built.Although the authors did not separately discuss 
the results pertaining to individual variation, they reported both a marginal R2, a 
measure of the variance explained by the fixed effects only, and a conditional R2, 
a measure of the variance explained by the fixed and random effects combined 
for each model they built. Their results showed that random effects accounted 
for a larger amount of variance than the fixed effects for all features (e.g., for 
concreteness, marginal R2=.251, conditional R2=.610; for imageability, marginal 
R2=.256, conditional R2=.644; for familiarity, marginal R2=.025, conditional 
R2=.282; for meaningfulness, marginal R2=.317, conditional R2=.647), 
indicating a substantial amount of individual participant variation in their 
data. In examining a group of L2 Italian learners’ longitudinal development of 
Noun+Adjective (N+Adj) combinations, Siyanova-Chanturia and Spina (2020) 
entered learners nested within class as random effects and time as a random slope 
for learners in the GLMMs they built to capture individual variation. However, 
they found little individual variation in how their 175 learners within 17 different 
classes produced N+Adj combinations. Finally, in examining a group of L1 Dutch 
EFL learners’ longitudinal development of a number of usage-based indices of 
verb-argument construction (VAC) sophistication, Kyle et al. (2021) also entered 
participant as a random effect (with random intercepts) in the GLMMs they 
built to capture individual variation. Similar to Crossley and Skalicky (2019), they 
reported marginal R2 as well as conditional R2 (when the between-participant 
differences were large enough to be fit by the model).Their results showed no

---

<!-- page 153 -->

 
 
 
  
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
136 Variability and Variation in Second Language Development 
meaningful individual variation in most of the syntactic complexity and VAC 
sophistication indices considered, with the largest difference between marginal R2 
and conditional R2 seen for the main verb frequency measure (marginal R2=.457, 
conditional R2=.467). 
A further methodological contribution of Murakami’s (2016) study was his 
illustration of how systematicity, individuality, and nonlinearity could be examined 
simultaneously using generalized additive mixed models (GAMMs), which 
extend GLMMs “by modelling nonlinear relationships between independent and 
dependent variables” (p. 851). GAMMs combine smooth terms with parametric 
terms to allow “a statistical test of the significance of some terms while controlling 
for the nonlinear effects of other terms” (Murakami, 2016, p. 854), and recent 
incorporation of random effects into such models makes it possible to account 
for nonlinear patterns of individual learners. Given the relatively large amounts 
of mathematical details involved, we summarize Murakami’s (2016) findings 
pertaining to individual variation in the final model here without elaborating on 
the process of model building and selection.The parametric terms of the model 
suggest that the PRESENT group outperformed the ABSENT group, that article 
accuracy improves with proficiency, that accuracy was higher for plural –s than 
for articles at the mean proficiency level, and that accuracy increase was smaller 
for plural –s than for articles cross-sectionally. The smooth terms of the model 
showed linearity in the partial effect of writing number for the ABSENT group, 
nonlinearity in the partial effect of writing number for the PRESENT group, and 
significant individual variation in longitudinal development.The random effects of 
the model showed between-L1 variation in absolute accuracy as well as individual 
variation in the accuracy difference between articles and other morphemes. All 
in all, the model demonstrated individual variation in both absolute accuracy and 
nonlinear development, L1 and proficiency effects on accuracy, and L1 effects 
on longitudinal development patterns. While few L2 development studies have 
employed GAMMs so far, the rich, complex results that can be obtained through 
GAMMs may “challenge the state of the art current theories” and “force the field 
to move more into the direction of dynamic systems approaches to language” 
(Baayen, 2014, p. 361, cited in Murakami, 2016, p. 864). 
5.5 Summary 
In this chapter, we have reviewed a relatively small body of studies that used 
longitudinal learner corpora, in some cases together with cross-sectional learner 
corpora, to examine individual variability or variation in L2 development. 
Studies of intra-learner variability have employed several novel quantitative 
procedures to describe the developmental patterns of individual variables, 
identify significant developmental jumps of individual variables, determine 
significant correlations between different variables, and assess the degree of 
variability of those correlations over time. Studies of inter-learner variability

---

<!-- page 154 -->

 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Variability and Variation in Second Language Development 137 
have combined various quantitative procedures with qualitative analyses to 
examine the developmental trajectories of each individual learner within a small 
longitudinal cohort, to compare the developmental trajectories of selected focal 
learners against the developmental trendlines of a larger cross-sectional cohort, 
or to closely analyze differences in the developmental profiles of selected focal 
learners. These studies have provided robust evidence to support the CDST 
view that variability is an inherent property of L2 development and that the 
interaction of different developmental variables shapes the developmental 
trajectories of different learners in varied ways. Recent studies of individual 
variation in L2 development have commonly utilized mixed-model designs 
to quantify individual variation while examining group developmental trends. 
These studies usefully complement studies of variability by accounting for 
individual differences when the focus is on the group.The studies reviewed have 
also repeatedly demonstrated the facilitative role of dense longitudinal learner 
corpora, automated or semi-automated corpus annotation, and quantitative 
and qualitative corpus analyses in uncovering variability and variation in L2 
development. 
As the field accumulates more evidence of variability and a more nuanced 
understanding of the ways in which variability manifests in L2 development, it 
would appear useful for future studies of variability to look more systematically 
into the learner, task, and contextual factors that drive variability and to explore 
whether small clusters of similar developmental trajectories of individual learners 
within a larger cohort exist. To complement research in individual variability 
and group developmental trends, researchers could also explore inter-group 
variability, i.e., differences in the developmental trajectories of different groups 
of learners, particularly if such group-level differences could be used to advance 
our understanding of the effects of various factors on group developmental 
trajectories and/or inform group-level pedagogy.As Murakami (2016) suggested, 
future studies of individual variation in L2 development may consider exploring 
the use of GAMMs to model individual variation and nonlinearity simultaneously 
when examining group developmental trends.
