<!-- page 158 -->

  
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
6 
CONCLUSION 
6.1 Introduction 
The final chapter of the book has two purposes.We first briefly revisit the main 
perspectives, issues, and findings of the four broad areas of second language acqui­
sition (SLA) research discussed in Chapters 2 to 5 in the book and summarize 
the ways in which corpus linguistic methods have contributed to these research 
areas. We then reflect on the validity, reliability, and limitations of the ways in 
which they have been applied and conclude the book by proposing a number of 
directions for future applications of corpus linguistic methods in SLA research, 
including both new ways of using existing techniques and new applications of 
emerging or novel techniques. 
6.2 Summary of the Book 
In this book, we have reviewed a body of SLA research that integrated corpus 
linguistic methods, including in particular native and/or learner corpora and tools 
and methods for corpus annotation and analysis, to examine issues in four core 
research areas.The first research area focused on group-level variation in second 
language (L2) use conditioned by theoretically and/or pedagogically important 
learner- and/or task-related variables (e.g., Römer & Garner, 2019). In terms of 
the types of variation in L2 use, research in this area has been primarily concerned 
with the degree of variation in the complexity, accuracy, and fluency (CAF) of L2 
learners’overall production as well as of their use of specific types of linguistic fea­
tures or constructions (including morphosyntactic constructions and phraseologi­
cal units). Researchers have developed a large set of indices for measuring these 
dimensions of L2 use (e.g., Housen & Kuiken, 2009; Lu, 2017; Norris & Ortega, 
2009). A further focus has been on variation in the structural and functional 
DOI: 10.4324/9781003054948-6

---

<!-- page 159 -->

 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
142 Conclusion 
characteristics and usage contexts of L2 learners’ use of specific linguistic fea­
tures or constructions, usually analyzed qualitatively. In terms of learner variables, 
existing corpus-based studies of variation in L2 use have focused heavily on the 
effects of L2 proficiency and to a lesser extent first language (L1) background, 
likely due to the fact that these two variables tend to be more commonly avail­
able in learner corpora than other variables (Gablasova et al., 2019). Examination 
of cross-proficiency variation in L2 use has been a core focus of learner corpus 
studies with a cross-sectional design (e.g., Garner, 2016; Lu, 2011).While various 
definitions of proficiency level have been used in different learner corpora, there 
has been increasing effort to map proficiency levels to a common scale such as the 
Common European Framework of Reference (CEFR) for Languages to improve 
the comparability and generalizability of results across different studies. Research 
in this area has reported varied patterns of cross-proficiency variation for differ­
ent dimensions of L2 use and different proficiency ranges, including the absence 
of cross-proficiency variation in some cases. Utilizing learner corpora containing 
language samples produced by learners from diverse L1 backgrounds, learner cor­
pus studies have also found that L2 learners’ L1 background can be expected to 
affect many but not all aspects of their L2 use, that typological similarities between 
the learners’ L1 and the target L2 do not always translate into usage patterns more 
similar to the usage patterns of L1 speakers of the target L2, and that it is impor­
tant to consider L1 and proficiency effects on variation in L2 use simultaneously 
(e.g., Lu & Ai, 2015; Shatz, 2019).While the effects of task-related variables on L2 
use have been more commonly examined in controlled experimental studies, a 
small body of learner corpus studies have shown how analyses of learner corpora 
with language samples representing different modes, genres, task types, and topics 
could be employed to examine such effects (e.g.,Yang et al., 2015;Yoon & Polio, 
2017).These studies have confirmed the effects of such task design features on L2 
learners’ linguistic performance and have sought to explain these effects from the 
perspectives of information-processing, functional, or interactive demands. 
The second research area focused on the effects of various input factors on 
L2 processing and production. Much research in this area was conducted from 
usage-based approaches to SLA, in which constructions are seen as the targets of 
language learning and the process of learning constructions as associative learning 
(Wulff & Ellis, 2018). Frequency and contingency constituted two of the most 
heavily examined input factors in corpus-based studies in this area. Research 
focusing on L2 processing has utilized L1 corpora to derive information about 
the frequency and/or contingency of target constructions and examined their 
effects on L2 learners’ processing of those constructions in various processing 
tasks (e.g., acceptability judgment and comprehension tasks). Research focusing 
on L2 production has obtained features of L2 production of target construc­
tions from learner corpora and examined the relationship of such features to 
the frequency and/or contingency of those constructions in L1 corpora. Overall, 
significant frequency and contingency effects have been found on L2 processing

---

<!-- page 160 -->

 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Conclusion 143 
and/or production of lexical categories, the article construction, phraseologi­
cal units, syntactic constructions, and phonological features. A small number of 
usage-based studies have also integrated corpus analysis to examine the effects 
of recency, semantic prototypicality, and saliency on L2 processing or production 
of syntactic or grammatical constructions. Corpus linguistic methods were used 
in varied ways in these studies: L1 corpora were used to extract instances and 
usage statistics of target constructions, corpus linguistic tools were employed to 
compute measures of semantic prototypicality, and L2 corpora were analyzed for 
features of L2 production (e.g., Zhao & Shirai, 2018).These factors were found 
to have significant or close to significant effects on L2 processing or production, 
although their effects appeared weaker or less ubiquitous than those of frequency 
and contingency. 
The third research area focused on the longitudinal trajectories of L2 devel­
opment at the group level. Much of this body of research was again conducted 
within the usage-based theoretical framework. These studies have used various 
corpus analytical tools to analyze longitudinal learner corpora to understand L2 
development of CAF, productive lexical knowledge, phraseological competence, 
grammatical and morphosyntactic constructions, and stance features (e.g., Ellis 
& Ferreira-Junior, 2009; Yoon & Polio, 2017). Using relevant learner and task 
variables encoded in the learner corpora, some studies examined how such vari­
ables (e.g., learner proficiency and genre) interacted with time spent learning 
the L2 to affect L2 learners’ developmental trajectories. Some studies have also 
used longitudinal L1 corpora or large-scale synchronous L1 reference corpora 
to examine the effects of various input and linguistic factors (e.g., the frequency 
of the target feature in L1 usage) on L2 development. Methodologically, this 
body of research has proposed and validated many new measures derived from 
L1 and/or L2 corpora for assessing L2 development in various dimensions and 
experimented with new data-driven approaches to discover areas of significant 
developmental changes. Collectively, these studies have shed valuable light on the 
amount of time it may take for significant developmental changes in different L2 
dimensions to occur, the ways in which significant development changes manifest 
themselves, and the interaction between learning time and different input, learner, 
and task variables. 
The fourth and last research area has employed longitudinal L2 corpora to 
examine inter- and intra-learner variability and individual variation in L2 devel­
opment. Research focusing on variability over time has been mostly informed by 
the Complex Dynamic Systems Theory (CDST), which highlights the complex, 
dynamic,and adaptive nature of the language system, nonlinearity of and variabil­
ity in language development, and the interaction among different subsystems in 
the development process (e.g., de Bot et al., 2007; Larsen-Freeman, 2006). Studies 
of intra-learner variability have employed multiple statistical methods to reveal 
patterns of longitudinal change for individual developmental variables, to exam­
ine the interaction between developmental variables over time, and to determine

---

<!-- page 161 -->

 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
144 Conclusion 
possible developmental jumps in the developmental process. These studies have 
yielded evidence that the behavior of intra-learner variability follows the prin­
ciples of dynamic systems. Studies of inter-learner variability have focused on 
delineating differences between the developmental trajectories or profiles of two 
or more individual learners, often bringing cohort-level longitudinal or cross-
sectional developmental trends into the comparative analysis to highlight the 
ways in which individual developmental trajectories may differ from cohort-level 
developmental trends. Finally, an increasing number of studies have used mixed-
effect models to quantify individual variation while investigating developmental 
patterns at the group level (e.g., Murakami, 2016). 
6.3 Refections and Future Research Direction 
Collectively, the studies reviewed in this book have showcased the usefulness of 
corpus linguistic methods in several core SLA research areas. In this section, we 
conclude the book by reflecting on the methodological considerations in employ­
ing such methods in SLA research and proposing several future research directions. 
Native and learner corpora have constituted a major data source for the body 
of studies reviewed in the book. A fundamental issue in using such corpora in 
SLA research has to do with the extent to which the corpora selected or com­
piled adequately represent what they are assumed to represent. For example, many 
studies examining the effects of input factors on L2 processing, production, or 
development have used large-scale general-purpose corpora such as the British 
National Corpus (BNC; Burnard, 2007) and the Corpus of Contemporary 
American English (COCA; Davies, 2009) to derive usage statistics from learner 
input.The validity of the assumption that such corpora adequately represent the 
language input to all L2 learners in diverse instructional and learning contexts 
(e.g., English as a foreign language vs. English as a second language environments) 
needs to be approached with caution. Some studies have taken a step forward to 
compile corpora consisting of instructional input provided by L1 teachers in the 
classroom or those consisting of interactional input produced by L1 speakers in 
interaction with L2 learners to better capture the actual input L2 learners receive. 
Such efforts are highly plausible, although the representation is still at best partial, 
as they leave out the types of input L2 learners may be exposed to outside of those 
instructional or interactional contexts. It would appear worthwhile to rigorously 
track the full range and types of actual instructional and incidental input L2 learn­
ers receive in the observation period of L2 development studies. Such representa­
tive input corpora would allow researchers to not only more reliably gauge the 
effects of input factors on L2 production and development but also systematically 
assess the extent to which large-scale general-purpose corpora can be used as 
proxies of L2 input corpora. 
Learner corpora have been widely used to analyze L2 usage, production, and 
development.There is increasing consensus regarding the importance for learner

---

<!-- page 162 -->

 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Conclusion 145 
corpora to encode rich learner and task variables with valid conceptualizations, 
reliable operationalizations, and representative value ranges. Consistent operation­
alizations of relevant variables across different learner corpora, such as the effort 
to map proficiency levels to a common scale mentioned above, make the find­
ings from studies based on different corpora more comparable and generalizable. 
A recurring theme across studies with diverse analytical concerns has been the 
potential effects of language tasks on the results pertaining to L2 production and 
development. In longitudinal studies, while asking L2 learners to repeat the same 
task will eliminate task effects, multiple repetitions may not always be compatible 
with instructional goals and may also lead to repetition effects and motivational 
attrition. Finding theoretically sound and pedagogically viable ways to address 
repetition and task effects would thus be a useful endeavor in future L2 devel­
opment research. One potentially fruitful method could be to identify sets of 
tasks with comparable task complexity, communicative functions, and linguistic 
demands as well as sets of tasks that differ in these aspects in a principled way 
(e.g., Robinson, 1995, 2001). L2 teachers and SLA researchers could then draw 
tasks from such a repertoire in designing pedagogical activities and compiling 
longitudinal or cross-sectional learner corpora. Another issue that emerged as 
particularly important in longitudinal developmental studies is the length of the 
observation period. In the studies reviewed, the observation period has mostly 
ranged from a few months to three years. Many studies have shown that signifi­
cant development may not take place in all language subsystems in relatively short 
observation periods such as a semester.At the same time, being able to delineate 
longer developmental trajectories of L2 learners will have immense theoretical 
and pedagogical implications. Larger-scale studies with longer-term designs that 
extend the observation period well beyond three years, along with an optimal 
density of data collection and adequate planning for potential participant attri­
tion, will undoubtedly generate richer longitudinal learner corpora that allow for 
much fuller descriptions of more complete L2 developmental trajectories. 
In addition to improving the representativeness and richness of native and learner 
corpora as data sources for SLA research, future research can also continue exploring 
and developing more robust tools for corpus annotation and analysis.The studies 
reviewed have taken advantage of a range of automated tools for corpus annota­
tion and analysis. Meanwhile, there is a clear need for future research to continue 
improving the accuracy of existing natural language processing tools for annotating 
and analyzing spoken or written learner corpora (e.g., Lu, 2022). The field will 
certainly also benefit from the development of additional tools to automate new 
and more fine-grained types of corpus annotation and analysis. Some examples 
of recent efforts in this area include the Authorial Voice Analyzer (AVA), a tool for 
analyzing interactional metadiscourse features in texts (Yoon, 2017), the Grammar 
and Mechanics Error Tool (GAMET), a tool for flagging and counting structural 
and mechanics errors in texts, including grammar, spelling, punctuation, white space, 
and repetition errors (Crossley et al., 2019), and AutoSubClause, a tool for automatic

---

<!-- page 163 -->

 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
146 Conclusion 
extraction of fine-grained types of subordinate clauses from texts (Chen et al., 2021). 
Additional research on tools for annotating and analyzing semantic and functional 
aspects of language use, such as metaphors, speech acts, and rhetorical moves, among 
others (Lu, 2014; Lu et al., 2021), would greatly facilitate SLA research into L2 learn­
ers’ use, processing, production, and development of these aspects. 
Another way in which corpus analytical methods can be used to enhance the 
types of SLA research reviewed in this book is the exploration of more robust data-
driven methods for detecting relevant linguistic features and constructions that show 
significant development or variation. Existing research has generally focused on pre­
determined sets of target features or constructions in investigating L2 usage, produc­
tion, or development.As discussed in Chapter 4, Ivaska (2015) demonstrated how a 
data-driven approach could be used to discover linguistic constructions with signifi­
cant developmental changes without preconceived hypotheses. Such an approach 
generally involves identifying a relevant search space (e.g., morphological 1-word, 
2-word,and 3-word skip-grams in Ivaska’s (2015) study of L2 morphological devel­
opment) and then employing appropriate methods to discover constructions within 
the search space that show significant developmental changes.The same approach 
can also be used to detect aspects of language use that vary significantly by relevant 
learner and task variables. Findings from this approach can usefully complement 
those focusing on predetermined target features or constructions. 
The search for valid and reliable measures of L2 variation and development 
has been a central methodological concern in SLA research.While earlier efforts 
focused primarily on the formal, structural, and distributional properties of dif­
ferent types of linguistic units, recent research has started to pay more atten­
tion to semantic, acquisitional, and psycholinguistic properties of such units (e.g., 
Kyle et al., 2018). Additional research into measures that tap into the meaning 
and functional aspects of language use is much needed. For example, Lu and Hu 
(2022) argued for the need to consider the degree of sophistication of different 
senses of polysemous words in measuring lexical sophistication.They employed a 
word sense disambiguation system to determine the senses of polysemous words 
used by L2 English learners and demonstrated the superiority of lexical sophisti­
cation measures that take into account the degree of sophistication of the word 
senses used to existing measures that do not. Lu (2022) outlined the possibility 
and usefulness of developing measures of the functional effectiveness of linguistic 
features, particularly with respect to the appropriateness of specific linguistic fea­
tures for the rhetorical moves and steps they are used to realize. Measures of this 
nature will help us gain a deeper understanding of L2 variation and development 
in the meaning and functional aspects of language, although automated compu­
tation of such measures will depend on the maturity of natural language process­
ing techniques for reliably automating relevant analyses of language meanings 
and functions. 
Finally, along with the methodological advances discussed above, future cor­
pus-based SLA research can engage with a number of emerging or new analytical

---

<!-- page 164 -->

 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
Conclusion 147 
foci. First, studies of L2 variation and development could attend more to the 
effects of sociolinguistic variables or variables pertaining to language learning 
experience, as learner corpora containing such information become increas­
ingly available (Gablasova et al., 2019). For example, systematic descriptions and 
comparisons of the developmental trajectories of L2 learners with different ages 
of first exposure in instructed vs. naturalistic acquisition in second vs. foreign 
language environments would be much welcomed. This might also be an area 
where group-level variability in L2 developmental trajectories could be highly 
informative, in addition to group-level variation in L2 use and individual vari­
ability and variation in L2 development conditioned by these variables. Second, 
more research in specialized and professional domains could be conducted to 
verify whether the theoretical claims that have been validated in the general 
language domain can be generalized to these domains. For example, it is theo­
retically useful to examine how the input statistics in specialized input corpora 
differ from that in general input corpora (e.g., Casal et al., 2022) and whether the 
effects of such input statistics exhibit similar effects on L2 processing, production, 
and development of linguistic features and constructions in specialized domains 
as they do in the general domain. Research in these areas can also offer findings 
that can inform the teaching and learning of key competences in specialized and 
professional domains (e.g., Crosthwaite & Jiang, 2017).Third, future studies of L2 
variation, processing, production, and development should also pay greater atten­
tion to the meaning and functional aspects of language. For example, how do 
different learner and task variables affect L2 learners’ use of word meanings with 
different degrees of sophistication (e.g., metaphorical meanings)? How do such 
variables affect their employment of different types of rhetorical functions in 
specific genres and communicative contexts? What factors may affect L2 learn­
ers’ processing of word meanings with different degrees of sophistication (e.g., 
metaphorical meanings)? How do the input statistics of different communicative 
or rhetorical functions affect L2 learners’ processing or production of such func­
tions? How do L2 learners develop the ability to use more sophisticated word 
meanings? How do L2 learners develop the ability to employ more appropriate 
linguistic features for specific communicative or rhetorical functions? Answers to 
these and other similar questions will greatly enhance our understanding of L2 
learners’ use, processing, and development of the meaning and functional aspects 
of language. 
References 
Burnard, L. (Ed.). (2007). Reference guide for the British National corpus (XML ed.). Oxford: 
Oxford University Computing Services. http://www.natcorp.ox.ac.uk/docs/URG/ 
Casal, J. E., Shirai, Y., & Lu, X. (2022). English verb-argument construction profiles in 
a specialized academic corpus: Variation by genre and discipline. English for Specific 
Purposes, 66, 94–107. https://doi.org/10.1016/j.esp.2022.01.004
